The Womb Hadith: Evidence for Predestination and Refutation of Kasb Theories
A footnote citing a hadith to support jabr and refute kasb theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a specific hadith about creation in the womb is used by the commentator to support jabr (predestination) and to refute several later Ash'ari theories of kasb (acquisition).

Core Idea
One hadith dismantles multiple Ash'ari attempts to soften predestination.

In this footnote, the commentator cites a hadith from al-Ash'ari's own work al-Ibana. The hadith describes the angel writing a person's fate before birth. Surprisingly, the commentator argues that this very hadith refutes the later Ash'ari theories of kitab, sura al-amal, and sabab wa musabbab, while agreeing with al-Ghazali's stricter predestinarian view. Let's unpack this dense passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
First, read the Arabic hadith and its literal translation carefully. Note the description of the angel writing the person's deeds, provision, lifespan, and final state.
This hadith is widely transmitted and is often used in predestination debates.
2
Identify the four doctrinal positions refuted
The commentator claims the hadith refutes three specific Ash'ari constructs: the 'kitab' (writing?) of al-Ash'ari, the 'form of the act' of al-Baqillani, and 'cause and effect' of al-Juwayni. It also agrees with al-Ghazali's position.
Each of these was an attempt to introduce some human agency within predestination.
3
Understand why this supports jabr
The hadith, by describing fate as written before birth, is taken as clear proof that all actions are predetermined, leaving no room for human power or choice.
This interpretation is contested by other schools, but the commentator presents it as decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤) في كتاب : الإبانة عن أصول الديانة ، استدل الأشعري بهذا الحديث : و إن أحدكم يجمع خلقه في بطن أمه : أربعين يوماً نطفة ، ثم يكون علقة مثل ذلك ، ثم يكون مضغة مثل ذلك ، ثم يبعث الله إليه ملكاً ، ويؤمر بأربع كلمات ، ويقال له : اكتب عمله ورزقه وأجله وشقي أو سعيد ثم ينفخ فيه الروح، والحديث صريح في إثبات مذهب الجبر ، والحديث أيضاً صريح في إنكار ( الكتب ، الذي قال به الأشعري للتوفيق بين الحنابلة وبين المعتزلة . وصريح في إنكار ( صورة العمل ، عند الباقلاني وصريح في إنكار : السبب والمسبب ) عند الجويني وموافق لرأي الغزالي (1) أي القدرة (۲) سقط ( ط ، ل ) .
(4) In the book 'al-Ibana 'an Usul al-Diyana', al-Ash'ari used this hadith as evidence: 'Indeed, one of you is gathered in his mother's womb for forty days as a drop (nutfa), then he becomes a clot ('alaqa) like that, then a lump (mudgha) like that, then Allah sends an angel to him, and he is commanded with four words, and it is said to him: Write his deeds, his provision, his term, and whether he is wretched or blessed, then the spirit is breathed into him.' And the hadith is clear in proving the doctrine of jabr (predestination), and the hadith is also clear in denying the 'kitab' (writing?) which al-Ash'ari held in order to reconcile between the Hanbalis and the Mu'tazila. And it is clear in denying the 'form of the act' (sura al-'amal) according to al-Baqillani, and clear in denying the 'cause and effect' (al-sabab wa al-musabbab) according to al-Juwayni, and agrees with the opinion of al-Ghazali. (1) i.e., power (al-qudra) (2) Missing from (T, L).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a footnote that cites a famous hadith from al-Ash'ari's book 'al-Ibana 'an Usul al-Diyana'. The hadith describes the stages of human creation in the womb and the angel writing the person's deeds, provision, lifespan, and whether they will be blessed or wretched. The commentator claims the hadith clearly supports the doctrine of jabr (predestination). Furthermore, the commentator argues that this hadith refutes several theological constructs: it refutes the 'kitab' (perhaps meaning the written decree or the concept of acquisition) that al-Ash'ari used to reconcile Hanbalis and Mu'tazila; it refutes al-Baqillani's notion of the 'form of the act' (sura al-'amal); it refutes al-Juwayni's theory of 'cause and effect' (sabab wa musabbab); and it agrees with al-Ghazali's position. The footnote ends with editorial notes: 'i.e., power' and 'Missing from (T, L)'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith is often used to prove free will or human agency in actions.
Correction: Here, the commentator uses the hadith to support strict predestination (jabr) and to refute various Ash'ari theories that attempted to incorporate human acquisition (kasb).

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, what does the hadith about creation in the womb clearly prove?
A) The Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will
B) The doctrine of jabr (predestination)
C) The theory of kasb as taught by al-Ash'ari
D) Al-Ghazali's rejection of causality

Key Takeaway
The hadith of creation in the womb is used in this footnote to argue for strict predestination and against later Ash'ari attempts to introduce a role for human acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the angel writes your deeds, provision, lifespan, and destiny before you are born, do you feel a sense of helplessness or accountability? How does this truth affect the way you make choices today?
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars like al-Ash'ari tried to soften predestination to reconcile different groups. Are there areas in your life where you try to 'soften' Allah’s decree to feel more in control? What would it mean to fully submit to jabr?
?
If the hadith is clear in proving jabr, why do you think so many believers struggle to accept it in practice? What part of your heart resists this truth, and what would it take to surrender completely to Allah's will?

Hadith Evidence for Jabr and Refutation of Ash'ari Concepts
Razi cites a hadith to prove jabr and reject Ash'ari constructs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the hadith of the embryo's creation to argue for complete determinism (jabr) and to refute key Ash'ari doctrines of kasb, the image of the act, and cause and effect.

Core Idea
A single hadith about the embryo dismantles three Ash'ari attempts to soften determinism.

Razi presents a well-known prophetic hadith describing the stages of embryonic development and the angel writing destiny. He then argues that this hadith explicitly supports the deterministic doctrine of jabr. Moreover, he claims it implicitly refutes three distinct Ash'ari theological constructs: al-kasb (acquisition), the 'image of the act' (ṣūrat al-ʿamal), and the notion of 'cause and effect' (sabab wa musabbab). This passage thus uses scriptural authority to reject any middle ground between pure predestination and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic and English of the hadith describing the creation of the embryo. Notice the stages and the four things written by the angel.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Identify Razi's claim about jabr
Razi states that the hadith is explicit in affirming the doctrine of jabr (compulsion). Ask yourself: why does the writing of deeds before birth imply determinism?
3
Recognize the three Ash'ari concepts denied
The text lists three concepts: al-kasb (al-Ash'ari), the image of the act (al-Baqillani), and cause and effect (al-Juwayni). Note that Razi says the hadith is explicit in denying each.
These concepts were Ash'ari attempts to reconcile divine predestination with human responsibility.
4
Connect to al-Ghazali's view
Razi ends by saying the hadith agrees with al-Ghazali. Consider how al-Ghazali's position on this issue aligns with a more deterministic reading.
Al-Ghazali was also an Ash'ari but took a stronger predestinarian stance on some points.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
This passage shows how a single scriptural text is used to critique multiple theological positions. Think about the method of citing hadith to resolve kalam disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أحدكم يجمع خلقه في بطن أمه : أربعين يوماً نطفة ، ثم يكون علقة مثل ذلك ، ثم يكون مضغة مثل ذلك ، ثم يبعث الله إليه ملكاً ، ويؤمر بأربع كلمات ، ويقال له : اكتب عمله ورزقه وأجله وشقى أو سعيد ثم ينفخ فيه الروح، والحديث صريح في إثبات مذهب الجبر ، والحديث أيضاً صريح في إنكار ( الكتب ) ، الذي قال به الأشعري للتوفيق بين الحنابلة وبين المعتزلة . وصريح في إنكار ( صورة العمل ) عند الباقلاني وصريح في إنكار : ( السبب والمسبب ) عند الجويني وموافق لرأي الغزالي
And verily, one of you is formed in his mother's womb: for forty days as a drop, then as a clot for the like, then as a lump for the like, then God sends to him an angel, commanded with four words, and it is said to him: write his deeds, his provision, his term, and whether wretched or happy, then the spirit is breathed into him. And the hadith is explicit in affirming the doctrine of jabr (compulsion), and the hadith also is explicit in denying (al-kasb), which al-Ash'ari asserted to reconcile between the Hanbalites and the Mu'tazilites. And explicit in denying (the image of the act) according to al-Baqillani, and explicit in denying (the cause and effect) according to al-Juwayni, and it agrees with the opinion of al-Ghazali.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from prophetic hadith for the doctrine of jabr (predestination/compulsion). The hadith describes the creation of the human embryo in stages, and how an angel is commanded to write the person's deeds, provision, lifespan, and whether they will be wretched or happy before the spirit is breathed. The author argues that this hadith explicitly supports the deterministic view (jabr). Moreover, he claims that the hadith implicitly refutes several Ash'ari theological constructs: first, al-kasb (acquisition) which al-Ash'ari introduced to reconcile Hanbalite predestinarianism with Mu'tazilite free will; second, the 'image of the act' (ṣūrat al-ʿamal) posited by al-Baqillani; third, the notion of 'cause and effect' (sabab wa musabbab) advocated by al-Juwayni. The author concludes that the hadith aligns with the view of al-Ghazali. This passage thus uses a scriptural source to argue that the Ash'ari attempts to soften determinism are invalid.

Key Terms
جبر
حديث خلق الجنين
كتب
صورة العمل
سبب ومسبب
أشعري
باقلاني
جويني
غزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about embryonic development is merely a biological description with no theological implications for free will.
Correction: Razi argues that the hadith explicitly supports jabr (determinism) because it states that deeds, provision, lifespan, and final state are written before birth, leaving no room for human agency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Ash'ari concept does the hadith explicitly deny?
A) The image of the act (ṣūrat al-ʿamal)
B) The doctrine of acquisition (kasb)
C) The idea of cause and effect (sabab wa musabbab)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the hadith of the embryo to argue that all human actions are predetermined, directly refuting key Ash'ari attempts to introduce human agency through kasb, the image of the act, or cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that your deeds, provision, lifespan, and ultimate fate were written before you were born, does that truth make you feel a sense of profound trust in God, or does it unsettle you because it challenges your sense of control? How does your answer reflect the condition of your heart right now?
?
Razi uses this hadith to argue that there is no human agency at all. If you truly believed that, would you still get up tomorrow morning and plan your day? Examine your own actions: do you live as if your efforts matter, or do you surrender entirely to God's decree? What does your daily behavior reveal about your deeper belief regarding predestination?
?
If the hadith explicitly denies that you 'acquire' your deeds, then your successes and failures are entirely from God. Can you honestly thank God for your achievements without any feeling of personal pride? And can you accept your shortcomings without despair, knowing they too were decreed? Which response is more difficult for you, and why?

Imam Ali's Speech on the Heart: A Proof for Determinism
Imam Ali's sermon on the heart as support for determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ali's description of the heart's states demonstrates a necessary causal chain that supports the doctrine of determinism.

Core Idea
The heart's states are not random; they follow a necessary causal order.

This lesson examines a key passage from Imam Ali quoted by the author to support the rational proof of determinism. By analyzing the heart's reactions, the author shows that actions are necessarily ordered, leaving no room for free will. We will explore the original sermon, the author's commentary, and the further evidence from the Psalms of David.

Guided Path
1
Read Imam Ali's sermon
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the pattern: each state of the heart leads inevitably to a specific consequence (e.g., hope leads to humiliation).
The sermon uses a series of conditional statements.
2
Identify the causal chain
List the pairs: hope->humiliation, greed->destruction, etc. See how each is presented as a necessary consequence.
The author later calls this a 'necessary order' (tarattuban daruriyyan).
3
Examine the author's commentary
Read the author's explanation: he says the actions of the limbs depend on the heart's actions, and the heart's actions are necessarily ordered. There is no room for the servant to interrupt.
This directly supports determinism.
4
Consider the Psalms support
Read the quote from Psalms: God knows hearts, then actions, then final abode. This implies a causal chain from antecedent to consequent.
The author uses this to reinforce the idea of necessary causal inference.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask yourself: why does the author use transmitted proof (naql) after rational proof? How does this passage strengthen the case for determinism?
This shows the harmony between reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنختم هذا البرهان القاطع بفصل منقول عن أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه يقوي ما ذكرناه. روي أنه رضي الله عنه خطب فقال: وأعجب ما في الإنسان قلبه. فإن سنح له الرجاء أذله الطمع. وإن هاج به الطمع أهلكه الحرص. وإن ملكه اليأس قتله الأسف. وإن عرض له الغضب اشتد به الغيظ. وإن أسعده الرضى نسى التحفظ. وإن ناله الخوف شغله الحذر. وإن أصابته مصيبة قصمه الجزع. وإن وجد مالاً أطفاء الغني. وإن عضته الفاقة شغله البلاء. وإن جهده الجوع قعد به الضعف. فكل تقصير به مضر وكل إفراط له مفسد. قال المصنف: هذا الفصل تصريح منه عليه الرحمة بأن أفعال الجوارح مترتبة على أفعال القلوب وأن أفعال القلوب مترتب بعضها على بعض ترتبا ضروريا. لا مجال للعبد في قطع بعضها عن بعض. وذلك يحقق ما قلنا. وجاء في كتاب الزبور داود عليه السلام ما يقرب معناه من هذا وهو: إن الذي خلق أمزجة العباد علم أحوال قلوبهم ومن علم أحوال قلوبهم علم أفعالهم ومن علم أفعالهم علم أحوال معادهم. وكل ذلك إشارة إلى ما ذكرناه من كون كل ما تأخر كالمعلول لما تقدم وإن تأدى ذلك المتقدم إلى ذلك المتأخر تأديا اضطراريا. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك كان من علم المتقدم فلا بد وأن يعلم المتأخر استدلالا بالعلة على المعلول. وبالله التوفيق.
And we conclude this conclusive proof with a chapter transmitted from the Commander of the Faithful, Ali ibn Abi Talib—may Allah be pleased with him—which strengthens what we have mentioned. It is narrated that he—may Allah be pleased with him—delivered a sermon and said: The most amazing thing in man is his heart. For if hope presents itself to him, covetousness humiliates him. And if covetousness stirs him, greed destroys him. And if despair seizes him, grief kills him. And if anger appears to him, rage intensifies in him. And if contentment makes him happy, he forgets caution. And if fear befalls him, caution occupies him. And if a calamity afflicts him, impatience breaks him. And if he finds wealth, opulence extinguishes him. And if poverty afflicts him, hardship occupies him. And if hunger exhausts him, weakness prostrates him. So every shortcoming in him is harmful, and every excess in him is corrupting. The author said: This chapter is a declaration from him—may mercy be upon him—that the actions of the limbs are dependent upon the actions of the hearts, and that the actions of the hearts are dependent on one another in a necessary order. There is no room for the servant to separate any of them from another. And that confirms what we have said. And it is mentioned in the book of the Zabur (Psalms) of David—peace be upon him—something close in meaning to this, which is: He who created the temperaments of servants knows the conditions of their hearts; and he who knows the conditions of their hearts knows their actions; and he who knows their actions knows the conditions of their final abode. And all of that is an indication of what we have mentioned: that everything that comes later is like an effect (al-ma'lul) of what precedes it, and that what precedes leads to what follows necessarily. And if this is so, then whoever knows the antecedent must know the consequent, by inferring the effect from the cause. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a transmitted support (naqli ta'yid) for the preceding rational proof. The author begins by stating that he concludes the conclusive proof (al-burhan al-qati') with a passage from Imam Ali. He then quotes a well-known sermon in which Imam Ali describes the heart's various states and their inevitable consequences—hope leads to humiliation, greed to destruction, etc. The quote ends with the maxim that every shortcoming is harmful and every excess is corrupting. The author then provides his commentary: this passage explicitly shows that the actions of the limbs (af'al al-jawarih) are consequent upon the actions of the heart (af'al al-qulub), and the heart's actions themselves are necessarily ordered; the servant cannot interrupt this chain. This confirms the author's earlier claim. He then adds further support from the Psalms of David, which states that God, who created human temperaments, knows the conditions of hearts, and from that He knows actions, and from that He knows the final abode. This indicates that each subsequent thing is like an effect of its antecedent, leading necessarily. Therefore, whoever knows the antecedent must know the consequent, using knowledge of the cause to infer the effect.

Key Terms
القلب
أفعال الجوارح
أفعال القلوب
ترتبا ضروريا
العلة والمعلول
الزبور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The heart's many states and reactions prove that humans have free will and can choose their responses.
Correction: Imam Ali's sermon shows a necessary causal chain: each state inevitably leads to a specific outcome, indicating determinism, not free will. The author explicitly states there is no room for the servant to separate any link.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from Imam Ali's description of the heart's states?
A) The heart is unpredictable and humans have free will.
B) The actions of the limbs follow the heart's actions in a necessary order.
C) The heart's states are random and have no causal relationship.
D) The heart can choose to break the chain of causality.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ali's sermon shows that the heart's actions are necessarily ordered, confirming that human actions are determined by prior causes and leaving no room for free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which one of these heart-to-consequence pairs describes you most accurately right now? How does knowing it is part of an unbreakable chain change what you think you can do about it?
?
If every inner state is necessarily linked to the next, where is your real repentance? Is it merely a consequence itself, or can you genuinely turn to Allah? How does this lesson force you to re-examine your understanding of your own accountability?
?
The Zabur chain ends with 'knowledge of the final abode.' How does it make you feel that your end is already known from your inner state? What does that mean for any attempt to hide or pretend? What will you do with this awareness starting now?

God's Unchangeable Report
Scriptural proof for the impossibility of certain people believing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the immutability of God's truthful report entails the impossibility of certain events, using scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
God's truthful report can never become false, making some outcomes absolutely impossible.

This lesson presents a proof from divine speech: God has declared in the Quran that certain people will never believe. If they were to believe, God's report would turn false—an impossibility. This argument establishes the unchangeable nature of God's truthful report and its implications for predestination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divine declaration
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that God informs about specific peoples that they will not believe. This is the starting premise.
The passage begins with 'والثالث' indicating this is the third proof.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses cited: al-Baqara 6 and Ya-Sin 7. They are presented as evidence that God has indeed declared some will not believe.
The verses are quoted in the passage.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Consider the hypothetical: if those people believed, God's truthful report would become false. Recognize that this is impossible for God.
The passage says 'لو آمنوا، لانقلب خبر الله، الصدق: تكذباً'.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequence (the report becoming false) is impossible, the condition (their faith) must also be impossible. This proves that their unbelief is inevitable.
The principle: 'والمؤدي الى المحال محال'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنه تعالى أخبر عن أقوام معينين : أنهم لا يؤمنون كقوله : إن الذين كفروا . سواء عليهم الأنذرتهم ، أم لم تنذرهم . لا يؤمنون وقوله : ( لقد حق القول على أكثرهم . فهم لا يؤمنون ) فهؤلاء الذين أخبر الله عنهم ، أنهم لا يؤمنون . لو آمنوا ، لانقلب خبر الله ، الصدق : تكذباً ، وهذا محال والمؤدي الى المحال محال . فوجب أن يكون صدور الإيمان منهم محالاً
The third: He, exalted, informed about specific peoples that they will not believe, as His statement: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve, it is all the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them; they will not believe' and His statement: 'The word has indeed been fulfilled against most of them, so they will not believe.' So those about whom God informed that they will not believe—if they were to believe, then God's truthful report would turn into falsehood, and that is impossible. And what leads to the impossible is impossible. So it is necessary that the occurrence of faith from them is impossible.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a scriptural proof. It cites two Quranic verses (al-Baqara 6 and Ya-Sin 7) where God explicitly declares that certain people will not believe. If those people were to believe, God's report would no longer be true—it would become a lie, which is impossible for God. Following the principle that anything leading to an impossibility is itself impossible, the occurrence of faith from them is deemed impossible. The argument reinforces the immutability of divine speech and its correspondence to reality.

Key Terms
خبر الله
صدق
كذب
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge or reports can change over time.
Correction: God's truthful report is immutable; changing it would imply falsehood, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What would happen if the people God declared will not believe actually believed?
A) God would change His mind
B) God's report would become false
C) God would punish them more
D) The verses would be allegorical

Key Takeaway
God's truthful report is unchangeable, making certain outcomes absolutely impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's report is unchangeable, what does that mean for your own efforts to change your heart? Are you relying on your own ability or on His decree?
?
What if you are among those whom God has described as not believing? How does that thought make you feel, and what immediate action does it demand from you today?
?
When you hear God's warnings in the Quran, do you assume they apply to others or do you tremblingly examine your own soul? What would it take for you to take His report as personally addressed to you?

Fifth Proof: Attempting What God Said Will Not Happen Is Blameworthy
The author uses a Quranic verse to prove that intending what God said will not happen is forbidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that trying to make happen what God has said will not occur is an attempt to change God's words, and therefore forbidden and impossible.

Core Idea
Trying to change God's decree is both blameworthy and futile.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from divine censure. God explicitly criticizes the disbelievers for wanting to alter His words. The author draws a direct line between God's prior information and the impossibility of the opposite occurring. We will see how a simple Quranic verse grounds a strong theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to grasp the flow.
Pay attention to the Quranic citation.
2
Identify the Quranic citation
Notice how the author uses the verse as the foundation for the argument.
The verse is from Surah Al-Fath (48:10).
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow how the argument moves from blame to prohibition to impossibility.
Each step builds on the previous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه . فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله . قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم وقوعه : قصد إلى تبديل كلام الله . وذلك منهي عنه فلما أخبر الله عنهم : أنهم لا يؤمنون ، كان القصد إلى تكوينه قصدا إلى تبديل كلامه . وذلك منهي عنه وكان ذلك حاصلاً، سواء حاول تكوين الإيمان أو لم يحاول . فثبت : أن الذي علم الله أنه لا يقع ، وأخبر أنه لا يقع ، كان وقوعه ممتنع الحصول
The fifth: He, exalted, blamed the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about. He said: 'They want to change the words of God. Say: you will not follow us. That is what God has said before.' So it is established that the intention to bring about what God informed will not occur is an intention to change God's speech, and that is forbidden. So when God informed about them that they will not believe, the intention to bring it about is an intention to change His speech, and that is forbidden. And that is true whether they attempt to bring about faith or not. So it is established that what God knows will not occur and has informed will not occur, its occurrence is impossible.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof from divine censure (عيب) in the Quran. God criticizes the disbelievers for wanting to 'change the words of God' (Quran 48:10). The author argues that anyone who tries to make happen what God has said will not happen is attempting to change divine speech, which is prohibited and inherently impossible. Therefore, the very attempt is blameworthy, and the occurrence of that thing is impossible. Hence, faith from those God has said will not believe is unattainable.

Key Terms
تبديل كلام الله
منهي عنه
ممتنع الحصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can freely act against God's foreknowledge.
Correction: This argument shows that even the attempt is blameworthy, and the outcome is inherently impossible because God's information is immutable.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is the implication of trying to bring about something God said will not happen?
A) It is a sign of strong faith
B) It is an attempt to change God's words
C) It proves human free will
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
God's foreknowledge and speech cannot be changed; any attempt to do so is blameworthy and futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Answering the Fourth Objection: Commanding the Disbeliever Does Not Imply Ignorance
The author begins his response to the fourth objection: commanding the disbeliever does not imply ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the command itself and the fulfillment of the commanded act in responding to the objection that commanding the disbeliever implies ignorance of God.

Core Idea
God's command to the disbeliever is valid regardless of His foreknowledge of disbelief.

In this passage, the author begins to dismantle a powerful objection: if God already knows the disbeliever will not believe, doesn't commanding him to believe imply that God is ignorant or lying? The author's response hinges on a careful distinction between the command and its realization. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objector's claim that commanding the disbeliever would mean making God ignorant and lying.
2
Identify the author's distinction
Notice that the author splits the objection into two interpretations: one about the command itself, and one about the occurrence of the commanded act.
3
Grasp the denial
The author denies the first interpretation—that the command itself requires ignorance. The second interpretation is left unfinished.
The passage is cut off, but the key point is that the command is valid even if God knows it won't be fulfilled.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction is important for defending divine justice and wisdom: God's commands are not contingent on human response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the fourth question: [which is] his statement: 'And if the matter were as you say, then commanding the disbeliever to believe would be a command to make God ignorant and to call Him a liar,' we say: If you mean by it that commanding the disbeliever to believe is not achievable except with making God ignorant and calling Him a liar, then this is denied. And if you mean that the occurrence of the commanded thing is not possible except with making God ignorant...
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This fragment presents the beginning of the answer to the fourth objection. The objector argues that if God knows the disbeliever will not believe, then commanding him to believe would be commanding something impossible, which would imply ignorance or lying of God. The author begins a distinction: if the objector means that the command itself requires making God ignorant, that is denied (the command does not entail ignorance). If the objector means that the actual realization of the commanded act would require ignorance (since it would contradict God's knowledge), the author's answer is incomplete here.

Misconception: One might assume that commanding what God knows will not happen is absurd.
Correction: The author distinguishes between the command itself and its fulfillment; the command is valid even if God knows it will not be obeyed, and this does not entail ignorance.

The author denies that commanding the disbeliever requires making God ignorant; the command's validity is independent of God's foreknowledge.

The Tenth Proof: Scriptural Evidence Yields Only Speculative Knowledge
Al-Razi enumerates his tenth proof that scriptural evidences cannot be decisive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that scriptural evidence (dalil lafzi) cannot produce certainty, and why only speculative knowledge can be derived from it.

Core Idea
Scriptural words are open to interpretation, so they cannot give absolute certainty.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents his tenth proof that verbal evidence from the Quran is not decisive. He argues that if the Quran's words were absolutely clear, the earliest Muslims would have agreed on their meaning, but they did not. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic original and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two reasons al-Razi gives for why verbal evidences are not preventive of the opposite: (1) because they are conventional, (2) because the Companions would have agreed if they were decisive.
Notice the structure: 'أما أولاً' and 'وأما ثانياً'.
3
Understand the conclusion
Note how al-Razi concludes that verbal evidences are only speculative and that using them for certain matters is invalid.
This is the key takeaway: scriptural proof is not enough for certainty in theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology: it limits the use of Quranic verses in certain theological debates.
Al-Razi is defending the necessity of rational proof (kalam) over pure scriptural citation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن دلالة ألفاظ القرآن على هذا المطلوب . إما أن تكون دلالة من النقيض أو غير مانعة منه . والأول باطل . أما أولاً فلان الدلائل اللفظية : وضعية والوضعيات لا تكون مائعة من النقيض . وأما ثانياً : فلان هذه الدلائل ، لو كانت مانعة من النقيض. لكان الصحابة والتابعون أولى الناس بالوقوف عليها والإحاطة بمعانيها ، لأنهم كانوا أرباب تلك اللغة . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكانوا عالمين بالضرورة بأن القول بصحة هذا المطلوب : من دين محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام. ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما وقع الاختلاف في هذه المسألة قديماً وحديثاً ، بين أمة محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام . فثبت : أن دلالة هذه الألفاظ على هذه المطالب ، ليست دلائل قاطعة مانعة عن النقيض ، محتملة للنقيض . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت دلالة الدلائل اللفظية ، ليست إلا ظنية . فثبت بهذه الوجوه العشرة : أن الدلائل اللفظية لا تفيد إلا الظن . وظاهر أن هذه المسألة يقينية ، والتمسك بالدليل الظني في المطلوب اليقيني : باطل قطعاً (۱) فهذا تقرير البحث عن قولنا : التمسك بالدلائل اللفظية في المطالب اليقينية لا يجوز . وبالله التوفيق
The Tenth: Indeed the indication of the words of the Qur'an concerning this sought matter: either it is an indication that is preventive of the opposite or not preventive. The first is false. First, because verbal evidences are conventional (wad'iyya) and conventional things are not preventive of the opposite. Second, because if these evidences were preventive of the opposite, then the Companions and the Successors would be the most entitled to grasp them and comprehend their meanings, since they were the masters of that language. And if that were the case, they would necessarily know that the statement affirming the correctness of this sought matter is from the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And if that were the case, disagreement would not have occurred in this issue, past and present, among the nation of Muhammad (peace be upon him). So it is established that the indication of these words concerning these sought matters are not decisive proofs preventive of the opposite; rather they are probable of the opposite. And whenever this is the case, the indication of verbal evidences is only speculative. So it is established by these ten aspects that verbal evidences only yield speculation. And it is apparent that this issue is certain, and adhering to speculative evidence for a certain sought matter is absolutely false. This is the exposition of the investigation concerning our statement: 'Adhering to verbal evidences in certain matters is not permissible.' And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof in a series of ten arguments that scriptural evidences (dalil lafzi) only yield speculative knowledge (zann), not certainty (yaqin). The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that if a Quranic text were to be decisive (qati') in both transmission and meaning, it would have to be so clear that all rational people would agree. But since scholars have disagreed on these issues despite the Quran being a common source, no verse can be decisively probative. He gives two reasons: (1) linguistic conventions cannot be absolutely preventive of alternative meanings; (2) if they were, the earliest Muslims (sahaba and tabi'un) would have unanimously known the intended meaning, preventing later disagreements. Since disagreement exists, the verses must be open to interpretation. Thus verbal proofs are only speculative, and since the matter under discussion (likely divine attributes or something) is certain, relying on speculative evidence for certain conclusions is invalid. The passage concludes by affirming the rule that verbal proofs cannot be used for certain theological questions.

Key Terms
دلالة لفظية
قطعية
ظنية
مانعة من النقيض
وضعية
دليل سمعي
يقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every verse of the Quran is decisive and cannot be interpreted differently.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that even Companions disagreed, so no verse is absolutely preventive of alternative meanings.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi in this passage, what is the status of verbal evidences (dalil lafzi) concerning certain theological issues?
A) They are decisive (qati'iyya)
B) They are only speculative (zanniyya)
C) They are irrelevant
D) They are both certain and uncertain

Key Takeaway
Verbal evidences from scripture are not decisive, but only speculative, so they cannot be used to settle certain theological questions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Many of the beliefs you hold dear are probably based on verses of the Quran. After reading this lesson, are you willing to admit that your understanding of those verses might be only probable, not certain? What would change if you held your beliefs with humility?
?
The Companions—people who lived with the Prophet—disagreed on theological matters. If they disagreed despite being masters of Arabic, why do you assume that your interpretation is the only correct one? Can you sincerely pray to Allah for guidance and accept that you might be wrong?
?
This passage argues that scripture alone cannot settle certain theological questions. Have you ever used a verse to shut down a discussion or to feel superior to others? What would it look like to engage with others' interpretations with intellectual honesty, recognizing that your evidence may be speculative?

Why Denying Human Agency Makes the Quran Unreliable
Author argues that denying human agency makes the Quran unreliable and refutes the objection of eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari position that humans do not create their own actions leads to the possibility that the Quran could be false, and why the objection that God's eternal speech cannot lie is refuted.

Core Idea
If God can create evil, He could lie—so the Quran might be false.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument against the Ash'ari view that humans are not the creators of their actions. He claims that this view makes the Quran unreliable as a proof, since if God can create evil, He could also lie. He then anticipates and refutes the objection that God's speech is eternal and therefore cannot be false. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the passage is dense and contains both an argument and a refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence; don't worry about the missing part.
2
Identify the First Aspect of the Argument
Find the part that says 'since it is not evil on His part to create misguidance... then how can it be evil on His part to lie?' Understand that if lying is possible for God, then the Quran could be false.
This is the core move: equating creation of evil with lying.
3
Understand the Objection About Eternal Speech
Look for the objection: 'God's speech is an eternal attribute, and the eternal attribute cannot be false.' This is the opponent's defense.
The opponent thinks eternality guarantees truthfulness.
4
Follow the Refutation
The author gives two refutations: first, he denies that God's speech is eternal (controversial). Second, he argues that even if it were eternal, reports in the Quran about past events would be false because the events would not precede the speech.
This second point is key: if speech is eternal, it cannot report events that happened later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أن القول بأن العبد غير موجد لأفعال نفسه، ينافي الإقرار بكون القرآن حجة : فبيانه من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : إنه لما لم يقبح منه تعالى، خلق الضلالات والفواحش . فكيف يقبح منه أن يكذب ؟ ومع هذا التجويز لا يبقى القرآن حجة . لا يقال كلام الله صفة قديمة، والصفة القديمة يمتنع أن تكون كذباً . لأنا نقول : إن هذا الكلام : باطل من وجوه : الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن كلام الله صفة قديمة. فإن ذلك مما عظم الخلاف فيه، بيننا وبينكم ثم نقول : لو كان كلام الله قديماً، لكان كذباً . والدليل عليه : أنه مشتمل على الإخبار عن الأمور الماضية .. كقوله تعالى : ( إنا أنزلناه في ليلة القدر ) - ( إنا أرسلنا نوحاً إلى قومه ) والإخبار عن الأمور الماضية لا يكون صدقاً إلا إذا كان المخبر عنه سابقا على الخبر . وكون الأزلي مسبوقاً بغيره : محال فثبت بهذا : أن كلام الله تعالى، لو كان قديماً، لكان كذباً . وأنتم ادعيتم أن كونه قديما، ينافي كونه كاذباً . فوجب أن يقال : إن كونه قديماً،
And as for the explanation that the statement that the servant is not the creator of his own actions contradicts the acceptance of the Quran as a proof: its explanation is from three aspects: First: Since it is not evil on His part, Almighty, to create misguidance and shameful deeds, then how can it be evil on His part to lie? And with this permissibility, the Quran does not remain a proof. It cannot be said: [God's] speech is an eternal attribute, and the eternal attribute cannot be false. For we say: This statement is false from several aspects: First: We do not accept that God's speech is an eternal attribute, for there is great disagreement about that between us and you. Then we say: If God's speech were eternal, it would be false. The evidence for that is that it contains reports about past matters, such as His saying: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' and 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people.' Reports about past matters are only true if the reported matter precedes the report. And for the eternal to be preceded by something else is impossible. Thus it is established by this that if God's speech were eternal, it would be false. And you claimed that its being eternal prevents it from being false. So it must be said that its being eternal...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage introduces a new argument that the Ash'ari position (that the servant does not create his own actions) undermines the Quran's authority as a proof. The author states the argument from three aspects, but only the first is detailed here. The first aspect: since it is not evil for God to create misguidance and evil acts, then it would not be evil for God to lie. If lying is possible for God, then the Quran, which is God's speech, could be false, and thus it cannot be a reliable proof. The author then anticipates an objection from opponents who say that God's speech is an eternal attribute, and an eternal attribute cannot be false. The author refutes this in two ways: first, by denying that God's speech is eternal—this is a contentious point between Ash'aris and others. Second, even if it were eternal, it would still be false because the Quran contains reports about past events (e.g., the revelation on Laylat al-Qadr, sending Noah). For a report to be true, the event must precede the report. If the speech is eternal (co-eternal with God), then there is no temporal priority; the event cannot precede the speech. Therefore, the speech would be false. So the opponents' claim that eternal attribute prevents falsehood is invalid.

Key Terms
عبد (servant)
موجد (creator)
كلام الله (speech of God)
قديم (eternal)
كذب (falsehood)
حجة (proof)
إخبار (report)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's speech is eternal, it cannot be false because eternal attributes are perfect.
Correction: The author shows that an eternal speech containing reports of past events would be false because the events cannot precede the speech if it is co-eternal with God.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the argument that denying human agency contradicts the Quran being a proof?
A) God cannot lie.
B) If God can create evil, He could lie, so the Quran could be false.
C) The Quran is eternal.
D) The servant must be the creator of his actions.

Key Takeaway
Denying human agency makes the Quran potentially unreliable, and the claim that eternal speech cannot be false is refuted by both denying eternality and showing that eternal reports are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believed that your actions are not truly caused by you but by God alone, how would that affect your sense of personal responsibility for your sins? Are you ever tempted to use predestination as an excuse to avoid repentance and self-improvement?
?
The author argues that if God could create evil, He could also lie, which would make the Quran unreliable. Have you ever struggled with doubts about the Quran's reliability due to your own experiences? How does this argument challenge you to confront those doubts honestly?
?
The refutation of eternal speech forces us to think logically about God's attributes. Are there aspects of your faith that you avoid thinking about because they seem contradictory? What would it mean for you to embrace intellectual honesty in your belief, even when it is uncomfortable?

The Charge of Contradiction in the Qur'an on Jabr and Qadr
Razi presents the objection of contradiction and gives a brief reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Qur'an contains contradictory verses on jabr and qadr, and Razi's initial response that a wise speaker may use both literal and figurative language.

Core Idea
Contradiction is only apparent when forced interpretations are needed.

Razi begins by presenting the strongest objection against the Qur'an's consistency on predestination. Both the Jabriyya and Qadariyya claim support from many verses, each requiring forced interpretations of the other's proofs. This, they argue, shows the Qur'an is contradictory. Razi offers a preliminary response before delving deeper.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of the objection. Notice how the Jabriyya and Qadariyya both have strong proofs, yet each side's answer to the other relies on forced interpretations.
The objectors are of two types: those who deny prophethood and those who accept it but claim alteration.
2
Identify the two groups of objectors
Razi mentions that the objectors are divided: some deny prophethood altogether, while others accept it but argue that the Qur'an has been changed. Understand the difference in their arguments.
3
Understand Razi's brief answer
Razi states that a wise speaker may use both literal (haqiqa) and figurative (majaz) language. Therefore, interpreting some verses figuratively is not a problem. This is his preliminary refutation.
This is a concise answer; the detailed answer follows in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث أن القرآن هل يصير مطعوناً فيه بسبب ما فيه من الآيات الدالة تارة على الجبر ، وأخرى على القدر ؟ · قالوا : إن الجبرية تمسكوا بآيات كثيرة ، قوية الدلالة على الجبر . والقدرية أيضاً تمسكوا بآيات كثيرة ، قوية الدلالة على القدر . فترى كل واحد من هذين الخصمين ، إذا حاول الجواب عن دلائل خصمه ، فإنه يحتاج إلى تأويلات مستكرهة، ووجوه متعسفة. ولا يليق بالحكيم أن يتكلم بكلام، ويريد به تلك المعاني. فعلمنا منهم أن القرآن مشتمل على التناقض . ثم الذاكرون لهذا السؤال فريقان . منهم من ينكر نبوة محمد - عليه الصلاة والسلام - فيستدل بهذه الشبهة على قوله ، ومنهم من يقر بنبوة محمد - عليه الصلاة والسلام . وحينئذ يستدل بهذه الآيات المتعارضة على أن القرآن قد دخله التغيير والتحريف ، وأنه ما بقي كما أنزله الله . فإن من المحال أن يقول الحكيم كتاباً يشتمل على هذا الحد من التناقض . واعلم أن هذه الشبهة أيضاً : باطلة . لأن الحكيم كما يتكلم بالحقيقة ، فقد يتكلم بالمجاز . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم من حمل بعض الآيات على مجازاتها : محذور . فهذه جملة ما يتعلق بهذه المقدمة . وبالله التوفيق (1) واعلم : أن هذه الشبهة باطلة . ويدل على بطلانها : معرفة المحكم والمتشابه . فالمحكم قوله تعالى : ( اعملوا ما شئتم ) [فصلت ٤٠] والمتشابه قوله تعالى : ( والله خلقكم وما تعلمون ) [ الصافات ٩٥ - ٩٦ ] فالمحكم بين أن العبد مستقل بإيجاد الفعل . والمتشابه يحتمل معنيين . الأول : والله خلقكم وخلق عملكم والثاني : أن الآيات في معرض الاستهزاء والتهكم بقوم إبراهيم عليه السلام . أتعبدون ما تنحتون من هذه الحجارة ؟ والحال : أن الخالق لكم هو الله تعالى . ثم وبخهم بقوله : ( وما تعملون ) ؟ أي أي شيء تعملونه ؟ إن نحتكم الأصنام من الحجارة عمل باطل . والمنتفي مع المتشابه : هو المعنى الثاني ، فيكون هو المراد الله عز وجل . وعلى هذه الطريقة لا يكون في القرآن موهم للتعارض ولا موهم للتناقض . ولو أن القرآن كله على نسق واحد من الفهم ، لما تميز العالم عن الجاهل . والله يريد أن يتميز العالم عن الجاهل. ومثل هذا كثير في التوراة . فإن في سفر أشعياء أن الله تعالى ( مبدع النور ، وخالق الظلمة ، ومجري السلام ، وخالق الشر ) [ أشعياء ٤٣ : ٧ ] ومنه نعلم أن الله خالق الشر. وإن في سفر التكوين : ورأى الله جميع ما صنعه فإذا هو حسن جداً [ التكوين 1 : ٣١ ] ومنه نعلم : أن الله ليس خالقا للشر. لأن الشر ليس حسناً جداً . وعلى ذلك فعبارة أشعياء معناها : أن خالق الدنيا على ما هي عليه هو الله تعالى . وخلقها على ما هو عليه حسن جداً . فإن الله خلق الإنسان وسخر له ما في السموات وما في الأرض . ولكن قد يسيء الإنسان استعمال النعمة فيحولها إلى نقمة . فمثلاً خلق العنب نعمة وهو حسن والله مبدعه وخالقه . وإذا جعله الإنسان خمراً وسكر وبالسكر أضاع عقله وفقد عرضه وماله . نقول حينئذ إنه قد صار شراً في العالم . وأصل الشر من الله ، لأن العنب الذي هو أصل الشر من إبداع الله وخلقه والإنسان الذي أساء هو أيضاً من إبداع الله وخلقه، فقول أشعياء : خالق الشر، هو على هذا ، بحسب إيجاد أصل الأنواع وقد بان أن الله خالق الشر ، لئلا يتوهم متوهم أن الشرور في العالم تأتى من إله آخر . كما كان يعتقد المجوس . في الإلهين اثنين . واحد يأتي بالخير وواحد يأتي بالشر . فلكي يمنع أشعياء من هذا الاعتقاد، قال بأن الخير والشر من إله واحد والشرور على أنواع ثلاثة : ۱ - ما يصيب الإنسان من جهة طبيعة الكون والفساد ٢ ـ ما يصيب الناس من بعضهم لبعض ٣ - ما يصيب الإنسان بسبب فعله هو . وتبين التوراة أن الله قال لبني اسرائیل : وهذا قد كان من أيديكم [ ملاخي ١ : ٩] وفي سفر الأمثال : وإنما يعمل هذا مهلك نفسه [ أمثال ٦ : ٣٢ ] ونفس المعنى في القرآن الكريم ، يقول تعالى : ( وما أصابكم من مصيبة فيها كسبت أيديكم ) [الشورى ٣٠] ( ولا تلقوا بأيديكم إلى التهلكة ) [ البقرة -
The Third Investigation: Is the Qur'an impugned because of the verses in it that sometimes indicate jabr (compulsion) and sometimes qadr (capacity)? They said: The Jabriyyah cling to many verses with strong indication of jabr. The Qadariyyah also cling to many verses with strong indication of qadr. So you see each of these two opponents, when he tries to answer the proofs of his opponent, he needs forced interpretations (ta'wilāt mustakraha) and strained ways. It is not befitting of a wise person to speak speech and intend those meanings by it. So we know from them that the Qur'an contains contradiction. Then those who mention this question are two groups: some of them deny the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and use this suspicion to support their claim; and some of them affirm the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) and then use these contradictory verses to argue that the Qur'an has undergone change and alteration, and that it has not remained as God revealed it. For it is impossible for a wise person to say a book containing this level of contradiction. Know that this suspicion is also false. Because the wise person sometimes speaks literally (ḥaqīqa) and sometimes figuratively (majāz). And if that is the case, then there is no harm in interpreting some verses figuratively. This is the sum of what pertains to this premise. And with God is success. (1) Know that this suspicion is false. And what indicates its falsehood is knowledge of the muhkam (clear) and mutashābih (ambiguous). The muhkam is His saying: 'Do what you will' [Fussilat 40]. The mutashābih is His saying: 'And Allah created you and what you do' [Saffat 95-96]. The muhkam clarifies that the servant is independent in bringing about the act. The mutashābih admits of two meanings: first, 'And Allah created you and created your work'; second, that the verse is in the context of mockery and rebuke of the people of Abraham (peace be upon him): 'Do you worship what you carve from these stones?' while the Creator of you is Allah. Then He rebuked them by saying: 'And what do you do?' i.e., what are you doing? Your carving of idols from stones is a vain work. The consistent one with the mutashābih is the second meaning, and therefore it is what is intended by Allah Almighty. And in this way there is no suggestion of contradiction or inconsistency in the Qur'an. If the entire Qur'an were of one pattern of understanding, the scholar would not be distinguished from the ignorant. And Allah wants the scholar to be distinguished from the ignorant. And many examples like this exist in the Torah. For in the book of Isaiah that God Almighty is 'the creator of light, the creator of darkness, the maker of peace, and the creator of evil' [Isaiah 43:7], and from this we know that God is the creator of evil. And in the book of Genesis: 'And God saw everything that He had made, and behold, it was very good' [Genesis 1:31], and from this we know that God is not the creator of evil, because evil is not very good. Accordingly, the phrase of Isaiah means: the creator of the world as it is is God Almighty. And He created it as it is, very good. For God created man and subjected to him whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth. But man may misuse the blessing and turn it into a curse. For example, He created the grape as a blessing and it is good, and God is its originator and creator. But if man makes it into wine and becomes intoxicated and by intoxication loses his mind and loses his honor and wealth, then we say that it has become evil in the world. And the origin of evil is from God, because the grape which is the origin of evil is from God's origination and creation, and the man who misused it is also from God's origination and creation. So the statement of Isaiah: 'creator of evil' is on this basis, according to the bringing into existence of the origin of types. And it has been clarified that God is the creator of evil, lest someone imagine that evils in the world come from another god, as the Magi used to believe in two gods: one brings good and one brings evil. So to prevent this belief, Isaiah said that good and evil are from one God. And evils are of three types: 1- what afflicts man from the nature of generation and corruption; 2- what people inflict on each other; 3- what afflicts man due to his own action. And the Torah shows that God said to the Children of Israel: 'And this has been from your hands' [Malachi 1:9]; and in Proverbs: 'He who does this destroys himself' [Proverbs 6:32]; and the same meaning is in the Noble Qur'an: 'Whatever misfortune befalls you is due to what your hands have earned' [Shura 30]; 'And do not throw yourselves into destruction' [Baqarah -
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the charge that the Qur'an is contradictory because it contains verses supporting both jabr and qadr. Razi first presents the objection: both the Jabriyyah and Qadariyyah have strong scriptural proofs, each side resorts to forced interpretations, and this suggests contradiction. The objectors are of two types: those who deny prophethood and those who accept it but claim the Qur'an has been altered. Razi then gives a twofold response. First, a brief answer: the wise speaker may use both literal and figurative language, so there is no problem. Second, a detailed answer: he invokes the principle of muhkam and mutashabih. The muhkam verse (Fussilat 40) shows human agency; the mutashabih (Saffat 95-96) admits of two interpretations, and the one consistent with the muhkam is preferred. He argues that such diversity is intentional to distinguish the scholar from the ignorant. To further support his point, he cites examples from the Torah where similar apparent contradictions exist (Isaiah vs. Genesis), and explains how they are reconciled: the 'creation of evil' refers to the origin of the types of things that may become evil through misuse, and God is the sole creator of all to counter dualist beliefs. Finally, he lists three types of evil and shows that scriptural statements attributing evil to human actions are consistent with this.

Key Terms
الجبر
القدر
التناقض
المجاز
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an is contradictory because it contains verses supporting both jabr and qadr.
Correction: Razi shows that the presence of both literal and figurative language does not indicate contradiction; rather, it is a feature of eloquent speech.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of objectors who raise the charge of contradiction?
A) Those who deny prophethood and those who accept it but claim alteration.
B) The Jabriyya and the Qadariyya.
C) The polytheists and the Jews.
D) The literalists and the allegorists.

Key Takeaway
Razi's initial counter is that a wise speaker may employ both literal and figurative speech, so no contradiction necessarily follows from the diversity of Qur'anic expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to contradict your understanding of God's justice or mercy, do you immediately question the Qur'an or your own assumptions? How does Razi's point about literal and figurative speech challenge you to trust the All-Wise Author? Before tomorrow's prayer, will you journal about one verse you've struggled with and commit to seeking a sound interpretation instead of doubting?
?
The objectors in this passage used the apparent contradiction either to reject prophethood or claim alteration. Have you ever, even in small ways, allowed a difficult verse to shake your trust in the Qur'an as God's preserved word? What would change in your relationship with the Book if you approached every challenging verse with the conviction that its diversity of expression has a wise purpose, not a flaw?
?
Razi says a wise person speaks both literally and figuratively. When you read the Qur'an, do you insist that every verse must be taken literally, or are you open to figurative meanings? Which verses challenge you the most in this regard? This week, pick one such verse and study the classical interpretations, then reflect: did your initial reaction assume contradiction, or did you trust the Author's wisdom?

Unseen Matters
advanced
10 min
Resolving the Contradiction: Muhkam, Mutashabih, and the Torah Analogy
Razi provides the detailed refutation using muhkam/mutashabih and Torah analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the principles of muhkam and mutashabih to resolve the apparent contradiction, and why similar diversity exists in the Torah.

Core Idea
The muhkam clarifies the mutashabih, and diversity of expression is deliberate.

Razi now gives his detailed refutation. He introduces the key distinction between muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous) verses, using two specific examples. He also draws an analogy from the Torah, where similar apparent contradictions are resolved by understanding context and purpose. This not only defends the Qur'an but also refutes dualist beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Understand muhkam and mutashabih
Read the passage where Razi defines muhkam and mutashabih. The muhkam verse (41:40) indicates human agency; the mutashabih verse (37:95-96) admits two interpretations. Note how the muhkam guides the interpretation of the mutashabih.
Razi says the mutashabih has two possible meanings, and the one consistent with the muhkam is chosen.
2
See the purpose of diversity
Razi explains that if the entire Qur'an were on one pattern, the scholar would not be distinguished from the ignorant. This diversity is intentional as a test.
3
Examine the Torah examples
Razi cites verses from Isaiah and Genesis that seem to contradict on whether God creates evil. He reconciles them by explaining that evil in Isaiah refers to the origin of things that become evil through misuse, and that God is the sole creator to refute dualism.
He also lists three types of evil and shows that human responsibility is affirmed in both Torah and Qur'an.

This is the sum of what pertains to this premise. And with God is success. (1) Know that this suspicion is false. The consistent one with the mutashābih is the second meaning, and therefore it is what is intended by Allah Almighty. And in this way there is no suggestion of contradiction or inconsistency in the Qur'an. If the entire Qur'an were of one pattern of understanding, the scholar would not be distinguished from the ignorant. And Allah wants the scholar to be distinguished from the ignorant. And many examples like this exist in the Torah. For in the book of Isaiah that God Almighty is 'the creator of light, the creator of darkness, the maker of peace, and the creator of evil' [Isaiah 43:7], and from this we know that God is the creator of evil. And in the book of Genesis: 'And God saw everything that He had made, and behold, it was very good' [Genesis 1:31], and from this we know that God is not the creator of evil, because evil is not very good. Accordingly, the phrase of Isaiah means: the creator of the world as it is is God Almighty. And He created it as it is, very good. For God created man and subjected to him whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth. But man may misuse the blessing and turn it into a curse. For example, He created the grape as a blessing and it is good, and God is its originator and creator. But if man makes it into wine and becomes intoxicated and by intoxication loses his mind and loses his honor and wealth, then we say that it has become evil in the world. And the origin of evil is from God, because the grape which is the origin of evil is from God's origination and creation, and the man who misused it is also from God's origination and creation. And it has been clarified that God is the creator of evil, lest someone imagine that evils in the world come from another god, as the Magi used to believe in two gods: one brings good and one brings evil. So to prevent this belief, Isaiah said that good and evil are from one God. And evils are of three types: 1- what afflicts man from the nature of generation and corruption; 2- what people inflict on each other; 3- what afflicts man due to his own action. And the Torah shows that God said to the Children of Israel: 'And this has been from your hands' [Malachi 1:9]; and in Proverbs: 'He who does this destroys himself' [Proverbs 6:32]; and the same meaning is in the Noble Qur'an: 'Whatever misfortune befalls you is due to what your hands have earned' [Shura 30]; 'And do not throw yourselves into destruction' [Baqarah -
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the charge that the Qur'an is contradictory because it contains verses supporting both jabr and qadr. Razi first presents the objection: both the Jabriyyah and Qadariyyah have strong scriptural proofs, each side resorts to forced interpretations, and this suggests contradiction. The objectors are of two types: those who deny prophethood and those who accept it but claim the Qur'an has been altered. Razi then gives a twofold response. First, a brief answer: the wise speaker may use both literal and figurative language, so there is no problem. Second, a detailed answer: he invokes the principle of muhkam and mutashabih. The muhkam verse (Fussilat 40) shows human agency; the mutashabih (Saffat 95-96) admits of two interpretations, and the one consistent with the muhkam is preferred. He argues that such diversity is intentional to distinguish the scholar from the ignorant. To further support his point, he cites examples from the Torah where similar apparent contradictions exist (Isaiah vs. Genesis), and explains how they are reconciled: the 'creation of evil' refers to the origin of the types of things that may become evil through misuse, and God is the sole creator of all to counter dualist beliefs. Finally, he lists three types of evil and shows that scriptural statements attributing evil to human actions are consistent with this.

Key Terms
المحكم
المتشابه
الشر
الإلهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an is contradictory because of verses on jabr and qadr.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the mutashabih is understood in light of the muhkam, and that such diversity is intentional for testing scholars.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the Qur'an contain both muhkam and mutashabih verses?
A) To confuse the reader.
B) To distinguish the scholar from the ignorant.
C) Because the Prophet could not decide.
D) Because of later alterations.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction is resolved by interpreting ambiguous verses in light of clear ones, a method that distinguishes scholars and is consistent with earlier scriptures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever encountered a verse that seemed to contradict your understanding of Allah's justice or power? Did you dismiss it, or did you seek the muhkam to clarify? Today, resolve to never let a difficult verse shake your faith; instead, commit to learning the principles of tafsir that the scholars have laid down.
?
Razi says that diversity in the Qur'an distinguishes the scholar from the ignorant. Are you content to remain ignorant of the deep wisdom in Allah's speech, or will you strive to be among those who understand? Ask yourself: What one step will you take this week to increase your understanding of the Qur'an beyond the surface?
?
This lesson shows that evil enters the world when humans misuse Allah's good blessings. Think of a blessing you have misused recently—perhaps your wealth, health, or time. Will you continue to blame circumstances or others, or will you repent and realign your use of that blessing to what pleases Allah?

Evils from Human Action: Jewish Scholars and Maimonides
The author cites Jewish scholars and Maimonides to show that evils come from human action, not directly from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Jewish scholars and Maimonides argued that evils originate from human excess and choice, not directly from God's creation.

Core Idea
Evils stem from human pursuit of excess, not from God's decree.

In this passage, the author draws on two sources from the Jewish tradition—a midrash and Maimonides' Guide of the Perplexed—to reinforce the argument that God does not create evil directly. The midrash states plainly that no evil descends from above, while Maimonides provides a detailed psychological explanation of how human desires for non-necessities lead to suffering and blame of God. This external witness supports the Islamic theological position on the origin of evil.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Jewish scholarly approach
The author notes that Jewish scholars treat the issue of evil as containing clear (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih) verses, citing a midrash that says no evil comes from above.
The midrash is Bereshit Rabbah 9, a key rabbinic source.
2
Read Maimonides' explanation of necessities and excess
Maimonides distinguishes between limited necessities and infinite desires for luxuries, showing that people pursue excess at great risk.
3
Follow the example of the person who complains
Maimonides describes how someone who seeks wealth and pleasure through dangerous means then blames fate when misfortune strikes.
The example illustrates self-inflicted suffering.
4
Identify the error of the masses
The masses blame God for creating a nature that does not facilitate every vice, but the evil is from their own choices.
5
Reflect on the lesson
This passage shows that most evils are self-inflicted due to human excess, not created by God.
Connect this to broader theological debates.

Source Text

And the scholars of the Children of Israel understand the issue as definitive (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih). For in the ninth chapter of Bereshit Rabbah, its text: 'And no evil descends from above.' And by this they have interpreted the text of Isaiah by referring it to the definitive. And Moses ben Maimon, who died in the year 602 AH, has a sound explanation for some types of evils. And the purpose of his explanation: that they are from people and not from Allah. Ibn Maimon says: 'As for necessities, all are limited and finite. As for excess, it is infinite. If your desire is attached to your vessels being silver, and being gold is more beautiful—and others have taken them as crystal—and perhaps you also will take from emeralds and rubies everything that can exist. Then every ignorant person of corrupt thought remains in distress and sorrow, because he cannot reach what so-and-so has done of excesses. And most often he exposes himself to great dangers, like sailing the sea and serving kings. The utmost of that is to attain those non-necessary excesses. Then when misfortunes befall him in those paths he took, he complains of his decree and fate, and begins to blame time, and wonders at its lack of fairness, how it did not help him to acquire abundant wealth, by which he would find much good, get drunk always, and have many concubines adorned with various types of gold and jewels, until they stir him to intercourse more than his capacity—to enjoy? The existential aim is only the pleasure of this vile one. And here ends the error of the masses. Even they blamed the Creator for this existence which He brought into being with this nature that necessitates these great evils—according to their imagination—because that nature does not help every possessor of vice to attain his vice, until he takes his evil soul to the utmost evil, which has no end.' [Guide of the Perplexed, p. 505]
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an external witness from Jewish tradition to support the argument that evils are not directly from God. The author first notes that Jewish scholars also treat the issue as a matter of muhkam (clear) and mutashābih (ambiguous), citing a midrash (Bereshit Rabbah) that says no evil descends from above, and they interpret Isaiah's statement (God creates evil) by referring it back to the clear verses (like Genesis that everything is good). Then he quotes Maimonides (Ibn Maymun) from his work 'Guide of the Perplexed'. Maimonides offers a rational explanation: necessities are limited, but human desires for excess (al-taṭāwul) are unlimited. People covet luxuries (silver, gold, jewels) and go to great lengths and dangers to obtain them. When they face misfortunes in these pursuits, they blame fate and God. Maimonides says this is the error of the masses (al-jumhūr). They even blame God for creating a nature that does not facilitate everyone's vices, but in reality, the evils they suffer are from their own choices. The quote ends with Maimonides pointing out that the existential aim they seek is merely base pleasure. This illustrates that most evils are self-inflicted due to human excess, not from God's creation of a flawed world.

Key Terms
علماء بني إسرائيل
الموسى بن ميمون
الضروريات
التطاول
غلط الجمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all evils are directly created by God.
Correction: Maimonides and the author argue that most evils result from human excess and misused freedom, not from God's direct creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maimonides as quoted in the passage, what is the main cause of the evils that people complain about?
A) God's direct creation of evil.
B) The nature of the world itself.
C) Human beings' pursuit of excessive non-necessities.
D) The devil's influence.

Key Takeaway
The passage uses an external witness from Jewish tradition to reinforce that evils are not directly from God but result from human choices and excess.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you complained about your fate for a hardship that actually resulted from your own pursuit of excess—like money, status, or pleasure? What would it take for you to stop blaming God and instead examine your choices honestly?
?
Maimonides says the error of the masses is to blame God for not enabling their vices. Where do you see this attitude in your own heart—even subtly—when you feel that God has not given you what you want?
?
The scholars of Israel said 'no evil descends from above.' If you truly believed that, how would your response to your next trial change? What would you do before you pray for relief—ask yourself what role your own choices played?
