Refuting the Claim that the Qur'an Calls Allah a 'Thing'
Sanusi refutes objections from two Qur'anic verses that seem to call Allah 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verses that seem to call Allah 'shay'' do not actually prove that Allah is named 'thing', and how the objections are refuted through proper interpretation.

Core Idea
Apparent verses do not prove Allah is a 'thing'; they require correct interpretation.

Some objectors argue that the Qur'an itself calls Allah a 'thing' based on verses like Q. 6:19 and Q. 28:88. In this passage, Sanusi refutes this claim by showing that these verses either do not refer to Allah or cannot be taken literally. By understanding the refutation, you will see why the divine name 'shay'' is not attributed to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Read the passage containing the objection that two verses prove Allah is called 'thing'. Notice which verses are cited and the logical structure of the objection.
The objectors use the rule of exception: 'everything perishes except His Face' implies the Face is a 'thing'.
2
Examine the refutation of the first objection
Focus on the refutation of the verse 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony?' Notice how Sanusi argues that the answer is an independent sentence unconnected to the question.
The word 'thing' in the question does not refer to Allah; it is a general query.
3
Examine the refutation of the second objection
Look at the refutation of the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'. Understand why a literal reading leads to impossibility (attributing a bodily part to Allah). Then see the metaphorical interpretation from Ibn 'Abbas.
Sanusi shows both the absurdity of literalism and provides a sound allegorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وليس لكم أن تقولوا : الدليل على أنه تعالى مسمى بالشيء قوله تعالى : ( قل : أي شيء : أكبر شهادة ؟ قل : الله شهيد بيني (۲) ) وأيضاً : قوله تعالى : كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه (۳) ) استثني وجهه عن الشيء . والمستثنى يجب أن يكون داخلاً تحت المستثنى منه . قالوا : لأن قوله تعالى : ( قل : أي شيء : أكبر شهادة ؟ ) سؤال وجوابه : ما جاء في قوله : قل : الله شهيد بيني وبينكم ) فإنها جملة تامة مستقلة بنفسها ، لا تعلق لها بما قبلها . فلم تدل هذه الآية على أنه تعالى مسمى بالشيء . أما قوله : ( كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ، فظاهر هذه الآية يقتضي أن يصير كل شيء هالكاً إلا وجه الله . وذلك محال . لأن الوجه هو العضو المخصوص ، وذلك على الله محال . ولأن بتقدير أن يكون جسماً ، فظاهر الآية يقتضي أنه بقي ، ولا يبقى منه إلا الوجه . وذلك محال فثبت : أنه لا يمكن إجراء هذه الآية على ظاهرها ، فلا بد من تأويلها ، وذلك التأويل ما نقل عن ابن عباس أنه قال : « إلا وجهه ، معناه : « إلا العمل الذي يؤتي به لمرضاة الله تعالى : وعلى هذا التقدير ، فيكون المراد بوجه الله : تلك الأعمال . وحينئذ لا تدل الآية على وقوع اسم الشيء على الله تعالى .
They said: You cannot say that the proof that He, exalted is He, is called 'thing' is His saying: (Say: 'What thing is greatest in testimony?' Say: 'Allah is witness between me and you') and also His saying: (Everything is perishing except His Face). They excepted His Face from 'thing', and the excepted must be included in the thing from which it is excepted. They said: Because His saying: (Say: 'What thing is greatest in testimony?') is a question, and its answer is what came in His saying: 'Say: Allah is witness between me and you' – it is a complete independent sentence, with no connection to what came before it. So this verse does not indicate that He, exalted is He, is called 'thing'. As for His saying: (Everything is perishing except His Face), the apparent meaning of this verse requires that everything becomes perishing except the Face of Allah. That is impossible, because the face is a specific organ, and that is impossible for Allah. And even if we suppose that He is a body, the apparent meaning of the verse requires that He remains, and nothing remains of Him except the face – that is also impossible. So it is established that this verse cannot be taken in its apparent meaning, and it must be interpreted. And that interpretation is what was transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas: he said: 'Except His Face' means 'except the deed that is performed seeking the pleasure of Allah, exalted is He.' On this estimation, the intended meaning of 'the Face of Allah' is those deeds. Then the verse does not indicate the occurrence of the name 'thing' upon Allah, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage records an objection and its refutation. The objectors cite two verses to argue that Allah is called 'thing' (shay'). First, Q. 6:19: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness between me and you.' They claim that 'thing' refers to Allah. But the author refutes this by noting that the verse is a question and answer, and the answer 'Allah is witness' is a complete sentence unconnected to the query – thus it does not prove Allah is called 'thing'. Second, Q. 28:88: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' They argue that since 'His Face' is excepted from 'everything', Allah's Face is a 'thing', so Allah is called 'thing'. The author refutes this by showing the impossibility of the literal meaning: if taken literally, it would imply that all of Allah perishes except His face, which is impossible because Allah cannot have a physical organ, and even if He were a body, it would be absurd for only a part to remain. Therefore, the verse must be interpreted metaphorically. The author cites Ibn 'Abbas's interpretation that 'His Face' means 'the deed done for Allah's pleasure', so the verse does not imply Allah is a 'thing'.

Key Terms
الشيء (al-shay')
الوجه (al-wajh)
التأويل (al-ta'wil)
الاستثناء (al-istithna')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The occurrence of 'thing' in Q. 6:19 and 'His Face' in Q. 28:88 proves that Allah is named 'shay''.
Correction: Q. 6:19 does not refer to Allah with 'thing', and Q. 28:88 must be interpreted metaphorically (e.g., as deeds), so these verses do not establish the name 'shay'' for Allah.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection from the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'?
A) He says the verse is abrogated.
B) He argues the literal interpretation leads to impossibility, so it must be interpreted metaphorically.
C) He says 'His Face' refers to the Qur'an.
D) He denies that the verse mentions 'everything'.

Key Takeaway
The two cited verses do not prove that Allah is named 'thing'; the first is disconnected from the query, and the second must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid attributing bodily parts to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a Qur'anic verse that seems to describe Allah in physical terms, do you instinctively accept a literal meaning, or do you pause to consider the impossible consequences and seek the interpretation of the salaf? What steps will you take to ensure you don't fall into anthropomorphism?
?
The objectors in this passage used the Qur'an to support a flawed concept of Allah. Have you ever used a verse out of context or without proper understanding to support a belief you hold? How can this lesson remind you to handle Allah's words with greater care and humility?
?
If someone argues that 'Everything is perishing except His Face' proves Allah has a face, how would you use the reasoning from this lesson to correct them? What does this teach you about the necessity of relying on sound scholarly interpretation rather than your own initial understanding?

Particularization by Connected Evidence: The Context of Praise
Sanusi presents the first connected evidence for particularization based on context of praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a verse's placement in a context of praise can serve as connected evidence for particularizing its general meaning, using the example of 'Creator of everything' in Surat al-An'am.

Core Idea
Praise context particularizes general verses.

Sanusi continues his argument that the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything' is not absolutely general but particularized regarding human actions. He introduces a first connected evidence: the verse occurs in a passage of praise. Since praise and blame cannot apply to the same attribute, the verse must refer only to praiseworthy creations, excluding evil acts. Let's examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi states the principle of permissible particularization using evidence, then introduces connected evidences and begins the first one.
2
Identify the context of praise
The author quotes Surat al-An'am 6:102-103. Observe how the verse is framed: beginning with 'La ilaha illa Huwa' (praise of oneness) and ending with 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (praise of His transcendence). The whole context is praise.
3
Understand the implication for particularization
Since the verse is in a context of praise, the phrase 'Creator of everything' must also be praiseworthy. This implies that it does not include evil or blameworthy creations. Thus, it is particularized.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Note that the source passage is cut off. The author likely concluded that the middle part must also be praise. Think about how this connects to the broader debate about human acts and divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو تقرير قول من يقول : إلا أنا نقول : لا شك أن تخصيص عموم القرآن بالدليل : جائز . وههنا قد وجدت دلائل متصلة بهذا النص، ودلائل منفصلة عنه تقتضي تخصيص هذا العموم بالنسبة إلى أفعال العباد أما الدلائل المتصلة : فمن وجوه : الأول : إن قوله تعالى : ( خالق كل شيء ) مذكور في معرض المدح والثناء . لأنه تعالى قال في سورة الأنعام : ( لا إله إلا هو ، خالق كل شيء . فاعبدوه . وهو على كل شيء وكيل. لا تدركه الأبصار ، وهو يدرك الأبصار ) فأول هذه الآية مدح ، وآخرها مدح ، فوجب أيضاً : أن يكون .
And this is the explication of the saying of one who says: But we say: There is no doubt that particularization (takhsis) of the general of the Qur'an by evidence is permissible. And here there have been found evidences connected to this text, and evidences disconnected from it, which require particularization of this general with respect to the acts of servants. As for the connected evidences: they are of several aspects. The first: That His saying, exalted is He: (Creator of everything) is mentioned in the context of praise and commendation. Because He, exalted is He, said in Surat al-An'am: (There is no god but He, the Creator of everything. So worship Him. And He is over everything a Trustee. Vision does not perceive Him, and He perceives vision.) So the beginning of this verse is praise, and the end of it is praise, so it is also necessary that what is in the middle be...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage continues the author's rebuttal. He states that it is permissible to particularize a general Qur'anic text with evidence. For the verse 'Allah is the Creator of everything', there are both connected and disconnected evidences that particularize it, specifically concerning the acts of servants (i.e., that Allah is not the Creator of human actions?). The author then begins listing the connected evidences. The first connected evidence is that the verse appears in a context of praise: the verse in Surat al-An'am begins with declaring Allah's oneness, then 'Creator of everything', then 'so worship Him', then 'over everything a Trustee', then 'vision does not perceive Him'. The entire context is one of praise and commendation. Since the beginning and end are praise, the middle (which includes 'Creator of everything') must also be praise. The implication is that if the verse were taken to include all things without exception, it might conflict with praise? The passage is cut off, but the argument likely concluded that the verse's placement in a context of praise indicates it is particularized to exclude things that would contradict praise, such as evil acts?

Key Terms
التقرير (al-taqrir)
التخصيص (takhsis)
الدلائل المتصلة (al-dala'il al-muttasila)
المدح (al-madh)
الثناء (al-thana')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that particularization only comes from disconnected evidence like another verse or hadith. But here, the context itself within the same verse can serve as evidence.
Correction: Connected evidence, such as the context of praise, is a valid tool for particularizing general Qur'anic statements. The author shows that a verse set in a praising context cannot include blameworthy objects.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the first connected evidence for particularizing the verse 'Creator of everything'?
A) It is abrogated by another verse.
B) It is mentioned in a context of praise, so it must be about praiseworthy things.
C) It is a Meccan verse.
D) It follows a command to worship.

Key Takeaway
Connected evidence from praise context shows that 'Creator of everything' is particularized to exclude blameworthy creations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah creates only what is praiseworthy, then where do your sins come from? Are you willing to stop blaming Allah for your shortcomings and instead take full responsibility for your own choices?
?
How does knowing that the phrase 'Creator of everything' is restricted to good things in a context of praise change the way you view your abilities and successes? Do you attribute every good deed to Allah's creation and every mistake to your own acquisition?
?
When you recite verses about Allah's creation, do you remember that His creation is always good, and that evil is a product of your own actions? How will you let this lesson reshape your gratitude and repentance starting today?

Answer to the Objection on Asking to be Made Muslims
The author responds to the objection that the dua 'make us Muslims' asks for what is already possessed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational responses to the objection that the dua 'make us Muslims' is impossible because it asks for what is already possessed.

Core Idea
Asking for what you already have can be a request for its future recreation or increase.

This passage addresses a subtle theological objection related to dua. The opponent argues that asking to be made Muslims when one is already Muslim is an impossibility, like asking for what is already obtained. The author provides two distinct answers, each rooted in the nature of accidents and the concept of increase in faith. These answers not only refute the objection but also clarify key Islamic doctrines about the persistence of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection presented at the beginning of the passage. Why would asking to be made Muslims be considered impossible? The opponent argues it is a request for what is already possessed.
This objection is based on a logical principle: asking for something already possessed is futile.
2
First answer: accident and persistence
The author's first answer explains that Islam is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that does not persist; it must be recreated each moment. Therefore, requesting it in the future is not the same as requesting what is already present now.
This answer relies on the kalām concept of the non-persistence of accidents.
3
Second answer: increase in faith
The second answer interprets the dua as a request for increase in Islam or faith, supported by Quranic verses about increasing faith and guidance.
Examples: Surah al-Fath and Surah Muhammad, and the example of Abraham asking for reassurance.
4
Reflect on the combined force
Consider how both answers together refute the objection. They show that even if the dua is not for future creation, it can be for increase. The objection is thus invalid.
This demonstrates the depth of Islamic theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله: «لما كانا مسلمين، كان إقدامها على طلب الإسلام طلبا لتحصيل الحاصل. وهو محال»، فنقول: الجواب عنه من وجوه: الأول: إن الإسلام عرض قائم بالقلب، والعرض لا يبقى. فقوله: اجعلنا مسلمين لك، معناه: طلب أن يخلق الله ذلك العرض في قلبه في الزمان المستقبل. ومعلوم أن طلب تحصيله في الزمان المستقبل، لا ينافي كونه حاصلا في الحال. الثاني: أن يكون المراد منه: الزيادة في الإسلام، كقوله تعالى: ليزدادوا إيمانا مع إيمانهم ((١)) وقوله: (والذين اهتدوا زادهم هدى ((٢))) وقال إبراهيم عليه السلام: (ولكن ليطمئن قلبي ((٣))) ((١)) سورة الفتح، آية: 1. 17: ((٢)) سورة محمد، آية: ((٣)) سورة البقرة، آية: 260
As for his statement: 'Since they were Muslims, their undertaking to ask for Islam would be a request to obtain what is already obtained, and that is impossible,' we say: The answer to it is from aspects: First: Islam is an accident subsisting in the heart, and the accident does not persist. So his statement: 'Make us Muslims for You' means a request that God create that accident in his heart in the future time. And it is known that requesting its obtaining in the future time does not contradict its being obtained in the present. Second: That what is intended by it is increase in Islam, like His statement, exalted: 'That they may increase in faith upon their faith' ((1)) and His statement: 'And those who are guided, He increases them in guidance' ((2)) and Abraham, peace be upon him, said: 'But that my heart may be reassured' ((3)). ((1)) Surah al-Fath, verse: 1. 17: ((2)) Surah Muhammad, verse: ((3)) Surah al-Baqarah, verse: 260
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that asking 'make us Muslims' (as in the Quranic dua of Abraham and Ishmael) is impossible because they were already Muslims, so it would be asking for what is already possessed. The author gives two answers. First, Islam is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that does not persist over time; it must be recreated at each moment. Therefore, the dua requests the future creation of that accident, which is not contradictory to its present existence. Second, the dua could be for increase in Islam or faith, similar to Quranic verses that speak of increasing faith (Surah al-Fath 4) and guidance (Surah Muhammad 17), and the example of Abraham asking for reassurance (al-Baqarah 260). Thus the objection is invalid.

The Fourth Proof: Zakariyya's Prayer and the Meaning of 'Pleasing'
Cites Quran 19:6, addresses objection, and interprets 'pleasing' as in religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how context from the Quran and consensus of commentators establish that Zakariyya's prayer for an heir refers to religious inheritance, and that 'pleasing' means righteousness in religion.

Core Idea
Context and prophetic priority show that 'pleasing' means religious righteousness.

This lesson examines the fourth proof from Surah Maryam, where Zakariyya prays for a son. An objection is raised that 'pleasing' could mean physical perfection. The author responds by pointing to the preceding context of inheritance of religion and prophethood, supported by commentator consensus and prophetic priorities. This lesson shows how ambiguous terms are resolved by context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the structure of proof, objection, and answer become clear.
Notice the objection begins with 'فإن قيل' (if it is said).
2
Understand the objection
Identify what the objection claims: that 'pleasing' could refer to physical and moral perfection, which are also from God. Reflect on why this might seem plausible.
The objection lists several physical and moral traits.
3
Analyze the answer
The author uses the preceding context of 'inheritance' to argue that it refers to religion and prophethood, not wealth. Then he applies the principle that an ambiguous word ('pleasing') should be interpreted in light of what preceded.
Two proofs support this: consensus of commentators and Zakariyya's priority as a prophet.
4
Learn the principle
The key hermeneutical point: 'carrying the summary word upon what is previously mentioned is more appropriate than carrying it upon something extraneous.' This is a general rule for interpreting ambiguous terms.
This principle helps avoid arbitrary interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قوله تعالى : ( واجعله رب رضيا (1) ) فإن قيل : عليه الأسئلة التي تقدم ذكرها في الحجة الأولى . فالذي نزيده ههنا أن نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد كونه رضيا في الخلقة والصورة . وهو كونه تام الخلقة ، الحواس ، كامل العقل ، شديد القوة ، طلق الوجه ، حسن الصورة ، موصوفاً بالأخلاق الحميدة ، محبوباً بين الناس ؟ وكل ذلك من أفعال الله تعالى . والجواب : إن مقدمة الآية هي قوله : ( يرثني ، ويرث من آل يعقوب (2) ) : وهذه الوراثة هي وراثة الدين والنبوة ، لا وراثة المال . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن جمهور المفسرين قالوا بذلك . والثاني : إن عمل مبالغة الرسول المعصوم وهو زكريا عليه السلام على حفظ مصالح الدين ، أولى من عمله على حفظ مصالح الدنيا ، فثبت : أن المراد من قوله يرثني ويرث من آل يعقوب وراثة الدين والنبوة. ثم إنه تعالى قال بعده : ( واجعله رب رضياً ) فوجب أن يكون مراده منه : كونه رضياً في الدين . لأن حمل اللفظ المجمل على المذكور السابق ، أولى من حمله على الشيء الأجنبي .
The fourth proof: His saying, the Exalted: 'And make him, my Lord, pleasing.' If it is said: upon it are the questions that were previously mentioned in the first proof. So what we add here is that we say: why is it not permissible that what is meant is his being pleasing in creation and form, i.e., being perfect in creation, senses, complete in intellect, strong, cheerful in face, beautiful in form, described with praiseworthy morals, beloved among people? And all of that is from the acts of God Almighty. The answer: The premise of the verse is His saying: 'He inherits from me and inherits from the family of Jacob.' And this inheritance is the inheritance of religion and prophethood, not the inheritance of wealth. Two aspects indicate it: First, the majority of commentators said so. Second, the action of the infallible messenger, Zakariyya, peace be upon him, in being concerned with the interests of religion is more appropriate than his being concerned with worldly interests. So it is established that what is meant by his saying 'he will be pleasing' is his being pleasing in religion, because carrying the summary word upon what is previously mentioned is more appropriate than carrying it upon something extraneous.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof from the verse (19:6) and addresses an objection. The objection suggests that 'pleasing' could refer to physical and moral perfection, which are also from God. The answer relies on context: the preceding phrase 'he inherits from me and from the family of Jacob' refers to inheritance of religion and prophethood, not wealth. Two proofs are given: (1) majority of commentators hold this; (2) Zakariyya's concern as a prophet is more fittingly for religious matters. Therefore, 'pleasing' must mean being pleasing in religion. The principle is that an ambiguous word is best interpreted by the preceding context.

The Proof from Allah's Universal Power
The author presents the proof from Qur'an 2:20.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth scriptural proof for Allah's creation of human actions, based on Qur'an 2:20.

Core Idea
The servant's act is a thing, so Allah's power must extend to it.

In this passage, the author presents a simple but powerful proof from the Qur'an. The verse 'Allah is over all things competent' is taken to mean that every thing, including the servant's act, falls under Allah's power. The proof then draws a logical conclusion about where the act must originate.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and initial claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the author cites the verse 'Allah is over all things competent' and immediately applies it to the act of the servant.
The key step is that the servant's act is a 'thing' (shayʾ), so it is covered by the verse.
2
Follow the logical argument
Trace the reasoning: if the servant's act is within Allah's power, and if the act occurred by the servant's power alone, then Allah would be unable to create it (since it already exists), which is absurd.
The argument assumes that re-creating something already existing is impossible.
3
Reflect on the key premise
Consider why the author claims that if the act occurred by the servant's power, it would lead to the servant rendering Allah incapable. This is the core of the proof.
The proof relies on the principle that bringing into existence what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd) is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله : ( إن الله على كل شيء قدير ) ومجيء ما يجري مجرى هذا اللفظ في القرآن كثير . فنقول : إن فعل العبد شيء ، فوجب أن يكون الله قادراً عليه ، بحكم هذا النص ، فثبت : أن مقدور العبد ، مقدور الله تعالى ، فوجب أن يقع بقدرة الله تعالى . لأنه لو وقع بقدرة العبد ، لامتنع على الله تعالى إيقاعه. لأن إيجاد الموجود محال . وذلك يفضي إلى أن العبد يعجز عن فعله . وذلك محال
فإن قيل : قوله : ( إن الله على كل شيء قدير ) عام دخله التخصيص من وجوه : منها : إن ذات الله وصفاته أشياء ، وهو غير قادر عليها . ومنها : إن الجواهر - حال بقائها - أشياء . وهو تعالى غير قادر عليها . لأن إيجاد الموجود محال . ومنها : إن الحركة شيء ، ثم إن إيجادها حال حصول ضدها محال . ومنها : إن الجوهر شيء ، ثم إن إيجاده حال عدم العرض محال . فثبت : أن هذا عام دخله التخصيص . فتصير دلالة هذا العام ظنية
·
سلمنا : أن مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى ، فلم قلتم : إنه يجب وقوعه بقدرة الله تعالى ، وما ذكرتموه من التعجيز ، فهو لازم عليكم . لأنه تعالى إذا خلق شيئاً ، امتنع عليه أن يخلقه مرة أخرى. فيلزمكم أن تقولوا : إن الله تعالى أعجز نفسه ، وهو محال
·
والجواب : إنا ذكرنا مراراً : أن هذه الدلائل السمعية ظنية. وأما قوله : ه [؟] يلزمكم أن الله إذا خلق شيئاً ، أن يكون قد أعجز نفسه، قلنا : الإعجاز هو أن يصير بحيث لا يمكنه أن يفعل ما كان يمكنه أن يفعل ، بسبب منفصل . فالمقدور الواحد ، إذا كان مقدوراً لله تعالى وللعبد . فإذا فعله العبد ، امتنع بهذا السبب أن يفعله الله تعالى، فكان هذا تعجيزاً . لأن هذا التعذر إنما جاء بسبب منفصل ، بخلاف ما إذا خلق الله ذلك الفعل . فإن التأثير قد حصل منه وبه ، فكيف يكون تعجيزاً ؟
The eighth proof: His statement: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' (Quran 2:20) and what runs in the course of this expression is frequent in the Quran. So we say: The act of the servant is a thing, so it is necessary that Allah be powerful over it, by the ruling of this text. Thus it is established: that that which is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah, may He be exalted. So it is necessary that it occurs by the power of Allah, may He be exalted. Because if it occurred by the power of the servant, it would be impossible for Allah, may He be exalted, to bring it about, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. And that leads to the servant being incapable of his act, and that is impossible.
If it is said: His statement: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' is a general (ʿāmm) statement that has been subject to specification (takhṣīṣ) in several ways: among them, that the essence of Allah and His attributes are things, and He is not powerful over them. And among them, that atoms (jawāhir) in their state of continuance are things, and He, exalted, is not powerful over them, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. And among them, that motion is a thing, and bringing it into existence while its opposite is occurring is impossible. And among them, that the atom is a thing, and bringing it into existence while the accident (ʿaraḍ) is absent is impossible. So it is established: that this general term has been subject to specification, so the indication (dalāla) of this general term becomes conjectural (ẓanniyya).
We grant that what is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah, may He be exalted. But why do you say that it must occur by the power of Allah, may He be exalted? And what you have mentioned regarding incapacitation (taʿjīz) necessarily applies to you: because when He, exalted, creates something, it is impossible for Him to create it again. So it necessarily follows for you that you say that Allah, exalted, has incapacitated Himself, and that is impossible.
And the answer: We have mentioned repeatedly that these scriptural proofs (dalāʾil samʿiyya) are conjectural. As for his statement: 'It necessarily follows for you that when Allah creates something, He has incapacitated Himself,' we say: Incapacitation (iʿjāz) is that one becomes such that he cannot do what he could have done, due to a separate cause. So one single thing within power (maqdūr wāḥid), when it is within the power of Allah, exalted, and the servant, then if the servant does it, it becomes impossible for that very reason for Allah, exalted, to do it, and this would be incapacitation. Because this impossibility arises from a separate cause, unlike if Allah created that act, for then the effect has come from Him and by Him, so how could it be incapacitation?
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth scriptural proof for the position that human actions are created by Allah. It is a proof (ḥujja) from revelation (samʿiyya) followed by two objections and a detailed answer.

The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' (2:20). The argument is that the servant's act is a 'thing' (shayʾ), so by the universal scope of the verse, Allah must be powerful over it. Therefore, the act is within both the servant's power and Allah's power. The proof then contends that if the act occurred by the servant's power alone, it would be impossible for Allah to bring it about, since creating something already existing is impossible. That would imply the servant renders Allah incapable, which is absurd.

The first objection challenges the proof by arguing that the verse is general (ʿāmm) but has been specified (mukhassas) in several ways: Allah is not powerful over His own essence and attributes; He cannot re-create substances (jawāhir) that already exist; He cannot create motion while its opposite occurs; He cannot create an atom without an accident. Thus the verse's indication is only conjectural (ẓannī), not certain.

The author then grants (sallamnā) the premise that the servant's act is within Allah's power, but the objector raises a second objection: if the same act is within both powers, then when the servant does it, Allah can no longer do it, implying that Allah has incapacitated Himself. This would be impossible.

The answer begins by conceding that these scriptural proofs are conjectural (ẓanniyya). Then it redefines incapacitation (iʿjāz): it occurs only when a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil) prevents the agent from doing what he could otherwise do. For one single thing within power (maqdūr wāḥid), if the servant does it, Allah's inability to do it is due to the servant's act as a separate cause, which is a form of incapacitation. But if Allah Himself creates it, the effect comes from Him without any external preventing cause, so there is no incapacitation.

Key Terms
قدير
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key premise about bringing into existence what already exists?
A) It is possible for Allah
B) It is impossible (muhāl)
C) It is only possible for the servant
D) It is conjectural

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic proof shows that the servant's act must be created by Allah, because otherwise Allah would be unable to create it, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single action you perform—every word, every thought, every movement—is only possible because Allah has power over it and brings it into existence? How would your daily life change if you internalized this belief deeply?
?
If your acts of obedience are created by Allah, then they are a gift from Him. How does this realization affect your gratitude and your fear of showing off (riya)?
?
When you sin, do you reflect that Allah allowed that sin to occur by His power, and that He could have prevented it? How does that humble you and drive you to seek His forgiveness with genuine brokenness?

First Objection: Specification of the General Verse
The objector lists four specifications of the general verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the eighth proof, which argues that the verse is general but has been specified and thus is only conjectural.

Core Idea
A general verse may be specified, making its indication conjectural.

The objector challenges the proof by pointing out that the verse is a general statement (ʿāmm) that has been subject to exceptions (takhṣīṣ). If the verse is not universally applicable, its evidence becomes uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's claim
The objector argues that the verse is general (ʿāmm) but has been specified (mukhassas) in several ways, so its evidence is conjectural (ẓannī).
Specification means that the general term does not apply to all things without exception.
2
Examine each of the four specifications
Read through the four examples: Allah's essence and attributes, substances in their state of continuance, motion while its opposite occurs, and an atom without its accident.
These are cases where Allah is not powerful, so the verse is not truly universal.
3
Understand the conclusion
The objector concludes that because the verse has been specified, its indication becomes conjectural, not certain.
This undermines the proof's certainty if it relies on a general text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فإن قيل : قوله : ( إن الله على كل شيء قدير ) عام دخله التخصيص من وجوه : منها : إن ذات الله وصفاته أشياء ، وهو غير قادر عليها . ومنها : إن الجواهر - حال بقائها - أشياء . وهو تعالى غير قادر عليها . لأن إيجاد الموجود محال . ومنها : إن الحركة شيء ، ثم إن إيجادها حال حصول ضدها محال . ومنها : إن الجوهر شيء ، ثم إن إيجاده حال عدم العرض محال . فثبت : أن هذا عام دخله التخصيص . فتصير دلالة هذا العام ظنية
·
سلمنا : أن مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى ، فلم قلتم : إنه يجب وقوعه بقدرة الله تعالى ، وما ذكرتموه من التعجيز ، فهو لازم عليكم . لأنه تعالى إذا خلق شيئاً ، امتنع عليه أن يخلقه مرة أخرى. فيلزمكم أن تقولوا : إن الله تعالى أعجز نفسه ، وهو محال
·
والجواب : إنا ذكرنا مراراً : أن هذه الدلائل السمعية ظنية. وأما قوله : ه [؟] يلزمكم أن الله إذا خلق شيئاً ، أن يكون قد أعجز نفسه، قلنا : الإعجاز هو أن يصير بحيث لا يمكنه أن يفعل ما كان يمكنه أن يفعل ، بسبب منفصل . فالمقدور الواحد ، إذا كان مقدوراً لله تعالى وللعبد . فإذا فعله العبد ، امتنع بهذا السبب أن يفعله الله تعالى، فكان هذا تعجيزاً . لأن هذا التعذر إنما جاء بسبب منفصل ، بخلاف ما إذا خلق الله ذلك الفعل . فإن التأثير قد حصل منه وبه ، فكيف يكون تعجيزاً ؟
The eighth proof: His statement: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' (Quran 2:20) and what runs in the course of this expression is frequent in the Quran. So we say: The act of the servant is a thing, so it is necessary that Allah be powerful over it, by the ruling of this text. Thus it is established: that that which is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah, may He be exalted. So it is necessary that it occurs by the power of Allah, may He be exalted. Because if it occurred by the power of the servant, it would be impossible for Allah, may He be exalted, to bring it about, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. And that leads to the servant being incapable of his act, and that is impossible.
If it is said: His statement: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' is a general (ʿāmm) statement that has been subject to specification (takhṣīṣ) in several ways: among them, that the essence of Allah and His attributes are things, and He is not powerful over them. And among them, that atoms (jawāhir) in their state of continuance are things, and He, exalted, is not powerful over them, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. And among them, that motion is a thing, and bringing it into existence while its opposite is occurring is impossible. And among them, that the atom is a thing, and bringing it into existence while the accident (ʿaraḍ) is absent is impossible. So it is established: that this general term has been subject to specification, so the indication (dalāla) of this general term becomes conjectural (ẓanniyya).
We grant that what is within the power of the servant is within the power of Allah, may He be exalted. But why do you say that it must occur by the power of Allah, may He be exalted? And what you have mentioned regarding incapacitation (taʿjīz) necessarily applies to you: because when He, exalted, creates something, it is impossible for Him to create it again. So it necessarily follows for you that you say that Allah, exalted, has incapacitated Himself, and that is impossible.
And the answer: We have mentioned repeatedly that these scriptural proofs (dalāʾil samʿiyya) are conjectural. As for his statement: 'It necessarily follows for you that when Allah creates something, He has incapacitated Himself,' we say: Incapacitation (iʿjāz) is that one becomes such that he cannot do what he could have done, due to a separate cause. So one single thing within power (maqdūr wāḥid), when it is within the power of Allah, exalted, and the servant, then if the servant does it, it becomes impossible for that very reason for Allah, exalted, to do it, and this would be incapacitation. Because this impossibility arises from a separate cause, unlike if Allah created that act, for then the effect has come from Him and by Him, so how could it be incapacitation?
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth scriptural proof for the position that human actions are created by Allah. It is a proof (ḥujja) from revelation (samʿiyya) followed by two objections and a detailed answer.

The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse: 'Indeed Allah is over all things competent' (2:20). The argument is that the servant's act is a 'thing' (shayʾ), so by the universal scope of the verse, Allah must be powerful over it. Therefore, the act is within both the servant's power and Allah's power. The proof then contends that if the act occurred by the servant's power alone, it would be impossible for Allah to bring it about, since creating something already existing is impossible. That would imply the servant renders Allah incapable, which is absurd.

The first objection challenges the proof by arguing that the verse is general (ʿāmm) but has been specified (mukhassas) in several ways: Allah is not powerful over His own essence and attributes; He cannot re-create substances (jawāhir) that already exist; He cannot create motion while its opposite occurs; He cannot create an atom without an accident. Thus the verse's indication is only conjectural (ẓannī), not certain.

The author then grants (sallamnā) the premise that the servant's act is within Allah's power, but the objector raises a second objection: if the same act is within both powers, then when the servant does it, Allah can no longer do it, implying that Allah has incapacitated Himself. This would be impossible.

The answer begins by conceding that these scriptural proofs are conjectural (ẓanniyya). Then it redefines incapacitation (iʿjāz): it occurs only when a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil) prevents the agent from doing what he could otherwise do. For one single thing within power (maqdūr wāḥid), if the servant does it, Allah's inability to do it is due to the servant's act as a separate cause, which is a form of incapacitation. But if Allah Himself creates it, the effect comes from Him without any external preventing cause, so there is no incapacitation.

Key Terms
تخصيص
ظنية
عام
مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does the specification make the verse's indication conjectural?
A) Because the verse is weak
B) Because the verse has exceptions, so its general claim is not universally true
C) Because the verse is ambiguous
D) Because the verse is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The first objection attempts to weaken the proof by showing that the verse is not universally applicable, reducing its certainty to conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah is not powerful over certain things like His own essence or re-creating what already exists, does that make you feel He is less powerful? Or do you see it as affirming that His power is beyond our limited understanding of 'things'? How can you trust His power more deeply despite these logical boundaries?
?
The objection reduces the certainty of the verse to conjecture. Have you ever felt that a Quranic argument you leaned on was less certain than you thought? How do you respond when a proof you relied on seems weakened? Will you seek stronger proofs or trust that the Quran's overall message remains firm?
?
This lesson shows that even general Quranic verses can be specified. Does this make you hesitant to use other general verses as proofs? Or does it teach you to always consider context and scholarly interpretation? What is one area of your faith where you need to move from absolute certainty to humble acceptance of nuance?

Second Objection and Answer: The Problem of Self-Incapacitation
Second objection and detailed answer redefining incapacitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection about divine self-incapacitation and the author's answer redefining incapacitation.

Core Idea
Incapacitation means being prevented by a separate cause, not merely not acting.

The second objection attempts to show that the Ashʿarī position leads to God incapacitating Himself. The author responds by defining incapacitation precisely and showing why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the objection's logic
The objector grants that the servant's act is within Allah's power but asks: if the same act is within both powers, then when the servant does it, Allah cannot do it, implying Allah has incapacitated Himself.
The objection uses the principle that if an act is within power, not being able to do it is a limitation.
2
Note the author's initial concession
The author first concedes that these scriptural proofs are conjectural, but then answers the specific objection about self-incapacitation.
This shows the author is not claiming absolute certainty for the proof.
3
Analyze the answer and definition of i'jāz
The author defines incapacitation as becoming unable to do what one could do due to a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil). Therefore, when the servant does the act, Allah's inability is due to the servant's act as a separate cause, not from Himself. This is not self-incapacitation.
Contrast with when Allah creates the act, there is no separate cause preventing repetition.

The Ninth Proof: Victory and Human Acts
The author presents the ninth proof by analyzing the meaning of 'victory'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse 'Victory is from Allah alone' serves as a proof that human actions are created by Allah.

Core Idea
Victory includes human acts; if it's only from Allah, so are those acts.

This lesson presents the ninth scriptural proof from the author, based on Qur'an 3:126. The author argues that since victory over an opponent involves physical or intellectual actions by the servant, and the verse declares victory to be exclusively from Allah, it follows that those human actions are brought into existence by Allah. Let us examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with the verse and then defines victory.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two kinds of victory
The author lists two ways victory occurs: through bodily acts (like wrestling) and through intellectual acts (like argument). Find these in the text.
These are both acts of the servant.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since victory is only from Allah, and victory here includes these acts, then those acts must also be from Allah, i.e., created by Him.
This is the proof's core deduction.
4
Reflect on the proof type
This is a scriptural (samʿī) proof relying on the Qur'an. Note how the author uses linguistic analysis to derive the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : ( وما النصر إلا من عند الله ) ومعلوم ان كون الإنسان منصوراً على الغير، إما أن يكون بأفعال الجوارح . كما إذا تصارع رجلان ، ثم إن أحدهما يصرع الآخر ، أو بالعلم واللسان ، كما إذا أغلب أحدهما صاحبه بالحجة والبرهان . فإذا كان النصر لا معنى له إلا ذلك وكل ذلك فعل العبد ، ثم دل هذا النص، على أن النصر ليس إلا من الله ، ثبت : أن فعل العبد حاصل بإيجاد الله
فإن قيل : النصر قد يكون بتقوية القلب ، وبالإمداد بالملائكة ، وبإزالة الخوف عن القلب . وكل ذلك من الله . قلنا : هب أن ما ذكرتموه يسمى بالنصر ، إلا أن الأفعال المؤثرة في الغلبة ، لا شك أنها أيضاً مسماة بالنصر . فلما قال تعالى : ( وما النصر إلا من عند الله ) دخل فيه ما ذكرتم وما ذكرنا . وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب
The ninth proof: His statement, exalted: 'And victory is not except from Allah' (Quran 3:126). And it is known that a person's being victorious over another is either through the acts of the limbs, as when two men wrestle and one throws the other, or through knowledge and the tongue, as when one overcomes his companion by proof and demonstration. So if victory has no meaning other than that, and all of that is the act of the servant, then this text indicates that victory is not except from Allah, so it is established that the act of the servant comes about by Allah's bringing into existence.
If it is said: Victory may be by strengthening the heart, by sending angels, and by removing fear from the heart, and all of that is from Allah. We say: Suppose what you have mentioned is called 'victory'; nevertheless, the acts that effect the overcoming are undoubtedly also named 'victory'. So when He, exalted, said: 'And victory is not except from Allah,' it includes what you mentioned and what we mentioned. And thus the desired conclusion is obtained.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth scriptural proof (al-ḥujja al-tāsiʿa ʿashara) for the view that human actions are created by Allah. It is a proof from revelation (samʿiyya) followed by an objection and answer.

The author quotes the Qur'anic verse: 'And victory is not except from Allah' (3:126). He then analyzes the concept of victory (naṣr). Victory over another can occur either through bodily actions (afʿāl al-jawāriḥ), like wrestling, or through intellectual and verbal means, like argument. These are all acts of the servant. Since the verse declares that victory is exclusively from Allah, it follows that these human acts are originated by Allah (bi-ījād Allāh).

The objector tries to restrict the meaning of 'victory' to divine gifts such as strengthening the heart, sending angels, or removing fear—things that are clearly from Allah alone. The author responds by conceding (hab) that these are called victory, but argues that the human actions that produce overcoming are also called victory. Therefore, the verse's statement that victory is only from Allah encompasses both the divine gifts and the human acts. Thus the conclusion (al-maṭlūb) that human acts are created by Allah is established.

Key Terms
نصر
أفعال الجوارح
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'victory' refers only to spiritual support, not human actions.
Correction: The author shows victory includes bodily and intellectual acts.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'victory' (naṣr) include?
A) Only divine support like strengthening the heart
B) Bodily acts like wrestling and intellectual acts like argument
C) Only acts of the limbs
D) Only acts of the tongue

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic statement that victory is exclusively from Allah implies that the human acts constituting victory are created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a victory in your life—whether in a debate, a competition, or a personal struggle—do you truly believe that the act itself was created by Allah? Or do you secretly attribute it to your own skill and effort? What does your heart say as you read that 'victory is only from Allah'?
?
The author includes intellectual victory, like overcoming someone in argument, as a human act. How often do you rely on your own eloquence and knowledge, forgetting that even the words you speak are brought into existence by Allah? Will you pause before your next argument and ask Allah for real victory?
?
If every act of victory is only from Allah, then your defeats are also from Allah. Can you accept that with the same certainty? When you fail, do you question His wisdom, or do you surrender to His decree? Reflect on a recent failure and see if you truly believed that it was from Allah.

Objection and Response: Victory as Divine Gifts
The author addresses an objection that victory refers only to divine support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that victory means divine gifts like strengthening the heart, and the author's response that it also includes human acts.

Core Idea
Even if victory includes divine gifts, it also includes human acts, so the verse covers both.

After presenting the proof, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps 'victory' in the verse refers exclusively to divine gifts such as strengthening the heart or sending angels, which are clearly from Allah alone. The author responds by conceding that these are called victory, but argues that the human actions that lead to overcoming are also called victory. Therefore, the verse encompasses both, and the conclusion that human acts are created by Allah remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims victory could be strengthening the heart, sending angels, removing fear—all from Allah.
This limits 'victory' to supernatural support.
2
Understand the objection's challenge
If victory is only those divine gifts, then human acts are not included, undermining the proof.
3
Read the author's response
The author says: even if those are called victory, the human acts that cause overcoming are also called victory. So the verse includes both.
The author uses the term 'hāba' (هب) to concede the point.
4
See how the argument is preserved
Since the verse says victory is only from Allah, and human acts are part of victory, they too are from Allah.

Misconception: Some may think that 'victory' in the verse only means divine support, excluding human acts.
Correction: The author demonstrates that victory includes human acts, so the verse encompasses them.

Checkpoint

A) It refers only to bodily actions
B) It refers to strengthening the heart, sending angels, and removing fear
C) It refers exclusively to human effort

Key Takeaway
Despite the objection that victory is divine support, the author shows that human acts are also part of victory, so the verse still proves they are from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something significant, do you instinctively credit your own skill and effort, or do you pause to recognize that even your ability to act is a gift from Allah? How does this lesson force you to reconsider where you draw the line between 'your doing' and 'Allah's doing'? This week, in one success, deliberately say 'This is from Allah' including your own action.
?
The objector tried to exclude human actions from being 'victory' to avoid attributing them to Allah. Where in your life do you subtly do the same—treating certain outcomes as entirely your own doing while labeling others as 'from Allah'? What would change if you accepted that every effective action you take is also a form of victory that only comes from Allah? Commit to one area where you will shift your attribution.
?
If victory includes both divine gifts and your own actions, how does that reshape your gratitude? After your next success, instead of thanking Allah only for 'help,' thank Him for creating the very act of your striving. Write down one concrete way you will express this expanded gratitude in your next moment of achievement.

The Tenth Proof: Birds Hovering as Evidence for Divine Creation of Acts
The author presents the tenth scriptural proof from Quran 16:79, arguing that birds' voluntary hovering is created by Allah, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse about birds hovering serves as a proof that Allah creates all voluntary acts, and how the author defends the apparent meaning against a reinterpretation.

Core Idea
The Quran's words are taken at face value as proof that Allah creates voluntary actions.

This lesson presents a concise scriptural argument from the Qur'an (16:79) that birds' hovering is a voluntary act created by Allah. The proof is straightforward, but includes an objection that tries to reinterpret the verse. The author refutes that objection by insisting on the apparent meaning. This illustrates a key method in theological reasoning: adhering to the ẓāhir unless a clear necessity demands otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and identify the verse and claim
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the author cites the Qur'anic verse and then draws an inference about voluntary acts.
The verse is from Surah An-Nahl (16:79).
2
Understand the logical structure
The author argues that the bird's hovering is voluntary, and since the verse says Allah holds them up, it follows that Allah creates that voluntary act.
This is a direct attribution of the act to Allah.
3
Examine the objection and the author's answer
The objector reinterprets 'holding up' as merely providing instruments. The author rejects this as a departure from the apparent meaning, which is not allowed without proof.
This shows the principle of adhering to ẓāhir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله تعالى : ( ألم يروا إلى الطير ، مسخرات في جو السماء . ما يمسكهن إلا الله ) ومعلوم أن وقوف الطير في الهواء ، فعل اختياري لذلك الطير. وهذا يدل على أن الأفعال الاختيارية للحيوانات مخلوقة الله تعالى
فإن قيل : المراد بالإمساك : إعطاء الآلات والقدر ، التي بها يتمكن الحيوان من فعل ذلك الاستمساك . قلنا : هذا عدول عن الظاهر . والأصل عدمه
The tenth proof: His statement, exalted: 'Do they not see the birds, subjected in the expanse of the sky? None holds them up except Allah' (Quran 16:79). And it is known that the bird's standing still in the air is a voluntary act of that bird. And this indicates that the voluntary acts of animals are created by Allah, may He be exalted.
If it is said: What is meant by 'holding up' is giving the instruments and power by which the animal is able to do that holding up. We say: This is a departure from the apparent meaning (ẓāhir), and the principle is that it is not to be departed from.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth scriptural proof (al-ḥujja al-ʿāshira) for the claim that voluntary actions of animals—and by extension humans—are created by Allah. It is a brief proof with an objection and a concise answer.

The author cites the Qur'anic verse: 'Do they not see the birds, subjected in the expanse of the sky? None holds them up except Allah' (16:79). The argument is that a bird's hovering in the air is a voluntary act (fiʿl ikhtiyārī) of the bird. Since the verse states that Allah alone holds them up (yumsikuhunna), this indicates that the act of hovering is created by Allah (makhlaqa li-llāh taʿālā).

The objector attempts to reinterpret the verse: 'holding up' (imsāk) means that Allah gives the bird the instruments (ālāt) and power (qudra) by which it performs the act itself. The author rejects this as a departure from the apparent meaning (ʿudūl ʿan al-ẓāhir). The principle (al-aṣl) is that one does not depart from the apparent meaning without necessity. Thus the verse is taken at face value as directly attributing the act of holding up to Allah.

Key Terms
طير
مسخرات
إمساك
فعل اختياري
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the verse only says God sustains birds in the sky, not that He creates their voluntary action.
Correction: The author argues that the holding up (imsāk) is itself the voluntary act of hovering, so the verse directly shows that Allah creates that act.

Checkpoint
How does the objector interpret the phrase 'None holds them up except Allah'?
A) It means God directly creates the act of hovering
B) It means God provides the birds with the instruments and power to hover
C) It means the birds hover by their own nature
D) It means angels hold them up

Key Takeaway
The apparent meaning of the Qur'anic verse directly shows that Allah creates the voluntary actions of animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a voluntary action, do you truly believe that Allah creates that act? Or do you still feel a sense of independent power? Examine your heart: do you attribute your successes to your own skill or to Allah's creation?
?
The objector in this passage reinterprets a clear verse to avoid its full implication. Are there verses in your own life that you reinterpret to avoid accountability? What would it cost you to take them at face value?
?
If Allah creates your voluntary acts, then what is your role? Does that make you a mere puppet, or does it heighten your gratitude and reliance? Sit with this tension and let it change how you pray before your next action.

The First Interpretation and Its Refutation
Presentation and refutation of first interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' by Abu Bakr al-Asamm and why the author considers it weak.

Core Idea
Prayer for something obligatory is meaningless.

The author begins by presenting the first interpretation offered by Abu Bakr al-Asamm, who takes the supplication as a request not to be burdened with trials that lead to deviation. Then he refutes it, arguing that according to the Mu'tazili doctrine of 'al-lata'if' (subtle kindnesses), God is obliged to provide all possible guidance, rendering prayer for it superfluous.

Guided Path
1
Read the first interpretation
Read the Arabic passage where the first interpretation is presented. Notice how al-Asamm reinterprets the verse as asking for protection from trials, citing Quranic parallels.
The text includes a parallel example of a similar expression.
2
Examine the refutation
Read the author's refutation. Identify the key premise: if God is obliged to do something, then asking for it is pointless.
The refutation targets the Mu'tazili view, not the interpretation itself directly.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why does the author think this interpretation is weak? Connect it to the broader theological debate about divine obligation and supplication.

الذين في قلوبهم زيغ ) . وأما السادس : وهو أن يحمل ذلك على الحراسة من الشيطان ، ومن شرور النفس . فنقول : ذلك إن كان مقدوراً الله ، فقد وجب فعله ، فلا فائدة في طلبه . وإن لم يكن مقدوراً فلا فائدة في الدعاء . فظهر بما ذكرنا : سقوط جملة هذه الوجوه . ثم نقول : المصير إلى هذه التأويلات. إنما يحسن إذا دل الدليل على تعذر إجراء هذا النص ، فهو الحق الصريح. وإذا كان كذلك ، فكيف يصار فيه إلى التأويل .
When they became deserving of that prevention, it is permissible to say: He, exalted, made them deviate. And the correctness of this aspect is indicated by His saying: (So when they deviated, Allah caused their hearts to deviate) [Surah Al-Saff, verse 5]. He said: (Abu Bakr al-Asamm: its meaning: do not impose upon us trials by which our hearts deviate. And it is like His saying: (And if We had decreed upon them: Kill yourselves or leave your homes, they would not have done it except a few of them) [Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96]. And He said: (And We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, roofs of silver for their houses) [Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33]. So the sum of this aspect is: do not burden us with acts of worship from which we do not feel safe from deviation. And one may say: do not cause me to harm you or to be ill-mannered toward you, meaning do not do to me something by which I become ill-mannered toward you. The third: al-Ka'bi said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') meaning do not name us with the name of the deviant, just as it is said: so-and-so declares so-and-so an unbeliever if he names him an unbeliever. The fourth: al-Jubba'i said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from the path to Paradise and the abode of reward. And its essence returns to the act of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf). So this is the first aspect. Or it may be carried to something else, namely that He, exalted, if He knows that he is a believer at present and knows that if he remains until the second year he will disbelieve, then His saying ('Do not make our hearts deviate') is taken to mean that He causes him to die before he becomes an unbeliever. For if He keeps him alive until the second year, it is like causing him to deviate from the path to Paradise. The fifth: al-Asamm said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from complete reason by creating insanity in us after You guided us with the light of reason. The sixth: Abu Muslim al-Isfahani said: protect us from Satan and from the evils of our souls so that we do not deviate. (1) Surah Al-Saff, verse 5; (2) Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96; (3) Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33. And the answer: As for the first interpretation: it is weak, because their doctrine is that everything possible in the power of Allah to do for them of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf) is obligatory; if He abandoned it, His divinity would be invalidated and He would become ignorant or needy. And the thing that is [obligatory], what need is there for supplication and entreaty in requesting it? As for the second: it is also weak, because the setting right in the obligation: if Allah knows that it has an effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then His doing it would be evil; and if He knows that it has no effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then its existence is like its non-existence with regard to the servant being obedient or disobedient. So there is no benefit in directing supplication toward it. As for the third: that the intended meaning is that Allah not apply to us the name of deviation; its answer has preceded. As for the fourth: its answer is that if His knowledge that he will disbelieve in the second year necessitates that He cause him to die immediately, then His knowledge that he will disbelieve for his entire life would necessitate that He not create him at all. As for the fifth: that it is carried to preserving reason; it is weak, because this verse is connected to what is mentioned before it, namely His saying: (As for those in whose hearts is deviation) [Surah Ali 'Imran, verse 7]. As for the sixth: that it is carried to guarding from Satan and from the evils of the soul; we say: if that is possible for Allah, then it is obligatory for Him to do it, so there is no benefit in requesting it; and if it is not possible, then there is no benefit in supplication. So it has become clear by what we have mentioned: the fall of all these aspects. Then we say: resorting to these interpretations is only appropriate if evidence indicates the impossibility of applying this text literally; but that is the clear truth. And if that is the case, how can one resort to interpretation?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of six alternative interpretations of the Quranic verse 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' (do not make our hearts deviate). The author begins by presenting six interpretive attempts by various scholars (Abu Bakr al-Asamm, al-Ka'bi, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Muslim al-Isfahani) to explain away the apparent meaning that God directly causes deviation of hearts. Then he systematically refutes each interpretation. The first interpretation (by al-Asamm) takes the supplication as asking not to be burdened with trials that cause deviation, similar to other verses. The author refutes it by noting that according to the Mu'tazili doctrine (implied by 'their doctrine'), God is obliged to provide all possible 'al-lata'if' (subtle kindnesses) to guide people, so there would be no need to pray for them; prayer would be superfluous. The second interpretation (al-Ka'bi) suggests the request is not to be called deviants. The author dismisses it as already answered (presumably earlier). The third (al-Jubba'i) interprets it as asking not to be deviated from Paradise; the author refutes that if God's foreknowledge that someone will disbelieve in the future required immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong disbelief would require never creating that person—an absurd conclusion. The fourth (another from al-Asamm) takes it as a request to preserve reason; the author notes the verse's context is about those with deviation in their hearts (Surah Ali 'Imran), so it's about moral deviation, not mental insanity. The fifth (Abu Muslim) sees it as a request for protection from Satan and evil selves; the author argues that if such protection is within God's power, it is obligatory and need not be requested; if impossible, prayer is futile. The author concludes that all these interpretations fail, and the literal meaning—that God is asked not to cause one to deviate—should be upheld, unless evidence forces otherwise, which it does not.

Key Terms
زيغ
ألطاف
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' means 'do not test us with trials that cause deviation'.
Correction: The author argues that this interpretation is weak because it implies God is not already doing what is obligatory, making supplication redundant.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the first interpretation weak?
A) Because it contradicts the apparent meaning.
B) Because it implies God is not fulfilling His obligation to provide subtle kindnesses.
C) Because it relies on weak Quranic parallels.
D) Because al-Asamm was not a reliable scholar.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation fails because it asks for something already obligatory, rendering supplication meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for guidance, do you truly believe that God has already obligated Himself to guide you, or do you treat your dua as a mere formality? How would your prayer change if you realized that asking for what is already guaranteed is an act of faith or a sign of ingratitude?
?
Consider a time you prayed for protection from misguidance. Did your prayer reflect a genuine fear of your own heart's tendency to deviate, or was it a comfortable habit? What would it mean to make that prayer with the same urgency as if you were asking for rescue from a fire?
?
If the supplication 'do not let our hearts deviate' is, according to this refutation, logically redundant given God's obligation, does that redundancy lessen its value for you? Or does it deepen your understanding that the very act of asking is a form of submission to God's ultimate control over your heart?

Second and Third Interpretations Refuted
Presentation and refutation of second and third interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation (al-Ka'bi) and third interpretation (al-Jubba'i's first) and the author's refutations based on theological coherence.

Core Idea
Naming and effect both fail as interpretations.

The author continues by presenting the second interpretation by al-Ka'bi that the verse asks not to be called deviants, and the third by al-Jubba'i that it asks not to be deviated from Paradise. He dismisses the second as already answered and refutes the third by arguing about divine knowledge and its implications for creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ka'bi's interpretation
Read the second interpretation: 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' is reinterpreted as 'do not name us as deviants'. The author says its refutation has preceded (likely earlier in the text).
The refutation is not elaborated here; it assumes prior knowledge.
2
Analyze al-Jubba'i's interpretation
Read the third interpretation: asking not to be deviated from Paradise, which is essentially the same as the first interpretation (subtle kindnesses). The author refutes it by criticizing the underlying logic about divine foreknowledge.
The refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the refutation argument
The author argues that if God's knowledge that someone will disbelieve later necessitates immediate death, then knowledge of lifelong disbelief would necessitate never creating that person - an absurd conclusion.

لأن التسديد في التكليف . إن علم الله أن له أثراً في حمل المكلف على القبيح ، كان فعله من الله قبيحاً . وإن علم أنه لا أثر له في حمل المكلف على فعل القبيح ، كان وجوده كعدمه ، فيما يرجع إلى كون العبد مطيعاً وعاصيا . فلا فائدة في صرف الدعاء إليه وأما الثالث : وهو أن يكون المراد أن لا يوقع الله علينا اسم الزيغ . فقد تقدم جوابه وأما الرابع : فجوابه : أنه لو كان علمه بأنه يكفر في السنة الثانية ، يوجب عليه أن يميته في الحال ، لكان علمه بأنه يكفر طول عمره ، يوجب عليه أن لا يخلقه البتة وأما الخامس : وهو حمله على إبقاء العقل . فضعيف . لأن هذه الآية متعلقة بما هو مذكور قبل هذه الآية . وهو قوله : ( فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيغ ) . وأما السادس : وهو أن يحمل ذلك على الحراسة من الشيطان ، ومن شرور النفس . فنقول : ذلك إن كان مقدوراً الله ، فقد وجب فعله ، فلا فائدة في طلبه . وإن لم يكن مقدوراً فلا فائدة في الدعاء . فظهر بما ذكرنا : سقوط جملة هذه الوجوه . ثم نقول : المصير إلى هذه التأويلات. إنما يحسن إذا دل الدليل على تعذر إجراء هذا النص ، فهو الحق الصريح. وإذا كان كذلك ، فكيف يصار فيه إلى التأويل .
When they became deserving of that prevention, it is permissible to say: He, exalted, made them deviate. And the correctness of this aspect is indicated by His saying: (So when they deviated, Allah caused their hearts to deviate) [Surah Al-Saff, verse 5]. He said: (Abu Bakr al-Asamm: its meaning: do not impose upon us trials by which our hearts deviate. And it is like His saying: (And if We had decreed upon them: Kill yourselves or leave your homes, they would not have done it except a few of them) [Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96]. And He said: (And We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, roofs of silver for their houses) [Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33]. So the sum of this aspect is: do not burden us with acts of worship from which we do not feel safe from deviation. And one may say: do not cause me to harm you or to be ill-mannered toward you, meaning do not do to me something by which I become ill-mannered toward you. The third: al-Ka'bi said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') meaning do not name us with the name of the deviant, just as it is said: so-and-so declares so-and-so an unbeliever if he names him an unbeliever. The fourth: al-Jubba'i said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from the path to Paradise and the abode of reward. And its essence returns to the act of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf). So this is the first aspect. Or it may be carried to something else, namely that He, exalted, if He knows that he is a believer at present and knows that if he remains until the second year he will disbelieve, then His saying ('Do not make our hearts deviate') is taken to mean that He causes him to die before he becomes an unbeliever. For if He keeps him alive until the second year, it is like causing him to deviate from the path to Paradise. The fifth: al-Asamm said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from complete reason by creating insanity in us after You guided us with the light of reason. The sixth: Abu Muslim al-Isfahani said: protect us from Satan and from the evils of our souls so that we do not deviate. (1) Surah Al-Saff, verse 5; (2) Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96; (3) Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33. And the answer: As for the first interpretation: it is weak, because their doctrine is that everything possible in the power of Allah to do for them of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf) is obligatory; if He abandoned it, His divinity would be invalidated and He would become ignorant or needy. And the thing that is [obligatory], what need is there for supplication and entreaty in requesting it? As for the second: it is also weak, because the setting right in the obligation: if Allah knows that it has an effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then His doing it would be evil; and if He knows that it has no effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then its existence is like its non-existence with regard to the servant being obedient or disobedient. So there is no benefit in directing supplication toward it. As for the third: that the intended meaning is that Allah not apply to us the name of deviation; its answer has preceded. As for the fourth: its answer is that if His knowledge that he will disbelieve in the second year necessitates that He cause him to die immediately, then His knowledge that he will disbelieve for his entire life would necessitate that He not create him at all. As for the fifth: that it is carried to preserving reason; it is weak, because this verse is connected to what is mentioned before it, namely His saying: (As for those in whose hearts is deviation) [Surah Ali 'Imran, verse 7]. As for the sixth: that it is carried to guarding from Satan and from the evils of the soul; we say: if that is possible for Allah, then it is obligatory for Him to do it, so there is no benefit in requesting it; and if it is not possible, then there is no benefit in supplication. So it has become clear by what we have mentioned: the fall of all these aspects. Then we say: resorting to these interpretations is only appropriate if evidence indicates the impossibility of applying this text literally; but that is the clear truth. And if that is the case, how can one resort to interpretation?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of six alternative interpretations of the Quranic verse 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' (do not make our hearts deviate). The author begins by presenting six interpretive attempts by various scholars (Abu Bakr al-Asamm, al-Ka'bi, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Muslim al-Isfahani) to explain away the apparent meaning that God directly causes deviation of hearts. Then he systematically refutes each interpretation. The first interpretation (by al-Asamm) takes the supplication as asking not to be burdened with trials that cause deviation, similar to other verses. The author refutes it by noting that according to the Mu'tazili doctrine (implied by 'their doctrine'), God is obliged to provide all possible 'al-lata'if' (subtle kindnesses) to guide people, so there would be no need to pray for them; prayer would be superfluous. The second interpretation (al-Ka'bi) suggests the request is not to be called deviants. The author dismisses it as already answered (presumably earlier). The third (al-Jubba'i) interprets it as asking not to be deviated from Paradise; the author refutes that if God's foreknowledge that someone will disbelieve in the future required immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong disbelief would require never creating that person—an absurd conclusion. The fourth (another from al-Asamm) takes it as a request to preserve reason; the author notes the verse's context is about those with deviation in their hearts (Surah Ali 'Imran), so it's about moral deviation, not mental insanity. The fifth (Abu Muslim) sees it as a request for protection from Satan and evil selves; the author argues that if such protection is within God's power, it is obligatory and need not be requested; if impossible, prayer is futile. The author concludes that all these interpretations fail, and the literal meaning—that God is asked not to cause one to deviate—should be upheld, unless evidence forces otherwise, which it does not.

Key Terms
زيغ
تأويل
تكليف
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse can be interpreted as asking God not to label one as deviant or to die before future sin.
Correction: The author shows these interpretations lead to logical contradictions or are redundant.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the third interpretation (al-Jubba'i's second aspect)?
A) It contradicts the explicit words of the verse.
B) It implies God should kill anyone who will sin in the future.
C) It relies on weak Quranic evidence.
D) It is the same as the first interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Interpretations that rely on naming or avoiding future sin fail because they lead to absurdities or are already covered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself making excuses for a sin—thinking 'God knew I would do this, so it's not entirely my fault'—how does this lesson's refutation of the third interpretation expose the flaw in that reasoning? Will you renounce that excuse today?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have been 'interpreting' your own deviation as something less serious, just as the second interpretation tried to rename the request? What would it cost you to honestly face that deviation and ask God for correction?
?
This lesson shows that interpretations that remove accountability lead to absurdity. In what specific situation are you tempted to shift responsibility for your spiritual state to God's decree, rather than taking ownership of your own choices? What step will you take today to reclaim that responsibility?

Fourth and Fifth Interpretations Refuted
Refutation of fourth and fifth interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth interpretation (al-Jubba'i's second aspect) and fifth interpretation (al-Asamm's second) and their refutations.

Core Idea
Context and purpose refute forced interpretations.

The author now addresses the fourth and fifth interpretations. The fourth, also from al-Jubba'i, involves asking to die before future disbelief, which he already refuted. The fifth, from al-Asamm, interprets the verse as a request to preserve reason. The author refutes the fifth by pointing to the verse's context: it is about moral deviation, not mental insanity.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth interpretation
This is actually a continuation of the third interpretation from the previous lesson. al-Jubba'i's second aspect is presented again, and the author refutes it.
You may notice repetition; the text is dense.
2
Read the fifth interpretation
al-Asamm's second interpretation takes 'deviation' as losing reason (insanity). The author refutes it using the immediate context of the verse in Surah Ali 'Imran.
Context: the verse about 'those in whose hearts is deviation'.
3
Reflect on the contextual argument
Consider how the author uses the surrounding verses to interpret the word 'deviation'. This is a classical method of tafsir.
