Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of six alternative interpretations of the Quranic verse 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' (do not make our hearts deviate). The author begins by presenting six interpretive attempts by various scholars (Abu Bakr al-Asamm, al-Ka'bi, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Muslim al-Isfahani) to explain away the apparent meaning that God directly causes deviation of hearts. Then he systematically refutes each interpretation. The first interpretation (by al-Asamm) takes the supplication as asking not to be burdened with trials that cause deviation, similar to other verses. The author refutes it by noting that according to the Mu'tazili doctrine (implied by 'their doctrine'), God is obliged to provide all possible 'al-lata'if' (subtle kindnesses) to guide people, so there would be no need to pray for them; prayer would be superfluous. The second interpretation (al-Ka'bi) suggests the request is not to be called deviants. The author dismisses it as already answered (presumably earlier). The third (al-Jubba'i) interprets it as asking not to be deviated from Paradise; the author refutes that if God's foreknowledge that someone will disbelieve in the future required immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong disbelief would require never creating that person—an absurd conclusion. The fourth (another from al-Asamm) takes it as a request to preserve reason; the author notes the verse's context is about those with deviation in their hearts (Surah Ali 'Imran), so it's about moral deviation, not mental insanity. The fifth (Abu Muslim) sees it as a request for protection from Satan and evil selves; the author argues that if such protection is within God's power, it is obligatory and need not be requested; if impossible, prayer is futile. The author concludes that all these interpretations fail, and the literal meaning—that God is asked not to cause one to deviate—should be upheld, unless evidence forces otherwise, which it does not.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse can be understood as a request to preserve mental sanity.
Correction: The context shows 'زيغ' refers to moral/spiritual deviation, not insanity.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main evidence against the fifth interpretation (preserving reason)?
A) It contradicts the literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
Contextual coherence within the Quran is used to refute interpretations that take 'deviation' out of its moral sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that if God's foreknowledge of disbelief required immediate death, it would lead to absurdity. Do you truly trust that God's knowledge and actions are perfectly wise, even when you cannot trace the logic? Or do you sometimes feel that God 'should have' acted differently?
?
The author refutes a scholar's interpretation by pointing to the immediate context. Are you willing to subject your own beliefs about Quranic verses to the same rigorous check? Will you commit today to reading verses in their full context before forming an opinion?

The Sixth Interpretation and the Conclusion
Presentation and refutation of sixth interpretation, then conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth interpretation (Abu Muslim) and the author's concluding argument that the literal meaning of the verse should be upheld.

Core Idea
If something is obligatory, prayer is pointless; if impossible, prayer is futile.

The final interpretation by Abu Muslim al-Isfahani interprets the verse as a request for protection from Satan and the soul. The author refutes this with a simple dilemma: if protection is possible for God, it is obligatory, so no need to ask; if impossible, asking is useless. He then concludes that all six interpretations fail, and the literal meaning must be followed unless clear evidence necessitates otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth interpretation
Abu Muslim takes 'do not make our hearts deviate' as asking for protection from internal and external sources of deviation.
2
Examine the refutation dilemma
The author presents a dilemma: if protection is within God's power, it is obligatory (according to Mu'tazili doctrine), so prayer is redundant; if not possible, prayer is futile.
This mirrors the refutation of the first interpretation.
3
Read the conclusion
The author declares that all interpretations have fallen, and the literal meaning remains unless evidence forces otherwise.
This is a key statement about the authority of the apparent meaning.

: : احرسنا من الشيطان ، ومن شرور أنفسنا حتى لا تزيغ (۱) سورة الصف ، آية : ٥ (۲) سورة النساء ، آية : ٩٦ (۳) سورة الزخرف ، آية : ٣٣ والجواب : أما التأويل الأول : فضعيف . لأن مذهبهم : أن كل ما أمكن في قدرة الله تعالى أن يفعل في حقهم من الألطاف ، فقد وجب ذلك وجوباً ، لو ترك لبطلت الهيته ، ولصاو جاهلاً أو محتاجاً. والشيء الذي يكون ، فأي حاجة إلى الدعاء والتضرع في طلبه ؟ وأما الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً . لأن التسديد في التكليف . إن علم الله أن له أثراً في حمل المكلف على القبيح ، كان فعله من الله قبيحاً . وإن علم أنه لا أثر له في حمل المكلف على فعل القبيح ، كان وجوده كعدمه ، فيما يرجع إلى كون العبد مطيعاً وعاصيا . فلا فائدة في صرف الدعاء إليه وأما الثالث : وهو أن يكون المراد أن لا يوقع الله علينا اسم الزيغ . فقد تقدم جوابه وأما الرابع : فجوابه : أنه لو كان علمه بأنه يكفر في السنة الثانية ، يوجب عليه أن يميته في الحال ، لكان علمه بأنه يكفر طول عمره ، يوجب عليه أن لا يخلقه البتة وأما الخامس : وهو حمله على إبقاء العقل . فضعيف . لأن هذه الآية متعلقة بما هو مذكور قبل هذه الآية . وهو قوله : ( فأما الذين في قلوبهم زيغ ) . وأما السادس : وهو أن يحمل ذلك على الحراسة من الشيطان ، ومن شرور النفس . فنقول : ذلك إن كان مقدوراً الله ، فقد وجب فعله ، فلا فائدة في طلبه . وإن لم يكن مقدوراً فلا فائدة في الدعاء . فظهر بما ذكرنا : سقوط جملة هذه الوجوه . ثم نقول : المصير إلى هذه التأويلات. إنما يحسن إذا دل الدليل على تعذر إجراء هذا النص ، فهو الحق الصريح. وإذا كان كذلك ، فكيف يصار فيه إلى التأويل .
When they became deserving of that prevention, it is permissible to say: He, exalted, made them deviate. And the correctness of this aspect is indicated by His saying: (So when they deviated, Allah caused their hearts to deviate) [Surah Al-Saff, verse 5]. He said: (Abu Bakr al-Asamm: its meaning: do not impose upon us trials by which our hearts deviate. And it is like His saying: (And if We had decreed upon them: Kill yourselves or leave your homes, they would not have done it except a few of them) [Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96]. And He said: (And We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, roofs of silver for their houses) [Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33]. So the sum of this aspect is: do not burden us with acts of worship from which we do not feel safe from deviation. And one may say: do not cause me to harm you or to be ill-mannered toward you, meaning do not do to me something by which I become ill-mannered toward you. The third: al-Ka'bi said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') meaning do not name us with the name of the deviant, just as it is said: so-and-so declares so-and-so an unbeliever if he names him an unbeliever. The fourth: al-Jubba'i said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from the path to Paradise and the abode of reward. And its essence returns to the act of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf). So this is the first aspect. Or it may be carried to something else, namely that He, exalted, if He knows that he is a believer at present and knows that if he remains until the second year he will disbelieve, then His saying ('Do not make our hearts deviate') is taken to mean that He causes him to die before he becomes an unbeliever. For if He keeps him alive until the second year, it is like causing him to deviate from the path to Paradise. The fifth: al-Asamm said: ('Do not make our hearts deviate') from complete reason by creating insanity in us after You guided us with the light of reason. The sixth: Abu Muslim al-Isfahani said: protect us from Satan and from the evils of our souls so that we do not deviate. (1) Surah Al-Saff, verse 5; (2) Surah Al-Nisa, verse 96; (3) Surah Al-Zukhruf, verse 33. And the answer: As for the first interpretation: it is weak, because their doctrine is that everything possible in the power of Allah to do for them of subtle kindnesses (al-altaf) is obligatory; if He abandoned it, His divinity would be invalidated and He would become ignorant or needy. And the thing that is [obligatory], what need is there for supplication and entreaty in requesting it? As for the second: it is also weak, because the setting right in the obligation: if Allah knows that it has an effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then His doing it would be evil; and if He knows that it has no effect in compelling the obligated person to do evil, then its existence is like its non-existence with regard to the servant being obedient or disobedient. So there is no benefit in directing supplication toward it. As for the third: that the intended meaning is that Allah not apply to us the name of deviation; its answer has preceded. As for the fourth: its answer is that if His knowledge that he will disbelieve in the second year necessitates that He cause him to die immediately, then His knowledge that he will disbelieve for his entire life would necessitate that He not create him at all. As for the fifth: that it is carried to preserving reason; it is weak, because this verse is connected to what is mentioned before it, namely His saying: (As for those in whose hearts is deviation) [Surah Ali 'Imran, verse 7]. As for the sixth: that it is carried to guarding from Satan and from the evils of the soul; we say: if that is possible for Allah, then it is obligatory for Him to do it, so there is no benefit in requesting it; and if it is not possible, then there is no benefit in supplication. So it has become clear by what we have mentioned: the fall of all these aspects. Then we say: resorting to these interpretations is only appropriate if evidence indicates the impossibility of applying this text literally; but that is the clear truth. And if that is the case, how can one resort to interpretation?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a theological refutation of six alternative interpretations of the Quranic verse 'لا تزغ قلوبنا' (do not make our hearts deviate). The author begins by presenting six interpretive attempts by various scholars (Abu Bakr al-Asamm, al-Ka'bi, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Muslim al-Isfahani) to explain away the apparent meaning that God directly causes deviation of hearts. Then he systematically refutes each interpretation. The first interpretation (by al-Asamm) takes the supplication as asking not to be burdened with trials that cause deviation, similar to other verses. The author refutes it by noting that according to the Mu'tazili doctrine (implied by 'their doctrine'), God is obliged to provide all possible 'al-lata'if' (subtle kindnesses) to guide people, so there would be no need to pray for them; prayer would be superfluous. The second interpretation (al-Ka'bi) suggests the request is not to be called deviants. The author dismisses it as already answered (presumably earlier). The third (al-Jubba'i) interprets it as asking not to be deviated from Paradise; the author refutes that if God's foreknowledge that someone will disbelieve in the future required immediate death, then foreknowledge of lifelong disbelief would require never creating that person—an absurd conclusion. The fourth (another from al-Asamm) takes it as a request to preserve reason; the author notes the verse's context is about those with deviation in their hearts (Surah Ali 'Imran), so it's about moral deviation, not mental insanity. The fifth (Abu Muslim) sees it as a request for protection from Satan and evil selves; the author argues that if such protection is within God's power, it is obligatory and need not be requested; if impossible, prayer is futile. The author concludes that all these interpretations fail, and the literal meaning—that God is asked not to cause one to deviate—should be upheld, unless evidence forces otherwise, which it does not.

Key Terms
زيغ
شيطان
نفس
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse can be interpreted as asking for protection that God may or may not grant.
Correction: The author argues that either protection is already obligatory (so asking is redundant) or impossible (so asking is futile).

Checkpoint
What is the author's final position regarding the six interpretations?
A) Some are acceptable with evidence.
B) All are refuted and the literal meaning stands.
C) The first interpretation is the most plausible.
D) The verse requires figurative interpretation.

Key Takeaway
All interpretations fail; the literal meaning of the supplication is to be accepted as is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for protection from evil, do you truly believe that only Allah can grant it, or do you rely on your own efforts? The dilemma challenges the very purpose of dua—how does this reframing change your heart when you ask?
?
The author insists on the literal meaning unless clear demands forced reinterpretation. Have you ever been tempted to reinterpret a clear command of Allah because it was uncomfortable? What would it cost you to simply accept it as it is?
?
The refutation leaves you with the direct meaning of the supplication. Are you willing to live with that direct meaning, even if it means admitting your own helplessness before the Shaytan and your own soul? How will this admission shape your daily prayers?

The 19th Proof from Quran 5:64: Two Lines of Reasoning
The author enumerates two inferences from Quran 5:64 against the Mu'tazilites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quran 5:64 to argue against the Mu'tazilites by showing that God's actions can necessitate disbelief.

Core Idea
God's causing to hear can necessitate disbelief, opposing Mu'tazilite views.

In this lesson, we examine the 19th proof from Surah Al-Ma'idah verse 64. The author presents two ways of inferring from the verse that God's actions can lead to disbelief, which contradicts the Mu'tazilite position that God does not create evil. The first way focuses on the increase in transgression, and the second on the casting of enmity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'في وجهين' indicating two approaches.
2
Understand the First Inference
Focus on the first way: how God's causing them to hear the verses necessitates an increase in inclination towards disbelief. This builds on the principle of preponderance (رجحان).
The author assumes that when preponderance occurs, the act becomes necessary.
3
Understand the Second Inference
The second way uses the phrase 'وألقينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء' and refers back to a previous argument. Note how it extends the same logic.
The author does not repeat the full reasoning but says 'كما تقدم' (as previously mentioned).
4
Connect to the Mu'tazilite Debate
Consider why this proof challenges the Mu'tazilites: they hold that God cannot create evil or necessitate disbelief. This verse suggests otherwise.
5
Reflect on Logical Structure
Identify the key terms: إسماع (causing to hear), ترجيح (preponderance), وجوب الفعل (necessity of action). See how they form a chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة عشر : قوله تعالى : ( وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ما أنزل إليك ، من ربك : طغياناً وكفراً . وألقينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء إلى يوم القيامة (١) ) والاستدلال به من وجهين :
الأول : إن قوله : ( وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ، ما أنزل إليك من ربك : طغياناً وكفراً ، معناه : أن إسماع الله إياهم هذه الآيات ، اقتضى أن يحصل لهم زيادة ميل ورغبة في الكفر . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إسماع تلك الآيات ، اقتضى ترجيح فعل الكفر على فعل الإيمان . وقد دللنا على أن عند حصول الرجحان يجب الفعل . فثبت : أن إسماع الله تعالى إياهم هذه الآيات ، يستلزم حصول الكفر . وذلك ينافي قول المعتزلة .
والوجه الثاني في الاستدلال : قوله تعالى : ( وألقينا بينهم العداوة والبغضاء إلى يوم القيامة ) والاستدلال به كما تقدم .
(١) سورة المائدة ، آية : ٦٤
The nineteenth proof: His statement, exalted is He: 'And it will increase many of them what has been revealed to you from your Lord in transgression and disbelief. And We have cast among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection' (1). And the inference from it is in two ways: The first: Indeed His statement: 'And it will increase many of them what has been revealed to you from your Lord in transgression and disbelief' means that God's causing them to hear these verses necessitated that they acquire an increased inclination and desire for disbelief. If this is established, we say: Causing them to hear those verses necessitated a preponderance of the act of disbelief over the act of faith. And we have already demonstrated that when preponderance occurs, the act is necessitated. So it is established that God's causing them to hear these verses entails the occurrence of disbelief. And that contradicts the statement of the Mu'tazilites. The second way of inference: His statement, exalted is He: 'And We have cast among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection.' And the inference from it is as previously mentioned. (1) Surah Al-Ma'idah, verse 64.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the 19th proof from Quran 5:64 and develops it through two lines of reasoning. The first line focuses on the part of the verse about increase in transgression and disbelief. The author argues that God's causing the people to hear these verses necessarily produces an increased inclination towards disbelief. Since it has been established earlier that when a preponderance (رجحان) exists, the action becomes necessary, it follows that God's action of causing them to hear leads necessarily to disbelief. This contradicts the Mu'tazilite position that God does not create evil. The second line of inference uses the part about casting enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection, and the argument is similar to what was previously stated (referring to an earlier proof). The footnote identifies the verse as Surah Al-Ma'idah, verse 64.

Checkpoint

D) The command to fight

Key Takeaway
Quran 5:64 provides a proof that God's actions can necessitate disbelief, opposing Mu'tazilite theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear a verse from the Quran, do you notice any change in your heart? Could it be that, like those mentioned in the verse, your listening actually increases your distance from faith if you hear with arrogance or rejection? What will you do today to ensure your hearing draws you closer to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazilites could not accept that God's actions might directly cause someone's disbelief. But the Quran tells you plainly that He does. Is there a part of you that still questions God's justice when you see people disbelieve? Can you, right now, submit to the truth that whatever God wills is perfectly wise, even if you don't understand?
?
You have read that God's causing to hear can necessitate disbelief. But what about your own hearing of this very lesson? Are you listening with a heart that seeks increase in guidance, or are you only going through the motions? Pause and ask yourself: after today, will you be more cautious about how you engage with Allah's words?

The Hadith of Jibril and Its Textual Variations
Author presents the fourth point of criticism using the hadith of Jibril's textual variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the textual variations in the hadith of Jibril as the fourth point of criticism against Abu Hurairah's narrations.

Core Idea
Fame does not guarantee textual uniformity.

This lesson explores a critical argument against the reliability of Abu Hurairah's hadith. The author points to one of the most famous hadiths—the hadith of Jibril on Islam, Iman, and Ihsan—and demonstrates that even this widely transmitted report suffers from significant additions and omissions. By examining a specific version recorded by al-Jawzaqi, you will see how textual inconsistencies can undermine confidence in a narration's exact wording.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the criticism's outline
Read the opening lines where the author states that the hadith of Jibril is the most famous sound hadith, yet it is extremely perturbed due to additions and omissions. Note that this is the fourth point in a series of criticisms.
The author is building a cumulative case against Abu Hurairah's reliability.
2
Examine the cited version
Read the version from Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi's 'Al-Muttafaq Bayna al-Shaykhayn'. Observe the narration chain and the content: definitions of Iman, Islam, Ihsan, and signs of the Hour.
This version is presented as an example of the hadith's content, but note the variations.
3
Understand al-Jawzaqi's comment
Al-Jawzaqi states that al-Bukhari and Muslim did not agree on the authenticity of this specific version, and that there are multiple chains with additions. Reflect on why this is significant for the criticism.
The lack of agreement between the two major hadith compilers weakens the hadith's certainty.
4
Connect to the broader criticism
Consider how this example supports the author's argument: even a famous hadith like Jibril's has textual instability, so Abu Hurairah's narrations may not be as reliable as claimed.
This is a specific application of the general criticism of Abu Hurairah's hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع في تقرير هذا الطعن : إنه ليس في جملة الأخبار الصحيحة أظهر ولا أشهر عند المحدثين من الخبر المشتمل على شرح الإسلام والإيمان والإحسان . ثم إنه اضطرب هذا الخبر ، اضطرابا شديدا ، بسبب ما فيه من الزيادات والنقصانات . ونحن نذكر القدر الذي ذكره الشيخ أبو بكر الجوزقي في كتابه الذي سماه بالمتفق بين الشيخين . وهو أول خبر أورده في ذلك الكتاب. فروى بإسناده عن أبي زرعة ، عن أبي هريرة قال : به بينا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، يوما بارزاً للناس . فأتاه رجل . وقال يا رسول الله : ما الإيمان ؟ قال : الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته ورسله ولقائه . وتؤمن بالبعث الآخر . قال يا رسول الله : ما الإسلام ؟ قال : أن تعبد الله لا تشرك شيئاً ، وتقيم الصلاة المكتوبة ، وتؤدي الزكاة المفروضة ، وتصوم رمضان . قال يا رسول الله : ما الإحسان ؟ قال : أن تعبد الله كأنك تراه ، فلو أنك لا تراه . فإنه يراك . قال يا رسول الله : متى الساعة ؟ قال : ما المسؤول عنها بأعلم من السائل. وأحدثك عن أشراطها : إذا ولدت الأمة ربتها ، فذلك من أشراطها، وإذا رأيت الحفاة العراة رؤوس الناس ، فذلك من أشراطها . وإذا تطاول رعاة الغنم في البنيان ، فذاك من أشراطها . في خمس لا يعلمهن إلا الله . وتلا : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) (۱) قال الشيخ أبو بكر الجوزقي مصنف كتاب المتفق بين الشيخين : هذا الحديث ما اجتمع الشيخان على صحته ، وله طرق . في بعضها زيادات (۱) سورة لقمان ، آية : ٣٤
The fourth point in establishing this criticism: There is not among the total of sound reports any more apparent or more famous among the hadith scholars than the report containing the explanation of Islam, Iman, and Ihsan. Then this report became extremely perturbed due to what is in it of additions and omissions. And we mention the extent that Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi mentioned in his book which he named 'Al-Muttafaq Bayna al-Shaykhayn' (The Agreed upon between the Two Sheikhs). And it is the first report he presented in that book. He narrated with his chain from Abu Zur'ah, from Abu Hurairah, he said: While the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was one day sitting with the people, a man came to him and said: O Messenger of Allah, what is Iman? He said: Iman is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the meeting with Him, and you believe in the final resurrection. He said: O Messenger of Allah, what is Islam? He said: That you worship Allah, not associating anything with Him, establish the prescribed prayer, give the obligatory zakat, and fast Ramadan. He said: O Messenger of Allah, what is Ihsan? He said: That you worship Allah as if you see Him, for even if you do not see Him, He sees you. He said: O Messenger of Allah, when is the Hour? He said: The one asked about it is not more knowledgeable than the questioner. But I will inform you of its signs: When the slave-girl gives birth to her mistress, that is among its signs; when you see the barefoot, naked [people] as the heads of the people, that is among its signs; when the shepherds of sheep compete in buildings, that is among its signs. [It is] among five [matters] which none knows except Allah. Then he recited: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour' (Surah Luqman, verse 34). Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi, author of the book 'Al-Muttafaq Bayna al-Shaykhayn', said: This hadith, the two Sheikhs [al-Bukhari and Muslim] did not agree on its authenticity, and it has chains; in some of them there are additions.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth point (الوجه الرابع) in the criticism of Abu Hurairah. The author begins by stating that among sound hadith, none is more famous than the hadith that explains Islam, Iman, and Ihsan (the Hadith of Jibril). However, this hadith has severe textual variations (اضطراب شديد) due to additions and omissions. The author then cites a version compiled by Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi in his book 'Al-Muttafaq Bayna al-Shaykhayn', which records the first hadith in that book. The narration from Abu Hurairah describes a man who asked the Prophet about Iman, Islam, Ihsan, and the Hour. The Prophet's answers include definitions and signs of the Hour (e.g., slave-girl giving birth to her mistress, barefoot shepherds becoming leaders, competition in buildings). The author then quotes al-Jawzaqi's note that the two Sheikhs (al-Bukhari and Muslim) did not agree on the authenticity of this specific version, and there are multiple chains with variations. This inconsistency is used to support the criticism of Abu Hurairah's narrations.

حديث جبريل

الإسلام
الإحسان
أشراط الساعة
زيادات
النقصانات
المتفق بين الشيخين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Famous hadiths like the hadith of Jibril are transmitted without any variation and are completely reliable in wording.
Correction: This passage shows that even the famous hadith of Jibril has significant textual additions and omissions, and that scholars like al-Jawzaqi note disagreements over its authenticity, undermining the assumption of perfect transmission.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author cites the hadith of Jibril in this passage?
A) To show it is the most famous hadith.
B) To demonstrate that it has textual variations.
C) To prove Abu Hurairah was a liar.
D) To discuss the signs of the Hour.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Jibril, despite its fame, contains textual variations that weaken its reliability as a precise transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learned that this famous hadith of Jibril has 'additions and omissions', did you feel a twinge of doubt about the reliability of hadith? Examine your heart: is your trust in Allah's preservation of the Sunnah deep enough to accept that scholarly scrutiny strengthens, not weakens, our faith? What will you do to ensure you approach such information with humility and trust in the scholars?
?
The author uses textual variation to criticize Abu Hurairah. Before this lesson, what was your view of him? Now that you have seen the evidence, will you commit to judging the Companions based on comprehensive scholarly research rather than isolated criticisms? Can you name one internal bias you need to overcome to be fair to the Companions?
?
This lesson shows that even widely transmitted hadiths require careful verification. In your own practice, how do you determine whether a hadith you follow is authentic? Starting today, what concrete step will you take to improve your ability to evaluate hadith reports yourself, rather than blindly accepting critiques or praises?

The Fourth Argument and First Narration of the Hadith of Jibril
Author presents fourth argument and quotes the first narration of the hadith of Jibril from al-Jawzaqi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's fourth argument regarding the confusion in the hadith of Jibril and the specific narration from al-Jawzaqi.

Core Idea
The famous hadith of Jibril is not as uniform as commonly thought; it has significant variations.

The author presents a critical argument: even the most famous hadith on the fundamentals of Islam has been transmitted with inconsistencies. He begins by citing the version recorded by Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi, which itself acknowledges the lack of complete agreement by Bukhari and Muslim. This passage sets the stage for examining the textual variations in this important hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's tone: he calls the hadith 'confused' due to additions and omissions.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has a clear two-part structure: first the author's critical argument (up to 'اضطرابا شديدا'), then the hadith narration from al-Jawzaqi. Mark where the shift occurs.
3
Analyze the hadith content
Within the narration, note the definitions of Iman, Islam, Ihsan, and the signs of the Hour. Ask yourself: do these match what you already know?
Pay special attention to al-Jawzaqi's remark at the end: 'this hadith is not agreed upon by the two shaykhs.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع في تقرير هذا الطعن : إنه ليس في جملة الأخبار الصحيحة أظهر ولا أشهر عند المحدثين من الخبر المشتمل على شرح الإسلام والإيمان والإحسان . ثم إنه اضطرب هذا الخبر ، اضطرابا شديدا ، بسبب ما فيه من الزيادات والنقصانات . ونحن نذكر القدر الذي ذكره الشيخ أبو بكر الجوزقي في كتابه الذي سماه بالمتفق بين الشيخين . وهو أول خبر أورده في ذلك الكتاب. فروى بإسناده عن أبي زرعة ، عن أبي هريرة قال : بينا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، يوما بارزاً للناس . فأتاه رجل . وقال يا رسول الله : ما الإيمان ؟ قال : الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته ورسله ولقائه . وتؤمن بالبعث الآخر . قال يا رسول الله : ما الإسلام ؟ قال : أن تعبد الله لا تشرك شيئاً ، وتقيم الصلاة المكتوبة ، وتؤدي الزكاة المفروضة ، وتصوم رمضان . قال يا رسول الله : ما الإحسان ؟ قال : أن تعبد الله كأنك تراه ، فلو أنك لا تراه . فإنه يراك . قال يا رسول الله : متى الساعة ؟ قال : ما المسؤول عنها بأعلم من السائل. وأحدثك عن أشراطها : إذا ولدت الأمة ربتها ، فذلك من أشراطها، وإذا رأيت الحفاة العراة رؤوس الناس ، فذلك من أشراطها . وإذا تطاول رعاة الغنم في البنيان ، فذاك من أشراطها . في خمس لا يعلمهن إلا الله . وتلا : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) قال الشيخ أبو بكر الجوزقي مصنف كتاب المتفق بين الشيخين : هذا الحديث ما اجتمع الشيخان على صحته ، وله طرق . في بعضها زيادات
The fourth aspect in establishing this criticism: There is no hadith among the authentic reports more apparent or more famous among the hadith scholars than the report that includes the explanation of Islam, faith (iman), and excellence (ihsan). Then this report became extremely confused due to what is in it of additions and omissions. And we will mention the portion that Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi mentioned in his book which he named 'Al-Muttafaq bayn al-Shaykhayn.' It is the first report he presented in that book. He narrated with his chain from Abu Zur'a, from Abu Hurairah, who said: While the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was one day sitting with the people, a man came to him and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, what is faith (iman)?' He said: 'Faith is to believe in Allah, His angels, His messengers, and the meeting with Him, and to believe in the Final Resurrection.' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is Islam?' He said: 'To worship Allah and not associate anything with Him, to establish the prescribed prayer, to give the obligatory charity (zakat), and to fast Ramadan.' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is excellence (ihsan)?' He said: 'To worship Allah as if you see Him, and if you do not see Him, indeed He sees you.' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, when is the Hour?' He said: 'The one questioned about it is not more knowledgeable than the questioner. But I will tell you about its signs: when the slave woman gives birth to her mistress, that is one of its signs; when you see the barefoot, naked, destitute shepherds competing in building tall buildings, that is one of its signs. There are five things that none knows except Allah.' Then he recited: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour...' (Luqman 34). Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi, the author of 'Al-Muttafaq bayn al-Shaykhayn,' said: 'This hadith is not agreed upon by the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) as authentic, and it has multiple chains; in some of them there are additions.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument and a presentation of evidence. The author begins by stating the fourth argument (الوجه الرابع) in a broader critique (likely directed against Abu Hurairah). He asserts that the most famous and apparent hadith among authentic reports is the one that explains Islam, Iman, and Ihsan—the famous hadith of Jibril. However, he claims that this hadith has become extremely confused (اضطرب اضطرابا شديدا) due to many additions and omissions. To demonstrate this, he quotes the version recorded by Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi in his book 'Al-Muttafaq bayn al-Shaykhayn' (the agreed-upon between Bukhari and Muslim). Interestingly, al-Jawzaqi himself notes that this hadith is not actually agreed upon by Bukhari and Muslim as authentic, and it has multiple chains with variations. The author then provides the text of the first narration from Abu Hurairah via Abu Zur'a. This narration covers the definitions of Iman, Islam, Ihsan, and the signs of the Hour, including the verse from Surah Luqman. The passage ends with al-Jawzaqi's remark that there are additions in some chains, setting the stage for the subsequent narrations. The author's purpose is to highlight the inconsistency in the hadith, which he uses as grounds for criticism.

الإسلام
الإحسان
أشراط الساعة
المتفق بين الشيخين
أبو بكر الجوزقي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of Jibril is transmitted identically in all authentic collections.
Correction: This passage shows that the hadith has variations, and al-Jawzaqi explicitly states it is not unanimously considered authentic by Bukhari and Muslim.

Checkpoint

B) They did not agree on its authenticity.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Jibril, despite its fame, exists in multiple versions with variations, leading the author to question its reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Second Narration: 'Ask Me' and the Identity of Jibril
Author presents the second narration with the addition 'Ask me' and Jibril's mention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive features of the second narration of the hadith, particularly the command 'Ask me' and the explicit mention that the questioner was Jibril.

Core Idea
This narration reveals the questioner as Jibril, emphasizing his role in teaching the religion.

In this second chain, the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructs the companions to ask, but they hesitate. A man then comes and asks about Islam. The unique addition in this version is the identification of the questioner as Jibril. This variant highlights the depth of the companions' reverence and the divine instruction through the angel. Let's explore this narration and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the narrative flow.
Pay attention to the words 'سلوني' and 'هذا جبريل'.
2
Identify key additions
Notice the two distinctive elements: the Prophet's command 'Ask me' and the line 'This is Jibril'. These are not in the standard version.
The narration explicitly identifies the questioner as Jibril.
3
Compare with the first narration
Recall or refer to the first narration (not in this passage) – that version contains detailed definitions of Islam, Iman, Ihsan, and signs of the Hour. This version is much shorter.
The brevity is a key difference.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why Jibril appeared in human form and why the companions were hesitant. This teaches us reverence and the angel's role in teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر طريقاً ثانياً : وروى هذا الحديث بإسناد آخر عن أبي زرعة عن أبي هريرة ، أن رسول الله عليه السلام ، قال : ( سلوني ، فهابوا أن يسألوه فجاء رجل ، فجلس عند ركبته . وقال يا رسول الله : ما الإسلام ؟ قال : لا تشرك بالله شيئاً . وزاد في الإيمان : هذا جبريل
Then he mentioned a second chain: He narrated this hadith with another chain from Abu Zur'a, from Abu Hurairah, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Ask me.' Then they were too awestruck to ask him, so a man came and sat by his knee and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is Islam?' He said: 'Do not associate anything with Allah.' And he added concerning faith: 'This is Jibril.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the second chain (طريق) of the hadith, again from Abu Hurairah but through a different chain. In this version, the Prophet (peace be upon him) initiates by saying 'Ask me,' but the companions hesitate. Then a man (revealed as Jibril) comes and asks about Islam. The answer given here for Islam is very concise: 'Do not associate anything with Allah.' The author notes that in this narration, there is an addition concerning Iman: 'This is Jibril,' implying that the questioner was the angel Jibril teaching the people their religion. This version omits the detailed definitions of Iman and Ihsan found in the first narration, and it does not include the signs of the Hour. The brevity and the emphasis on Jibril's identity are the distinctive features of this variant.

Key Terms
سلوني
جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All narrations of the hadith of Jibril contain the same detailed content.
Correction: This second narration is much shorter and uniquely identifies Jibril as the questioner, without the detailed explanation of Iman, Ihsan, and the signs of the Hour.

Key Takeaway

The Third Narration: Ibn Umar Includes Qadr
Third narration from Ibn Umar with additions to Islam and Iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third narration from Ibn Umar expands the definitions of Islam and Iman by including additional practices and belief in divine decree.

Core Idea
Hadith versions accumulate details reflecting theological development.

This lesson examines a third chain of the famous hadith of Jibril, narrated by Ibn Umar. In this version, the definition of Islam is expanded to include fasting Ramadan and Hajj, and the definition of Iman explicitly includes belief in the divine decree (qadr) in its entirety. This addition is significant for later theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation. Notice the chain and the two additions.
The passage starts with 'ثم طريقاً ثالثاً' indicating it is a third chain.
2
Identify the additions to Islam
Compare the definition of Islam in this narration to the standard one (prayer, zakat, fasting). Here, fasting Ramadan and Hajj are mentioned. Note the phrase 'إن استطعت إليه سبيلاً' (if able).
This addition is marked as 'الزيادات في ذكر الاسلام'.
3
Identify the addition to Iman
For Iman, this version adds 'والقدر كله خيره وشره' (qadr, all of it, its good and evil) to the usual articles. This is a key theological point.
The first narration did not mention qadr.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author (Ibn Taymiyyah) presents this variation. It shows that hadith texts can differ, reflecting differing contexts or memory. The inclusion of qadr indicates its importance in later debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طريقاً ثالثاً : عن عبد الله بن عمر بن الخطاب . وفيه من الزيادات في ذكر الاسلام : قال : أن تصوم رمضان ، وتحج البيت إن استطعت إليه سبيلاً وقال : الإيمان : أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر ، والقدر كله خيره وشره . وقال في آخره : ذلك جبريل أتاكم يعلمكم دينكم
Then a third chain: From Abdullah ibn Umar ibn al-Khattab. In it are additions concerning the mention of Islam: He said: 'That you fast Ramadan and perform Hajj to the House if you are able to find a way.' And he said: 'Faith (iman) is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day, and the divine decree (qadr), all of it, its good and its evil.' And he said at the end: 'That is Jibril; he came to teach you your religion.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage offers a third chain (طريق) of the hadith, now from Abdullah ibn Umar. It contains additional details regarding both Islam and Iman. For Islam, it adds fasting Ramadan and performing Hajj (if able), which were not mentioned in the first narration's definition of Islam (which only had prayer, zakat, and fasting). For Iman, this version explicitly includes belief in the divine decree (qadr), both its good and evil, in addition to the usual articles of faith (Allah, angels, books, messengers, Last Day). The narration ends with the familiar statement that the visitor was Jibril teaching the people their religion. The addition of qadr is significant because it reflects later theological debates. The variations continue to accumulate, showing the inconsistency that the author is highlighting.

Key Terms
القدر
خيره وشره
الحج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the definition of Iman is fixed across all narrations of the hadith.
Correction: This version includes qadr, which is absent in others, showing variation.

Checkpoint
What element is added to the definition of Iman in the third narration that is not in the first?
A) Belief in the prophets
B) Belief in the divine decree (qadr)
C) Belief in the angels
D) Belief in the resurrection

Key Takeaway
The third narration from Ibn Umar adds fasting and Hajj to Islam and explicitly includes qadr in Iman, showing that hadith versions vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that every hardship you face, including the most painful ones, is from Allah's decree? How does your reaction to trials reveal the strength of your belief in qadr? Will you commit to reciting 'Qadr all of it, its good and its evil' when faced with difficulty?
?
This hadith adds fasting and Hajj to the definition of Islam. Are you fulfilling these pillars with the awareness that they are part of what Jibril came to teach? If not, what is holding you back from completing your Islam?
?
The narration states that Jibril came to teach 'your religion'. Consider: have you been treating Islam and Iman as merely intellectual knowledge, or as a lived reality that demands your entire being? What one change can you make today to embody what you have learned from this hadith?

The Fourth Narration: Ibn Umar and the Visitor
Author presents the fourth narration with added details and qadr context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this version of the hadith provides additional historical context about qadr and a vivid description of the mysterious visitor.

Core Idea
Every detail in a hadith narration carries meaning and context.

This lesson presents a fourth chain for the famous hadith of Jibril, with a longer introduction that provides historical context about qadr and a vivid description of the visitor. Understanding these details helps us appreciate the richness of the hadith and the care with which narrators preserved variations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the chain (Yahya ibn Ya'mar) and the historical reference to Ma'bad al-Juhani and qadr.
Pay attention to the extra details not found in the first narration.
2
Examine the description of the visitor
Note the vivid description: 'very black of hair, very white of clothes', no travel signs, unknown. Compare with other versions of the hadith.
This description emphasizes the mysterious nature of the visitor (Jibril).
3
Reflect on the significance of this variation
Ask yourself why the author includes this version. How does the mention of qadr and the detailed description add to the hadith's meaning?
This version ties directly into the theological discussion of divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر طريقاً رابعاً : بإسناده عن يحيى بن يعمر ، قال : كان أول من تكلم في القدر : معبد الجهني بالبصرة. فانطلقت أنا وحميد بن عبد الرحمن الحميري حاجا ، فلما قدمنا المدينة إذا نحن بعبد الله بن عمر . وذكر الحديث إلى : حدثني عمر بن الخطاب قال : كنا عند رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فجاءه شاب شدید سواد الشعر ، شديد بياض الثياب . لا نرى عليه أثر السفر ، ولا يعرفه منا أحد . فجلس إلى رسول الله ، فألصق ركبتيه ووضع كفيه على فخذيه . ثم قال : يا محمد . ما الإيمان ؟ فذكر الحديث مع هذه الزيادات
Then he mentioned a fourth chain: With his chain from Yahya ibn Ya'mar, who said: The first person to speak about qadr (divine decree) was Ma'bad al-Juhani in Basra. So I and Humayd ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Himyari set out for Hajj. When we arrived in Medina, we came upon Abdullah ibn Umar. And he mentioned the hadith until: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab narrated to me, saying: We were with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when a young man came to him, very black of hair, very white of clothes; we did not see any sign of travel on him, and none of us knew him. He sat down by the Messenger of Allah, pressed his knees against his knees, and placed his palms on his thighs. Then he said: O Muhammad, what is faith (iman)?' So he mentioned the hadith with these additions.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth chain for the hadith, now through Yahya ibn Ya'mar, with a longer introduction. It provides historical context by mentioning that Ma'bad al-Juhani was the first to speak about qadr (divine decree) in Basra—this is relevant because the hadith itself includes qadr in some versions. The narrator then recounts going to Medina with a companion and meeting Abdullah ibn Umar, who relates the hadith from his father Umar ibn al-Khattab. In this version, the visitor is described in more detail: a young man with very black hair and very white clothes, with no signs of travel, and unknown to the companions. He sits intimately before the Prophet and asks about Iman. The author notes that the hadith was mentioned 'with these additions' (مع هذه الزيادات), implying that the narration contained extra elements not present in the first version. This version emphasizes the mysterious nature of the visitor and ties into the issue of qadr.

Key Terms
القدر
معبد الجهني
يحيى بن يعمر
شاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the story of the visitor is always told the same way, with no significant variations.
Correction: This version includes specific details about the visitor's appearance and the context of qadr, showing that narrators preserved different aspects for different purposes.

Checkpoint

B) Ma'bad al-Juhani
C) Humayd ibn Abd al-Rahman

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The visitor was unknown to the companions, yet he sat intimately with the Prophet and asked the most important questions. Are you willing to approach your own learning with that same humility and directness, even if you feel like an outsider? What question about your faith are you afraid to ask?

This narration emphasizes that not all versions of the hadith are identical—details matter. How carefully do you examine the details of the teachings you follow? Are there any subtle deviations in your understanding that you need to correct today?

Fifth Narration: Extensive Additions on Islam and Iman
Author presents the fifth narration with extensive additions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth narration expands the definitions of Islam and Iman with additional practices and beliefs, and why these additions are significant for the author's argument.

Core Idea
This narration adds Umrah and ghusl to Islam, and belief in Paradise, Hell, Scale, and Qadr to Iman.

This passage presents the fifth and most extensive chain of the hadith of Jibril. It includes elements absent in earlier narrations, such as Umrah, ghusl, and a longer list of beliefs. Understanding these additions helps see why the author considers the hadith confused due to many variations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the content. Note the question-and-answer format.
The passage has two main parts: the definition of Islam and the definition of Iman.
2
Identify the additions to Islam
Compare the list of Islamic practices here with those in earlier narrations (from previous lessons). Focus on the new elements: Umrah, ghusl from janaba, and completing wudu.
These additions are only found in this narration.
3
Identify the additions to Iman
Note the expanded list of beliefs: belief in Paradise, Hellfire, the Scale, resurrection, and divine decree (qadr). These were not in the earlier narrations.
The inclusion of qadr (both good and evil) is particularly significant.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Consider why the author presents this narration. He says he has sufficed with al-Jawzaqi's version, but there are even more differences in other books. This shows the hadith is not a fixed text.
This supports the author's initial claim that the hadith is confused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر طريقاً خامساً : وفيه من الزيادات : أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله ، وأن محمداً رسول الله ، وأن تقيم الصلاة ، وتؤتى الزكاة ، وتحج البيت وتعتمر وتغتسل من الجنابة ، وتتم الوضوء ، وتصوم رمضان . قال : فإن فعلت هذا فأنا مسلم ؟ قال : نعم . وقال : الإيمان : أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله وتؤمن بالجنة والنار والميزان وتؤمن بالبعث بعد الموت . وتؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره . قال : فإذا فعلت ، فأنا مؤمن ؟ قال : نعم . وقال : هل تدرون من هذا ؟ هذا جبريل أتاكم يعلمكم دينكم. فخذوا عنه . والذي نفسي بيده : ما شبه علي منذ أتاني قبل مرتي هذه ، وما عرفته حتى ولى وأقول : هذه طرق خمسة ذكرها الشيخ أبو بكر الجوزقي . ورأيت في سائر الكتب اختلافات كثيرة ، أزيد من هذا . إلا أني اكتفيت بما ذكره هذا
Then he mentioned a fifth chain: In it are the additions: That you bear witness that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, and that you establish prayer, give zakat, perform Hajj to the House and Umrah, perform ghusl from ritual impurity, complete the wudu, and fast Ramadan. He said: 'If you do this, am I a Muslim?' He said: 'Yes.' And he said: 'Faith (iman) is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and believe in Paradise, Hellfire, the Scale (mizan), and believe in resurrection after death, and believe in the divine decree (qadr), its good and its evil.' He said: 'If I do this, am I a believer (mu'min)?' He said: 'Yes.' And he said: 'Do you know who this is? This is Jibril; he came to teach you your religion. Take from him. By the One in Whose hand is my soul, he never appeared to me in this form before this occasion, and I did not recognize him until he left.' And I say: These are five chains that Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Jawzaqi mentioned. And I have seen in other books many differences, more than this. But I have sufficed with what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and most extensive chain of the hadith, containing numerous additions. For Islam, it now includes not only the shahada, prayer, zakat, fasting, and Hajj, but also Umrah, ghusl from janaba, and completing wudu. The dialogue is framed as a question-and-answer where the visitor asks if performing these actions makes him a Muslim, and the Prophet affirms. For Iman, the definition is expanded to include belief in Paradise, Hellfire, the Scale (mizan), resurrection after death, and divine decree (both good and evil). Again, the visitor asks if this makes him a believer, and the Prophet affirms. At the end, the Prophet identifies the visitor as Jibril and states that he only appeared in that form once before. The author then comments that these are the five chains al-Jawzaqi mentioned, but he has seen even more differences in other books. He concludes that he has only sufficed with what al-Jawzaqi recorded. This final narration underscores the extreme variability of the hadith, supporting the author's initial argument that the hadith is confused due to many additions and omissions.

Key Terms
العمرة
الغسل
الوضوء
الميزان
الجنة
النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of Jibril has a fixed, agreed-upon text.
Correction: The fifth narration shows significant variation, including Umrah, ghusl, and a longer list of beliefs, indicating the hadith is not uniformly transmitted.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is included as part of Islam in the fifth narration but NOT in the first?
A) Fasting Ramadan
B) Performing Umrah
C) Giving zakat
D) Believing in Allah

Key Takeaway
The fifth narration demonstrates that the hadith of Jibril is not a fixed text but varies significantly across chains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The hadith of Jibril is often cited as a summary of Islam, yet this version includes practices like Umrah and ghusl that many Muslims today treat as optional. Are there any sunnah or recommended acts you have been neglecting because you thought they were not essential? Will you commit to learning and practicing them from today?
?
The Prophet did not recognize Jibril until he left. How often do you fail to recognize the teachings of your religion when they come through unfamiliar sources or formats? What will you do to ensure you are learning the deen from its authentic sources, even when it challenges your expectations?
?
If the very narration that defines Iman can vary in detail, how does that affect your certainty about the fundamentals of your faith? Does it make you question the reliability of hadith, or does it deepen your trust in the scholars who preserved these variations? What concrete step will you take to strengthen your connection to the transmitted knowledge of Islam?

Missing Context in Hadith: Two Illustrative Examples
Two examples and a conclusion on how missing premises change hadith meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how missing contextual premises can drastically change the meaning of a hadith, as illustrated by two examples.

Core Idea
A hadith without its context is like a puzzle missing its key piece.

Hadith transmission often loses the situational context that clarifies the intended meaning. This lesson examines two examples—one about the signs of death and another about a specific child of adultery—to show how a missing premise can completely reverse the apparent message. Through these, we learn the critical importance of preserving the full background of prophetic statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noticing how the two examples are presented separately, followed by a conclusion.
2
Understand the first example
Identify the missing premise about the angel vs. devil before death. How does knowing this context change the meaning of 'loving meeting Allah'?
3
Understand the second example
Analyze the hadith about the child of adultery. Why did Ibn Umar correct the apparent meaning? What was the missing premise?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the concluding principle: speech often depends on a premise. Ask yourself how this applies to other narrations you know.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أراد بعبد خيراً قيض له ملكاً قبل موته ، فسلده حتى يموت على الخير . فإذا رأى عند القرب من الموت مما يتنزل عليه من الزحمة والبركة ، أحب لقاء الله ، وأحب الله لقاءه ، وإذا أراد بعبد شراً ، قيض له شيطاناً قبل موته ، فأغواه . فإذا حضره الموت رأى المكروه. فعند ذلك يكره لقاء الله ، ويكره الله لقاءه ، • المثال الرابع : روي أنه عليه السلام قال : « ولد الزنا شر الثلاثة ، فقال ابن عمر : بل ولد الزنا خير الثلاثة : والنبي عليه السلام إنما قال ذلك في ولد زنا بعينه • فثبت : أن الكلام قد يكون مسبوقاً بمقدمة ، ويتغير المعنى بسبب تغير تلك المقدمة . فإذا لم يسمعها الراوي ، تغير المقصود جداً
When Allah intends good for a servant, He appoints an angel before his death, who guides him so that he dies upon goodness. So when he sees near death what descends upon him of mercy and blessing, he loves meeting Allah, and Allah loves meeting him. And when Allah intends evil for a servant, He appoints a devil before his death, who leads him astray. So when death comes to him he sees disliked things, and then he hates meeting Allah, and Allah hates meeting him. • The fourth example: It is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The child of adultery is the worst of the three.' Ibn Umar said: 'Rather the child of adultery is the best of the three.' The Prophet (peace be upon him) only said that about a specific child of adultery. • So it is established that speech may be preceded by a premise, and the meaning changes due to change of that premise. So if the narrator does not hear it, the intended meaning changes greatly.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents two examples to illustrate how missing context can distort the meaning of a hadith. The first example, about death, shows that a believer's love for meeting Allah depends on seeing signs of mercy, while a disbeliever's hate depends on seeing disliked things; without this context, the statements could be misunderstood. The second example, the fourth of its kind, involves the hadith 'The child of adultery is the worst of the three.' Ibn Umar corrected the misunderstanding that it was a general statement, revealing that the Prophet said it about a specific child. The author concludes with the principle: speech often relies on a premise; if the narrator omits it, the intended meaning is drastically altered.

Key Terms
مقدمة
راوي
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a hadith can be transmitted out of context and still convey the same meaning.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that missing a single premise can reverse the meaning, as in the child of adultery example. Context is essential.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the two examples in this passage?
A) To show that narrators always lie
B) To demonstrate that missing context can change the meaning of a hadith
C) To prove that Ibn Umar contradicted the Prophet
D) To explain the signs of death

Key Takeaway
A hadith’s meaning can be completely reversed if its contextual premise is lost in transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held an opinion about a hadith or Islamic ruling based on a partial understanding, only to later realize you were missing context? How will you guard against that from now on?
?
The first example shows that Allah sends angels or devils based on His will. Does this truth make you more humble about your own ability to choose good or evil? What will you change in your daily life because of this awareness?
?
The second example reveals that even a prophetic statement can be misapplied if its premise is unknown. Are there any beliefs or practices you follow that might be based on incomplete knowledge? What step will you take this week to verify them?

First Transmitted Proof for Predestination: Hadith of Adam and Moses and Two Objections
Author presents the hadith as first proof and raises two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first transmitted proof for predestination (the hadith of Adam and Moses) and the two objections raised against its authenticity.

Core Idea
The hadith of Adam and Moses supports predestination, but its transmission variants raise doubts.

This lesson introduces the first transmitted proof for the belief in predestination (qadar). The author cites a well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim about a debate between Adam and Moses. However, he also presents two objections concerning the hadith's authenticity, focusing on discrepancies in narration and the impropriety of Moses' speech. We will explore both the proof and the critiques.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith of Adam and Moses
Read the Arabic hadith passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to understand the argument between Adam and Moses.
Note that the hadith is cited from the two Sahihs.
2
Observe the variations
Notice the author's comment that the hadith has many paths with additions and omissions. This is key to the first objection.
The author does not state his own view yet.
3
Study the first objection
The first objection claims that multiple variants indicate weakness, since a single event should have consistent narration.
This is a common argument in hadith criticism.
4
Study the second objection
The second objection uses a version where Moses speaks harshly to Adam. This contradicts the Quranic command to honor parents.
The author quotes Quran 17:23-24 to support this.

Chapter Two: Establishing the transmitted proofs for the correctness of the belief in predestination (al-qada' wa al-qadr). The first proof: What the two Shaykhs narrated in the two Sahihs with their chains from Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna, from 'Amr ibn Dinar, from Tawus, that he heard Abu Hurayra say: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Adam and Moses argued...' He said: 'O Adam, you are our father, and you caused us to be expelled from Paradise.' Adam said: 'O Moses, Allah chose you with His speech and wrote the Torah for you with His hand. Do you blame me for a matter that Allah decreed for me forty years before He created me?' So Adam prevailed over Moses. This report is narrated through various paths, and in them there are additions and omissions. If it is said: This hadith is weak. Its explanation is from several aspects: First: This hadith was narrated through various paths with additions and omissions, while the event is one. That indicates weakness. Second: The author of Sharh al-Sunna narrated this report through another chain from Hammam ibn Munabbih, who said: This is what Abu Hurayra narrated to us. He said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Adam and Moses argued...' Moses said to him: 'You are Adam who led people astray and caused them to be expelled from Paradise to the earth' to the end of the hadith. So how about when the father is like Adam, in that he...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage begins Chapter Two of the work, which aims to establish transmitted proofs (adilla ikhbariyya) for the doctrine of predestination (al-qada' wa al-qadr). The author presents the first proof: the well-known hadith of the argument between Adam and Moses. The hadith is cited from the Sahihs of Bukhari and Muslim. In it, Moses blames Adam for expelling humanity from Paradise, and Adam responds that it was a matter decreed by Allah before his creation. The author notes that this hadith has been transmitted through various paths with additions and omissions. Then he presents two objections that have been raised against the hadith. The first objection is that the existence of multiple variants with additions and omissions indicates weakness, because the same event should have consistent narration. The second objection is based on the version reported by the author of Sharh al-Sunna, where Moses harshly addresses Adam. This is problematic because Islamic teachings command respect for parents, especially for a prophet like Adam. The author quotes Quranic verses (17:23) to argue that harsh speech to a parent is forbidden, so Moses would not say such a thing. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that the discussion continues.

Key Takeaway
The first transmitted proof for predestination is the hadith of Adam and Moses, but its authenticity is challenged by two objections: varying narratives and inappropriate speech.

Defending the Embryonic Stages Hadith: Synecdoche in Prophetic Speech
Objection and answer on the embryonic stages hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the embryonic stages hadith contradicts medical evidence is answered by identifying it as a well-known metaphorical expression (synecdoche).

Core Idea
The hadith uses metaphor, not literal error.

This lesson presents a theological objection and its answer regarding a hadith describing the stages of embryonic development. The objector claims medical evidence disproves the hadith’s timeline, thereby implying an error in prophethood. The answer refutes this by explaining the hadith’s use of a common rhetorical device, showing that the objection stems from a literalist misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objection and the answer.
2
Understand the objection
What does the objector claim? Why does he think the hadith is erroneous? Note the phrase 'غير موافقة للمحسوس'.
3
Understand the answer
The answer says 'إطلاق اسم الكل على الأغلب: مجاز مشهور'. What does this mean? How does it resolve the contradiction?
4
Reflect on the defense
Consider why this figurative interpretation is valid in prophetic speech. How does this defend the integrity of the sunna?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوماً، ثم تبقى علقة أربعين يوماً، ثم تبقى مضغة أربعين يوماً . والمشاهدات الطبية دلت على أن الأمر ليس كذلك . فإنه في مدة أربعين يوماً يتكون الولد بتمامه ، ومشاهدة السقط يدل على هذه الأحوال . وإذا كان هذا الخبر مشتملاً على الخطأ ، وجب تنزيه منصب النبوة عنه . قوله : « إن مراتب النطفة والعلقة والمضغة المذكورة في النص ، غير موافقة للمحسوس ، فكان الخبر غلطاً ، قلنا : إطلاق اسم الكل على الأغلب : مجاز مشهور .
Day, then it remains a clot for forty days, then it remains a chewed lump for forty days. Medical observations indicated that the matter is not like that. For in a period of forty days the child is fully formed, and observation of miscarriage indicates these states. And if this report contains error, then the office of prophethood must be declared free of it. His statement: 'Indeed the stages of the sperm-drop, clot, and chewed lump mentioned in the text do not agree with the perceptible, so the report is mistaken.' We say: Applying the name of the whole to the majority is a well-known metaphor.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding a hadith that describes the embryonic stages over three forty-day periods. The objector argues that medical observation shows the child is fully formed within forty days, not in three separate stages, and that miscarriages confirm this. Therefore, the hadith must be erroneous, and prophethood must be absolved of such error. The answer responds by explaining that the hadith is employing a common rhetorical device: applying the name of the whole period to the majority. In other words, the description is not meant to be literal down to the exact day but is a synecdoche (إطلاق اسم الكل على الأغلب), which is a well-known metaphor (مجاز مشهور). Thus, the objection based on literal interpretation is invalid.

Key Terms
مجاز مشهور
إطلاق اسم الكل على الأغلب
مراتب النطفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes the hadith must be literally accurate in every detail.
Correction: The answer shows the hadith uses a common figurative expression (synecdoche), so literal precision is not required.

Checkpoint
How does the answer defend the hadith about the embryonic stages?
A) By denying the medical observations.
B) By arguing the hadith is a solitary report not binding in matters of science.
C) By explaining that the hadith uses a well-known metaphor where the whole is applied to the majority.
D) By claiming the hadith is only for the Arabs and not universal.

Key Takeaway
The hadith on embryonic stages uses a well-known metaphor (synecdoche), not literal precision, so it does not conflict with medical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Companions' Dispute on Qadar (Incomplete)
A hadith on the disagreement between Abu Bakr and Umar about divine decree, cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the content of a hadith about the disagreement between Abu Bakr and Umar on whether Allah decrees both good and evil, and why this hadith is presented as a proof in the discussion of qadar, even though it is incomplete.

Core Idea
The Prophet's reaction to Abu Bakr and Umar's dispute hints at the correct position on qadar.

This lesson presents a hadith that is used as a proof in the theological debate on divine decree (qadar). The story recounts a disagreement between Abu Bakr and Umar about whether Allah decrees both good and evil. Although the hadith is incomplete in the source, it offers a glimpse into the early Islamic discourse on this topic and how the Companions sought guidance from the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the disagreement between Abu Bakr and Umar. Note that the text cuts off before Umar finishes his answer.
The passage ends at the word 'الحسنات...'.
2
Identify the positions
Identify Abu Bakr's position (Allah decrees only good) and Umar's position (Allah decrees both). The Prophet's turning away from Abu Bakr may indicate disapproval, but the full context is missing.
3
Reflect on incompleteness
Consider why the author included this incomplete hadith. How might a scholar use this to support the Ash'ari position? Note that we cannot draw a definitive conclusion from the fragment.
This hadith is often cited in debates on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية والعشرون: ما روى عمرو بن شعیب، عن أبيه عن جده قال: كنت جالساً عند رسول الله، إذ أقبل أبو بكر وعمر، ومعهما قوم من الناس، قد ارتفعت أصواتهم. فكف رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الحديث وتسمع فلما دنوا، جاء أبو بكر فسلم على رسول الله وجلس إلى جنبه، ثم جاء عمر وسلم وجلس بعيداً منه. فقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: ما هذا الذي ارتفعت أصواتكم فيه؟ قال بعض القوم: يا رسول الله تكلم أبو بكر وعمر، واختلفا فاختلفنا باختلافهما. قال: وما ذاك؟ قالوا: في القدر. قال أبو بكر: يقدر الله الخير ولا يقدر الشر. وقال عمر: بل يقدرهما. فتبع بعض القوم أبا بكر، وبعضهم عمر. فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: وكيف قلت يا أبا بكر؟ قال: قلت الحسنات من الله، والسيئات من أنفسنا. فأعرض عنه. ثم أقبل على عمر، وقال: كيف قلت يا عمر؟ قال: قلت: الحسنات...
The twenty-second proof: What 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb narrated from his father from his grandfather: He said: I was sitting with the Messenger of Allah when Abu Bakr and 'Umar approached, and with them a group of people, their voices raised. The Messenger of Allah stopped talking and listened. When they drew near, Abu Bakr came and greeted the Messenger of Allah and sat beside him. Then 'Umar came and greeted and sat away from him. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'What is this that you have raised your voices about?' Some of the group said: 'O Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr and 'Umar spoke and disagreed, so we disagreed because of their disagreement.' He said: 'What is that?' They said: 'About qadar (predestination). Abu Bakr said: Allah decrees good but does not decree evil. 'Umar said: Rather, He decrees them both.' So some people followed Abu Bakr and some followed 'Umar. The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'How did you say, O Abu Bakr?' He said: 'I said good deeds are from Allah, and evil deeds are from ourselves.' So he turned away from him. Then he turned to 'Umar and said: 'How did you say, O 'Umar?' He said: 'I said good deeds...'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-second proof, a hadith about a disagreement between Abu Bakr and Umar on the scope of divine decree. The hadith begins with a scene where the Companions are arguing about qadar. Abu Bakr holds that Allah decrees only good, while Umar holds that He decrees both good and evil. The Prophet questions both. Abu Bakr responds that good comes from Allah, evil from ourselves. The Prophet turns away from him. Then he asks Umar, but the text cuts off before Umar's full answer. The incomplete nature prevents us from seeing the Prophet's final judgment. However, the author likely used this hadith to support the Ash'ari position that Allah decrees both, as the Prophet's turning away from Abu Bakr might indicate disapproval. But the lesson is incomplete.

Key Terms
القدر (al-qadar)
الحسنات (al-ḥasanāt)
السيئات (al-sayyi'āt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Abu Bakr's view is correct because he was a senior Companion.
Correction: The Prophet's turning away from Abu Bakr after his statement suggests disapproval, but the incomplete text does not provide a definitive correction.

Checkpoint

A) Allah decrees both good and evil
B) Allah decrees only good
C) Allah decrees nothing
D) People have complete free will

Key Takeaway
This hadith shows the early Muslim community grappling with the question of divine decree, though the incomplete text leaves the final answer unclear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious text that is unclear or incomplete, does your soul honestly remain patient in uncertainty, or does it rush to fill the gaps with comforting opinions? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?

?
The Prophet turned away from Abu Bakr’s answer. If the Prophet turned away from something you strongly believed, would you have the courage to let go of your position and seek the truth from him alone, even if it meant abandoning what your heart had settled on? Make that question specific to one belief you hold today.

The Sixth Proof: Command to Acquire Knowledge Implies Impossibility
The author presents the sixth argument from the command to acquire knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah' serves as a proof that obligation cannot exceed human capacity.

Core Idea
A divine command to do the impossible is self-contradictory.

In this brief but powerful argument, the author cites the Quran's command to know Allah as evidence that acquiring knowledge is beyond human capacity. Since Allah commands knowledge, but knowledge is not within our power, the command becomes a proof that obligations can only be for things within our capacity. This argument builds on earlier proofs about human inability.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic text carefully. Note the two parts: the command and the claim about capacity.
2
Identify the Quranic verse
The author quotes 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (Surah 47:19). This is the command.
3
Understand the claim about capacity
The author says acquisition of knowledge is not within capacity (ليس في الوسع). This is a previously established conclusion.
4
Connect to the overall argument
If the command is to do what is impossible, then the command would be meaningless. Thus, obligations must be within capacity.
This refutes opponents who say obligation can be beyond capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن الأمر بتحصيل العلم وارد . كقوله تعالى : ( فاعلم : أنه لا إله إلا الله )) وقد دللنا على أن تحصيل العلم : ليس في الوسع
The sixth: The command to acquire knowledge is present, like His statement, exalted: 'Know that there is no god but Allah.' And we have demonstrated that the acquisition of knowledge is not within capacity.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This is a very short argument. The author cites the Quranic command 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (Surah Muhammad, 19) as evidence that Allah commands the acquisition of knowledge (تحصيل العلم). However, the author claims to have already proven that acquiring knowledge is not within human capacity (ليس في الوسع). Thus, the command would be a command to do the impossible, which is absurd. The argument is presented as a proof against the opponent's view that obligation can be to something not within capacity. It relies on a previously established point that knowledge is given by God, not acquired by human effort.

Key Terms
تحصيل العلم
أمر
وسع
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is denying the Quranic command or saying it is invalid.
Correction: The author accepts the command as valid but uses it to demonstrate that since the commanded act (acquiring knowledge) is impossible, the obligation cannot be for something beyond capacity.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the acquisition of knowledge?
A) It is possible but difficult.
B) It is not within human capacity.
C) It is commanded but optional.
D) It is the same as faith.

Key Takeaway
The divine command to know Allah, combined with human inability to acquire knowledge, proves that obligation cannot require the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada or affirm 'there is no god but Allah,' do you ever consider that this knowledge itself is a gift from Allah, not something you achieved? How does that realization affect your humility and gratitude?
?
Think of a religious obligation you find difficult. If Allah's commands are not contingent on your ability, how does that change your approach to that obligation? Do you rely more on your own strength or on Allah's grace?
?
The author claims that knowledge of Allah is not acquired by effort. Does that make you feel less responsible for seeking knowledge, or more dependent on Allah? How can you actively seek knowledge while acknowledging that it is ultimately a gift?
