Two Interpretations of 'Ihdina' Supporting Ash'ari Doctrine
The author gives two meanings of 'ihdina' that support the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretations of the Quranic phrase 'ihdina' that support the Ash'ari doctrine of divine causation and refute the objection of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
The prayer for guidance is never redundant because true guidance is God's continuous creation.

The author addresses a potential objection: if guidance is already given, why pray for it? He then defines guidance as God's active indication leading to knowledge, and provides two scripturally grounded interpretations of 'ihdina' from the Fatiha. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the logical flow: objection, response, two interpretations.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the objection that praying for guidance would be requesting what is already attained (tahsil al-hasil). See how the author counters by redefining guidance as an indication that leads to knowledge through God's creation.
The key term 'dalala' (indication) is crucial.
3
Understand the two interpretations
The author offers two explanations from exegetes: (1) 'establish us upon this guidance' and (2) 'guide us to the knowledge of God's holiness, majesty, and greatness,' which is infinite.
Each interpretation avoids the redundancy problem.
4
Connect to Ash'ari doctrine
Reflect on how both interpretations affirm that all true guidance is from Allah and involves His continuous creative act, supporting the Ash'ari position.
The verse about 'those upon whom You have bestowed favor' reinforces this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد فعل ذلك . فلو حملنا هذا الدعاء عليه ، كان هذا طلباً لتحصيل الحاصل . وهو باطل . بل الهدى عبارة عن الدلالة بشرط أن تكون تلك الدلالة مفضية إلى المقصود ، وذلك إنما يكون بخلق العلم والمعرفة . ولا يقال : أن لا يكون الإيمان حاصلا لهم في الحال . لأنا نقول : هذا غير . فهذا يقتضي لازم . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : قال بعض المفسرين : المراد بقوله ( اهدنا ) : أي ثبتنا على هذه الهداية الحاصلة في الحال. وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى : ( يثبت الله الذين آمنوا بالقول الثابت في الحياة الدنيا ) والثاني : أن يكون المراد : اهدنا إلى معرفة قدسك وجلالك وكبريائك . فإنه لا نهاية لهذه المعرفة . الثاني : إنه قال : ( صراط الذين أنعمت عليهم ) بالهداية والمعرفة . وذلك يدل أيضاً على مذهبنا والله أعلم
He did that. So if we carry this supplication upon it, then this is a request for the attainment of what is already attained, which is invalid. Rather, guidance (al-huda) is expression of indication (al-dalala) on condition that that indication leads to the intended purpose, and that only occurs by the creation of knowledge and cognition. And it cannot be said that faith is not present for them at the moment. For we say: this is not [the case]. So this necessitates a necessary consequence. Its clarification is from two aspects: The first: Some exegetes said: The meaning of His saying (Ihdina) is: establish us upon this guidance that is present at the moment. And to this points His saying: 'Allah establishes those who believe with the firm word in the life of this world.' And the second: that the meaning is: guide us to the knowledge of Your holiness, majesty, and greatness, for there is no end to this knowledge. The second: He said: 'The path of those upon whom You have bestowed favor' with guidance and knowledge. And that also indicates our school. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and a presentation of two interpretations of the Quranic phrase 'ihdina' (guide us) that support the Ash'ari doctrine of divine causation. The author begins by addressing a hypothetical objection: if guidance (al-huda) is already attained, then praying for it would be asking for what is already present (tahsil al-hasil), which is futile. He counters this by defining 'guidance' as an indication (dalala) that leads to the intended purpose, and this only occurs through God's creation of knowledge and cognition. Thus, the prayer is not for what is already possessed in its entirety, but for the effective means. He then notes that this necessitates a further clarification (lazim), which he provides through two explanations from exegetes. First: 'ihdina' means 'establish us' (thabbitna) upon the guidance already given, as supported by the verse about God establishing believers. Second: it means 'guide us to the knowledge of God's holiness, majesty, and greatness', which is infinite and therefore always a legitimate request. He concludes by linking the phrase 'the path of those upon whom You have bestowed favor' to this same theme of guidance and knowledge, confirming the Ash'ari position that all guidance is from God alone.

Key Terms
الهدى
تحصيل الحاصل
الثبات
الدلالة
خلق العلم والمعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praying for guidance is redundant if one is already guided.
Correction: Guidance is not a static state but a continuous divine act of creating knowledge and steadfastness, so the prayer is for ongoing divine support.

Checkpoint
What two interpretations does the author give for the word 'Ihdina' in the Fatiha?
A) Guide us to paradise and protect us from hellfire.
B) Establish us on guidance and guide us to infinite knowledge of God.
C) Show us the way and keep us from error.
D) Grant us faith and increase our deeds.

Key Takeaway
The prayer 'ihdina' is both a request for steadfastness and for deeper knowledge, affirming that all true guidance is from Allah and involves His continuous creative act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Ihdina al-sirat al-mustaqim' in every prayer, do you realize you are asking Allah to create knowledge and cognition in your heart right now? How does that change the urgency and presence of your supplication?
?
Which interpretation of 'ihdina'—establishing you on existing guidance or guiding you to infinite knowledge of His majesty—strikes you as more needed in your life at this moment? Why?
?
If true guidance is a continuous act of Allah's creation, where in your daily choices are you relying on your own effort instead of begging Him to create the knowledge and steadfastness you lack? Will you change that today?

Objection and Answer: Is Debating with Allah Possible?
Objection and answer on debating with Allah, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians use Quranic verses to argue that debating with Allah is rationally possible despite divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The Quran itself shows that Allah debates with His servants.

The objection that debating with Allah is impossible because He is transcendent seems logical at first glance. However, the Quran contains numerous instances where Allah engages in argument with humans. In this passage, the author presents an objection and a two-part answer, grounding the possibility of debate in the very words of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand why someone might think debating with Allah is impossible, based on divine transcendence and the verse 'He is not questioned about what He does.'
The objector assumes that dialogue requires equality.
2
Examine the first answer
The author argues that if debate were impossible, Allah's own questions would be meaningless — a reductio ad absurdum that would disparage Allah's word.
This is a logical refutation, not a direct proof.
3
Study the six Quranic examples
The second answer provides six verses that show Allah debating with humans, explicitly or implicitly. Notice how each verse removes human excuses or depicts disputation.
The verses cover sending messengers, the Day of Judgment, and direct questioning.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This passage shows that kalam arguments often use the Quran itself to justify theological principles, not just rational proofs.
The Quran is the primary source for establishing the possibility of debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: إن السؤال والجواب إنما يتم مع الأكفاء والأمثال، والله منزه عن ذلك. فكيف تمكن المناظرة مع الله؟ ولهذا قال تعالى: (لا يسأل عما يفعل). فالجواب عنه من وجهين: الأول: إن المناظرة مع الله، إذا كانت ممتنعة كان إيراد هذا السؤال من الله يجب أن يكون عبثاً، وذلك يقتضي القدح في كلمة الله تعالى. الثاني: إن القرآن قد دل على أن هذه المناظرة غير ممتنعة. ويدل عليه آيات: أحدها: قوله تعالى: (يا أهل الكتاب قد جاءكم رسولنا يبين لكم على فترة من الرسل أن تقولوا: ما جاءنا من بشير ولا نذير. فقد جاءكم بشير ونذير) وثانيها: قوله تعالى: (رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين. لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل) وثالثها: قوله تعالى: (ولو أنا أهلكناهم بعذاب من قبله، لقالوا: ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولاً، فتتبع آياتك من قبل أن نذل ونخزى) ورابعها: قوله تعالى: (أو لم نعمركم ما يتذكر فيه من تذكر، وجاءكم النذير) ومعلوم أن المراد منه: إنا أمهلناكم وأزلنا عذركم حتى لا يبقى لكم عذر ولا حجة علينا. وخامسها: قوله تعالى: (يوم تأتى كل نفس تجادل عن نفسها) ومعلوم أن تلك المجادلة إنما تكون مع من يطالبها ويجانسها. وما ذاك إلا الله تعالى. فيلزم أن تكون مجادلة العبد مع الله. وسادسها: قوله تعالى حكاية عن الكفار: (رب لم حشرتني أعمى. وقد كنت بصيراً)؟ ثم لم يقل في
If someone says: Question and answer only occur between peers and equals, and Allah is transcendent above that. So how is debate with Allah possible? That is why He said: (He is not questioned about what He does). The answer to it is from two aspects: First: If debate with Allah were impossible, then the raising of this question by Allah would have to be futile, and that would imply disparagement of the word of Allah the Exalted. Second: The Quran has indicated that this debate is not impossible. Verses indicate it: One: His saying: (O People of the Scripture, there has come to you Our Messenger making clear to you, after a period of messengers, lest you say: There came not to us any bringer of good tidings or warner. But there has come to you a bringer of good tidings and a warner). Second: His saying: (Messengers as bringers of good tidings and warners, so that mankind will have no argument against Allah after the messengers). Third: His saying: (And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before him, they would have said: Our Lord, why did You not send to us a messenger so we could have followed Your verses before we were humiliated and disgraced). Fourth: His saying: (Have We not given you life long enough for whoever would remember therein to remember, and the warner had come to you). It is known that the intended meaning is: We gave you respite and removed your excuse so that you have no excuse and no argument against us. Fifth: His saying: (The Day every soul will come disputing for itself). It is known that that disputation is only with one who calls it to account and is of its kind. And that is none other than Allah the Exalted. So it follows that the servant's disputation is with Allah. Sixth: His saying, relating from the disbelievers: (My Lord, why have You raised me blind while I was sighted?) Then He did not say in...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the possibility of debating with Allah. The objector argues that questioning and answering only take place between equals, and since Allah is transcendent and incomparable, debating with Him is impossible. He cites the verse 'He is not questioned about what He does' as support. The answer is given in two parts. The first argues that if such debate were impossible, then Allah's own questioning in the Quran (e.g., 'Why did you do that?') would be futile, which would impugn His words. The second part provides six Quranic verses that show debate is possible. The first four verses establish that Allah sends messengers and gives respite to remove human excuses, implying that humans will argue with Him. The fifth verse speaks of the Day of Judgment when every soul will argue for itself, and since there is no one else on that day, the argument must be with Allah. The sixth verse quotes disbelievers asking why they were raised blind. The passage cuts off before completing the sixth example, but the argument is clear: the Quran itself contains instances of debate between Allah and His servants, so it is not impossible.

Key Terms
المناظرة
الجدال
الحجة
السؤال والجواب
تنزيه الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine transcendence makes any form of debate with Allah impossible.
Correction: The Quran itself contains instances of Allah questioning and being questioned by humans, showing that debate is possible without compromising transcendence.

Checkpoint

A) Citing verses that show Allah questions people
B) Arguing that if it were impossible, Allah's own questions would be futile
C) Saying that angels mediate the debate
D) Claiming that Allah temporarily lowers His status

Key Takeaway
Debating with Allah is not impossible; the Quran itself contains numerous examples of Allah engaging in argument with His servants.

Refuting the Objection: 'From Allah' Means Speech and Command
Three refutations of the objection regarding 'from Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments used to refute the claim that 'from Allah' in the Qur'anic verse refers only to command and speech, not creation.

Core Idea
The absolute negation proves 'from Allah' refers to creation, not just command.

The author now considers a potential objection to his earlier proof. Some might argue that when the Jews said 'this is from Allah,' they meant it is His speech or command, not that He created it. The author counters this objection with three incisive arguments, drawing on linguistic precision and logical consistency. Let's examine each refutation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the objection is presented: that 'from Allah' means His speech and command, not creation.
The objection tries to limit the meaning of 'from Allah' to command alone.
2
Examine the first refutation
Study al-Jubba'i's argument: if the objection were true, the verse would be redundant, making coordination of two clauses meaningless.
This refutation uses the principle that coordinating a thing with itself is corrupt.
3
Examine the second and third refutations
Review al-Kalbi's argument about strength of meaning and the third argument about absolute negation. Note how the absolute negation excludes any relation to Allah, including command.
The third argument is the most decisive, showing the negation is unconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ولا يقال : المراد من قولهم : هو من عند الله أنه كلام الله وحكمه فلا جرم قال تعالى : وما هو من عند الله أي ما هو من كلام الله ولا حكمه . قالوا : هذا السؤال باطل من وجوه : الأول : قال الجبائي : إن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى بين قوله : لتحسبوه من الكتاب وما هو من الكتاب وبين قوله : ويقولون هو من عند الله وما هو من عند الله فرق . وإذا لم يبق هذا الفرق لم يحسن العطف لأن عطف الشيء على نفسه فاسد . الثاني : قال الكلبي : كون المخلوق من عند الخالق أكد من كون المأمور به من عند الأمر وحمل الكلام على الوجه الأقوى أولى . الثالث : قوله : وما هو من عند الله نفي مطلق لكونه من عند الله وهذا ينفي كونه من عند الله بوجه من الوجوه فوجب أن لا يكون من عنده لا بالخلق ولا بالحكم .
They said: And it cannot be said: the intended meaning of their saying 'it is from Allah' is that it is Allah's speech and command. Therefore He said: 'and it is not from Allah,' meaning it is neither from Allah's speech nor His command. They said: This objection is invalid for several aspects: First: al-Jubba'i said: 'According to this interpretation, there would remain no difference between His saying: "that you may think it from the Scripture, but it is not from the Scripture" and His saying: "and they say, it is from Allah, but it is not from Allah." And if this difference ceases, coordination would not be good, because coordinating a thing with itself is corrupt.' Second: al-Kalbi said: 'The creation being from the Creator is more emphatic than the commanded being from the commander. To interpret the statement in the strongest way is more appropriate.' Third: His saying 'and it is not from Allah' is an absolute negation of its being from Allah, and this negates its being from Allah in any respect. Therefore it must not be from Him either by creation or by command.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the previous proof. The objection claims that when the Jews said 'it is from Allah,' they meant it is Allah's speech and command, not that it is created by Him. The author refutes this objection with three arguments. The first, from al-Jubba'i, points out that if the objection were correct, there would be no difference between two statements in the verse, making the coordination redundant and illogical. The second, from al-Kalbi, argues that the phrase 'from Allah' in the context of creation is stronger than in the context of command, so interpreting it as the stronger meaning (creation) is preferable. The third argument states that the verse says 'it is not from Allah' absolutely, which negates any relation to Allah, whether by creation or by command. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
خلق
أمر
عطف
نفي مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'from Allah' in the verse only means 'by His command,' excluding creation.
Correction: The third argument shows that the negation is absolute, covering any relation to Allah, including command and creation.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the objection?
A) The verse would be redundant.
B) The Jews were lying.
C) Al-Jubba'i said it is invalid.
D) The command is from Allah.

Key Takeaway
The absolute negation in the verse proves that the phrase 'from Allah' includes creation, not just command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone claim that their opinion or teaching is 'from Allah,' what criteria from this lesson do you use to test that claim? Are you quick to accept it without examining whether it truly aligns with revelation?
?
The absolute negation in this verse shows that nothing false can be attributed to Allah in any way. Have you ever mistakenly attributed something to Allah—perhaps out of enthusiasm or habit—without verifying its truth? How will you guard your speech from now on?
?
The third refutation removes all ambiguity: if something is not from Allah, it is not from His creation or His command. Does this challenge you to reconsider any beliefs or practices you once assumed were divinely sanctioned but now suspect might be otherwise? What step will you take to investigate?

Three Objections to the Interpretation of the Verse
Al-Jubba'i, Al-Kalbi, and a third objection against limiting the verse's negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical objections raised against interpreting the verse 'and it is not from Allah' as merely negating divine speech or judgment.

Core Idea
Three objections show that the verse's negation must be absolute, not limited.

This passage presents three objections—by Al-Jubba'i, Al-Kalbi, and a third general argument—against restricting the verse's negation to speech and judgment. Each objection uses a different reasoning: grammatical redundancy, rhetorical strength, and absolute negation. Together, they aim to show that the verse denies any relation to Allah, including creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three objections
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the passage first states the interpretation being criticized, then lists three objections introduced by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'. Identify each objector.
The first objection is by Al-Jubba'i, the second by Al-Kalbi, the third is anonymous.
2
Examine Al-Jubba'i's grammatical argument
Al-Jubba'i argues that if the verse only negates that the statement is from Allah in the sense of speech, then the two clauses 'you consider it from the Book and it is not from the Book' and 'they say it is from Allah and it is not from Allah' become redundant, making the conjunction invalid. Understand why this is a problem.
This objection relies on the principle of 'عطف الشيء على نفسه' (conjoining a thing to itself) being corrupt.
3
Understand Al-Kalbi's argument and the third absolute negation argument
Al-Kalbi argues that 'created from the Creator' is a stronger meaning than 'commanded from the Commander', so the verse should be interpreted with the stronger meaning (creation). The third argument states that the negation is absolute, so it cannot be limited to just judgment – it also denies creation. Reflect on how these two objections complement each other.
The third objection is a general argument, not attributed to a specific scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكمه قال تعالى : ( وما هو من عند الله ) أي ما هو من كلام الله ولا حكمه . قالوا : هذا السؤال باطل من وجوه : الأول : قال الجبائي : « إن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى بين قوله : لتحسبوه من الكتاب وما هو من الكتاب ) وبين قوله : ( ويقولون هو من عند الله ، وما هو من عند الله ) فرق . وإذا لم يبق هذا الفرق . لم يحسن العطف ، لأن عطف الشيء على نفسه فاسد ) الثاني : قال الكلبي : « كون المخلوق من عند الخالق أكد من كون المأمور به من عند الأمر ، وحمل الكلام على الوجه الأقوى : أولى » . الثالث : قوله : ( وما هو من عند الله ( نفي مطلق لكونه من عند الله وهذا ينفي كونه من عند الله بوجه من الوجوه . فوجب أن لا يكون من عنده لا بالخلق ولا بالحكم .
And its judgment: He said, Most High: 'And it is not from Allah' meaning that it is not the speech of Allah nor His judgment. They said: This question is invalid from several aspects: First: Al-Jubba'i said: 'Indeed, on this estimation, no difference remains between His saying: "you consider it from the Book and it is not from the Book" and His saying: "and they say it is from Allah and it is not from Allah." And if this difference remains no more, then the conjunction is not good, because conjoining a thing to itself is corrupt.' Second: Al-Kalbi said: 'The created thing being from the Creator is more emphatic than the commanded thing being from the commander, and carrying the speech on the stronger meaning is more appropriate.' Third: His saying: 'And it is not from Allah' is an absolute negation of its being from Allah, and this negates its being from Allah in any way. So it must be that it is not from Him, neither by creation nor by judgment.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised against a specific interpretation of the Quranic verse (59:14? Actually it is from 2:75? The verse discussed is 'They say it is from Allah, and it is not from Allah'). The interpretation being critiqued is that the verse simply means the Jews' statement is not from Allah in the sense of being His speech or judgment. The first objection, by Al-Jubba'i, argues that if the verse only negates that it is from Allah in that sense, then there would be no difference between the two clauses 'you consider it from the Book and it is not from the Book' and 'they say it is from Allah and it is not from Allah', making the conjunction redundant and invalid. The second objection, by Al-Kalbi, appeals to rhetorical strength: saying something is created by the Creator is more emphatic than saying it is commanded by the commander, so the verse should be interpreted with the stronger meaning (creation) rather than the weaker one (command). The third objection asserts that the negation in the verse is absolute, negating any relation to Allah whatsoever, so it cannot be limited to just not being His judgment; it also denies that it is His creation. The objectors conclude that the verse cannot be restricted to the interpretation of speech and judgment alone.

Key Terms
عطف الشيء على نفسه
نفي مطلق
الوجه الأقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third objection is also from Al-Kalbi.
Correction: The third objection is presented as a separate general argument, not attributed to Al-Kalbi or any specific scholar.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Al-Jubba'i's objection?
A) The interpretation leads to an invalid conjunction of identical things.
B) The verse must be taken in its most emphatic sense.
C) The negation is absolute and cannot be limited.
D) The Jews did not claim the distortion is Allah's creation.

Key Takeaway
The verse's negation is absolute and cannot be limited to speech or judgment—it denies any divine origin.

The Specific and General Negation
First answer distinguishing specific and general negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first answer distinguishes between a specific negation (not from the Book) and a general negation (not from Allah) to avoid redundancy.

Core Idea
Two different negations can be conjoined without redundancy.

The objection claimed that the verse conjoins two identical negations. The first answer shows that they are not identical: one denies that the distortion is in the Book, the other denies that it is from Allah at all. This subtle distinction is crucial for understanding the Qur'anic refutation of Jewish distortions.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logic come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the two negations
Locate the phrases 'ليس من الكتاب' and 'ليس من عند الله'. Observe how the first is called 'نفي خاص' and the second 'نفي عام'.
3
Understand the analogy with legal sources
The author compares the situation to how legal rulings can come from the Book, Sunnah, consensus, or analogy. All are from Allah, but only some are in the Book. This shows that not being in the Book does not mean it is not from Allah.
This analogy supports the distinction between the two negations.
4
Reflect on why the answer works
Ask yourself: if the first negation is simply about being in the Book, and the second is about being from Allah in any way, are they the same? No. Therefore, conjoining them is not redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قول اليهود إنه من عند الله - أنه كلام الله وحكمه - قول يلزم عليه عطف الشيء على نفسه ، ؟ قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إنه ليس كل ما لم يكن من الكتاب ، لم يكن من عند الله . فإن الحكم الشرعي قد يثبت تارة بالكتاب وتارة بالسنة ، وتارة بسائر الطرق مثل الإجماع والقياس في شرعنا . ثم كل هذه الطرق من عند الله وبحكمه فقوله تعالى : ( لتحسبوه من الكتاب . وما هو من الكتاب ( نفي خاص وهو كونه مذكور في كتاب الله ، ثم عطف عليه النفي العام فقال : ( ويقولون هو من عند الله ، وما هو من عند الله ) الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أنه يجوز أن يكون المراد من الكتاب : التوراة ويكون المراد في قولهم هو من عند الله : إنه موجود في كتب سائر الأنبياء مثل : إشعياء وإرمياء وحبقوق ، وذلك لأن اليهود كانوا في نسبة تلك التحريفات إلى الله منجرين . فإن وجدوا قوما أعمارا جهالا بالتوراة ، نسبوا تلك المحرفات إلى أنها من التوراة . وإن وجدوا عقلاء لا عليهم ذلك الكلام . زعموا : أنه موجود في كتب سائر الأنبياء الذين جاؤا بعد موسى عليه السلام . فثبت بهذين الوجهين : أنه لا يلزم عطف الشيء على نفسه .
And the answer: Why is it not permissible that the meaning of the Jews' statement 'it is from Allah' is that it is the speech of Allah and His judgment, a statement that necessitates conjoining a thing to itself? We said: The answer to it is from two aspects: First: Not everything that is not from the Book is not from Allah. For the legal ruling may sometimes be established by the Book, sometimes by the Sunnah, and sometimes by other methods such as consensus and analogy in our law. Then all these methods are from Allah and by His judgment. So His saying: 'you consider it from the Book, and it is not from the Book' is a specific negation, namely that it is mentioned in the Book of Allah, then He conjoined to it the general negation and said: 'and they say it is from Allah, and it is not from Allah.' Second aspect of the answer: That it is permissible that what is meant by 'the Book' is the Torah, and what is meant by their saying 'it is from Allah' is that it exists in the books of other prophets such as Isaiah, Jeremiah, and Habakkuk. This is because the Jews were daring in attributing those distortions to Allah. For if they found people ignorant of the Torah, they attributed those distortions to the Torah. And if they found intelligent people who would not accept that speech, they claimed that it exists in the books of other prophets who came after Moses, peace be upon him. So it is established by these two aspects that conjoining a thing to itself does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first objection (the redundancy objection). The answer is given in two ways. The first way argues that the two negations are not identical: the first negation ('not from the Book') is specific—it simply denies that the distortion is written in the Quran or Torah. The second negation ('not from Allah') is general—it denies that the distortion is from Allah in any way. These are different levels of negation, so conjoining them is valid. The argument draws an analogy with legal rulings that can come from multiple sources (Book, Sunnah, consensus, analogy), all of which are ultimately from Allah. The first negation points to a specific absence (not in the Book), while the second negates divine origin generally. The second way offers an alternative interpretation: 'the Book' in the first clause refers specifically to the Torah, and 'from Allah' in the second clause refers to claims that the distortion appears in other prophetic books (like those of Isaiah, Jeremiah, Habakkuk). The Jews used different strategies depending on their audience: for ignorant people, they said it was in the Torah; for knowledgeable people, they claimed it was in other prophets' books. This interpretation also avoids redundancy because the two clauses refer to different claims. Both together prove that conjoining a thing to itself does not occur.

Key Terms
نفي خاص
نفي عام
الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two negations in the verse are identical and redundant.
Correction: The first negation is specific (not in the Book), the second is general (not from Allah), so they are not identical.

Checkpoint
How does the first answer avoid the redundancy of conjoining a thing to itself?
A) By arguing that the two negations are different: one specific and one general.
B) By claiming the Jews never said it was from the Torah.
C) By stating that the Book refers only to the Quran.
D) By denying that the verse has two clauses.

Key Takeaway
The first answer resolves the redundancy by showing that 'not from the Book' is a specific negation while 'not from Allah' is a general negation.

The Book and Other Prophets
Second answer interpreting 'the Book' as Torah and 'from Allah' as other prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second answer interprets 'the Book' as the Torah and 'from Allah' as referring to other prophetic books, thereby avoiding redundancy.

Core Idea
The two clauses refer to different sets of scriptures.

The second answer offers a contextual interpretation: the Jews would claim distortions were in the Torah for ignorant audiences, but for knowledgeable audiences they claimed they were from other prophets' books. This explains why the verse uses two different phrases.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer passage

These prophets are not typically associated with the Torah.
2
Understand the alternative interpretation
The answer proposes that 'the Book' in the first negation means the Torah, and 'from Allah' in the second means from the books of other prophets. This makes the two clauses refer to different sources.
3
Consider the historical context
The author explains that the Jews would adapt their claims: to ignorant people they said the distortion was in the Torah; to intelligent people they said it was in other prophets' books. This shows the two phrases address different audiences.
4
See how this avoids redundancy
Since the two clauses refer to different scriptural sources (Torah vs. other prophets), they are not identical. Therefore, conjoining them is valid.

Context Over Emphasis in Interpreting 'From Allah'
The author refutes Al-Kalbi's emphasis argument and gives an additional answer to the absolute negation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author prioritizes contextual clues over rhetorical emphasis to determine the correct meaning of ambiguous phrases in theological argumentation.

Core Idea
Context determines meaning even over rhetorical strength.

In this passage, the author tackles two intertwined objections about the verse's negation. He argues that the Jews' own claim—that their distortion was divine judgment, not creation—provides a contextual clue that overrides the rhetorical emphasis argument. This reasoning also supplies an additional answer to the third objection: the word 'from Allah' is a homonym that cannot carry both meanings simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation fully. Note where the passage ends incomplete and how the author builds his argument.
The last sentence is cut off; focus on the logical flow up to that point.
2
Identify the two arguments
First, the author gives a contextual clue based on the claimants' position. Second, he provides a homonym argument. Distinguish these two distinct responses.
The first argument also serves as an answer to the third objection.
3
Reflect on the homonym principle
Understand why a homonym cannot carry both meanings and how the context forces a single interpretation.
This principle is crucial in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : كون المخلوق من عند الخالق ، أكد من كون المأمور به من عند الأمر ، وحمل الكلام على الوجه الأقوى : أولى، قلنا : لكن ههنا قرينة أخرى تدل على أن الذي قلناه أولى . وذلك لأن الجواب لا بد وأن يكون مطابقاً للسؤال ، والقوم ما ادعوا أن التحريف الذي ذكروه وفعلوه خلق الله تعالى ، وإنما ادعوا أنه حكم الله ونازل في كتابه . فوجب أن يكون قوله : ( وما هو من عند الله ) إلى هذا المعنى لا إلى غيره . وهذا الكلام يصلح لجعله جواباً عن السؤال الثالث وأيضا : فعنه جواب آخر ، وهو أن يكون تأدى هذا الحكم حكما الله تعالى وكون هذا الشيء مخلوقاً الله تعالى : معنيان مختلفان ، وحمل اللفظ المشترك على كل مفهوميه لا يجوز ، ولما ثبت أنه لا بد وأن يكون هذا اللفظ محمولا على نفي الحكم وجب أن لا يكون محمولا على نفي
And his saying: 'The created thing being from the Creator is more emphatic than the commanded thing being from the commander, and carrying the speech on the stronger meaning is more appropriate.' We said: But here there is another contextual clue indicating that what we said is more appropriate. That is because the answer must correspond to the question, and these people did not claim that the distortion they mentioned and performed is the creation of Allah, rather they claimed that it is the judgment of Allah and revealed in His Book. So it must be that His saying: 'and it is not from Allah' refers to this meaning and not another. And this statement is suitable to be an answer to the third question. Also, there is another answer to it, which is that this ruling being a ruling of Allah and this thing being a creation of Allah are two different meanings, and it is not permissible to carry a homonym on both of its senses. And since it is established that this word must be carried on the negation of judgment, it necessarily follows that it should not be carried on the negation of creation.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage addresses the second and third objections. First, it responds to Al-Kalbi's point about rhetorical strength. The author argues that there is another contextual clue (قرينة) that outweighs the emphasis argument: the answer must match the question. The Jews themselves did not claim that their distortion was Allah's creation; they claimed it was Allah's judgment and revealed in His Book. Therefore, the negation in the verse must refer to the claim of divine judgment, not the claim of creation. This reasoning also serves as an answer to the third objection (the absolute negation). Then the author adds a second answer to the third objection: being a ruling of Allah and being a creation of Allah are two distinct meanings. The word 'from Allah' is a homonym that could bear either sense, but it cannot be interpreted to mean both simultaneously. Since the context requires it to mean the negation of judgment (based on the Jews' claim), it cannot also negate creation. This refutes the objection that the absolute negation must extend to creation as well.

Key Terms
قرينة
اللفظ المشترك
معنيان مختلفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the stronger rhetorical emphasis (creation over judgment) always determines interpretation.
Correction: The author shows that contextual clues (قرينة) can override even strong rhetorical considerations. The specific claim of the interlocutor restricts the meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the verse should be interpreted as negating judgment rather than creation?
A) Because the Jews only claimed the distortion was Allah's judgment, not His creation.
B) Because creation is less emphatic than judgment.
C) Because the word 'from Allah' cannot be used for creation.
D) Because the question was about the Book, not about creation.

Key Takeaway
When interpreting ambiguous verses, the context of the claimants' assertion determines which meaning is intended, not just rhetorical strength.

The Futility of Accountability if God is Sole Agent
The author presents a brief argument that divine justice, as depicted in the Quran, necessitates human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic promise of judgment and accountability logically requires that humans are genuine agents of their actions, and why attributing all actions to God would make the Hereafter meaningless.

Core Idea
Accountability without agency is absurd.

This brief but powerful argument from Imam al-Maturidi exposes a fundamental flaw in the view that God is the sole agent of all human actions. If the Hereafter involves reward and punishment based on deeds, then those deeds must truly belong to the servant. Let's examine this concise proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If the servant did not do any of these actions... then his being called to account and rebuked would be futile.'
The Arabic uses 'فلو لم يفعل' to introduce the conditional.
2
Understand the logical force
The argument is simple: accountability presupposes agency. If God is the sole doer, then the servant cannot be justly held responsible. This is a powerful critique of extreme predestinarian views.
3
Connect to Quranic justice
The passage references verses about resurrection conditions (punishment, reward). Reflect on how the Quran itself describes judgment as just, which implies human choice.
4
Address the misconception
Some think attributing all actions to God still allows accountability, but this passage shows that would make God's judgment arbitrary. The Quranic promise of judgment itself proves human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع التاسع عشر لهم الآيات الدالة على أحوال أهل القيامة من العقاب والحسنات والثواب والعقاب فلو لم يفصل (۱) العبد شيئاً من هذه الأفعال ، بل كان فاعلهـا هـو الله تعالى ، كانت محاسبته ومعاتبته عبثاً.
The nineteenth type for them: the verses indicating the conditions of the people of the Resurrection, from punishment and good deeds and reward and punishment. So if the servant did not do any of these actions, but rather their doer was God (may He be exalted), then his being called to account and rebuked would be futile.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument from divine justice. It begins by introducing a section (the nineteenth type) concerning verses about the conditions of the people of the Hereafter, specifically punishment, good deeds, reward, and punishment. The argument then states a conditional: if the servant (al-ʿabd) did not perform any of these actions (i.e., the good or evil deeds), but instead God was the sole doer of them, then the very act of holding the servant to account (muḥāsaba) and rebuking him (muʿātaba) would be futile (ʿabathan). The point is that accountability presupposes that the individual is the true agent of his actions. If God were the only agent, then moral responsibility disappears, and the entire system of reward and punishment would be pointless.

Key Terms
محاسبة (muḥāsaba)
معاتبة (muʿātaba)
عبث (ʿabath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributing all actions to God still allows for human accountability because God can judge as He wills.
Correction: This passage argues that if God is the sole agent, then the very concepts of accountability and rebuke become meaningless (ʿabath), because the servant is not the doer. The Quranic promise of judgment itself implies that humans are genuine agents.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if God is the sole doer of human actions, what would be the result?
A) Servants would be rewarded for God's actions
B) Accountability and rebuke would be futile
C) Only punishment would be just
D) Good deeds would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The Quranic promise of accountability logically requires that humans are the true agents of their deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your deeds are truly your own—not merely created in you by Allah—how does that change your sense of responsibility for your actions today? Is there a specific sin you have been excusing by thinking 'Allah made me do it'? What will you confess and change right now?
?
If accountability without agency is futile, then every moment you are accountable for your choices. Are you living with the awareness that your smallest action will be weighed? What one habit will you begin today to take full ownership of your obedience to Allah?
?
The Quranic promise of judgment assumes you are the doer. Does your daily life reflect this belief, or do you sometimes live as if your actions are out of your control? What step will you take this week to actively choose a deed that you know will be accounted for?

Fifth Proof: The Hadith 'Work, for Each Is Made Easy'
Author proves human capacity from the hadith 'each is made easy for what he is created for.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Work, for each is made easy' serves as a proof for human capacity and free will, refuting compulsion.

Core Idea
The command to work proves we have the ability to obey.

In this passage, the author presents a fifth proof based on a well-known hadith. He connects it to the Qur'anic purpose of creation and argues that the Prophet's command to 'work' is only meaningful if humans have genuine capacity to act. This counters the deterministic view that God compels actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the structure: verse, command, logical condition, conclusion.
2
Identify the Qur'anic verse
Find the verse cited and understand its role: it establishes the purpose of creation as worship, which sets up the command to work.
The verse is 51:56.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow how the author moves from the command to the condition that servants must have ability, otherwise the command would be futile.
Note the contrast: if God compelled disbelief, the command would be meaningless.
4
Consider the conclusion
Understand why the author calls the hadith a 'clear text' on this issue. Reflect on how it supports human capacity against compulsion.
This refutes the idea that the hadith implies predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال به : أنه تعالى بين أن الخلق مخلوقون للعبادة كما قال تعالى : ( وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون ) فلا جرم قال : اعملوا بالطاعة . فإن جميع الخلق قد يسر الله لهم الاشتغال بالعبادة التي لأجلها خلقوا وإنما يكون هذا التيسير حاصلا إذا قلنا : العبيد قادرون على الفعل ، وأنه تعالى لا يخلق فيهم الكفر جبراً ، ولا يمنعهم عن الإيمان ، ولا يضلهم عن سواء السبيل . فثبت : أن قوله عليه السلام : ( اعملوا فكل ميسر لما خلق له ) نص صريح في هذه المسألة
The proof from it: That He, exalted, clarified that creation is created for worship, as He said: 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' So inevitably He said: 'Work in obedience.' For indeed He has made easy for all creation engagement in worship for which they were created. And this facilitation is only attained if we say: the servants are able (qādirūn) over the action, and that He, exalted, does not create in them disbelief by compulsion (jabr), nor prevent them from faith, nor lead them astray from the straight path. So it is established that his saying, peace be upon him: 'Work, for each is made easy for what he is created for' is an explicit text (naṣṣ ṣarīḥ) on this issue.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from a ḥadīth of the Prophet. It begins by citing the Qur'anic verse (51:56) that the purpose of creation is worship. Based on that, the Prophet commanded 'Work in obedience.' The argument is that this command only makes sense if servants have genuine ability (qudra) to perform the action. If God were to create disbelief in them by compulsion, prevent them from faith, or lead them astray, the command would be futile. The ḥadīth 'Work, for each is made easy for what he is created for' is therefore a clear textual proof that humans have free will and capacity, not that God predestines them to evil.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
التيسير
مخلوقون للعبادة
العبيد قادرون
جبراً
نص صريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'each is made easy' means God predestines each person to their fate, including disbelief and sin.
Correction: The author argues that the hadith indicates facilitation, not compulsion. The command to work implies real human capability, and God does not create disbelief by force.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fifth proof?
A) God creates all actions including disbelief.
B) The command to work implies humans have real ability to act.
C) The hadith shows God makes everyone do what they are created for.
D) Worship is only possible by God's direct compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Work, for each is made easy' is a clear textual proof that humans possess genuine capacity to act, opposing the doctrine of compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you have the genuine ability to obey God, or do you sometimes hide behind the excuse that your circumstances or 'fate' prevent you? How will you live differently knowing that God has made worship easy for you?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that it was destined or that you had no choice? In light of this hadith, what responsibility do you take for your actions?
?
If the command to work (obey) implies that you have the power to do so, what is one area of your life where you have been claiming inability? What step will you take today to act on that ability, trusting that Allah has facilitated it?

Evil Is Not from God: The Prophetic Supplication Proof
Author proves evil not from God via supplication and reconciles apparent contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic supplication provides explicit evidence that evil is not from God, and how an apparent contradiction with another part of the supplication is resolved through interpretation.

Core Idea
The Prophet's own words settle it: evil is not from God.

The author now turns to a direct textual proof from the Prophet's own supplication. This du'a, recited daily by Muslims, contains the explicit phrase 'evil is not to You.' However, it also says 'all good is in Your hands,' which might seem to suggest that even faith (the greatest good) is created by God. How do we reconcile these? The author shows that proper interpretation removes any conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the prophetic supplication
Read the Arabic supplication and its translation. Identify the key phrase 'evil is not to You' and the context of the du'a.
This supplication is known as the opening supplication (du'a al-istiftah).
2
Understand the explicit proof
Notice that the author takes 'evil is not to You' as a clear (ṣarīḥ) text that evil is not from God. This is the main argument.
The phrase is direct and unambiguous.
3
Resolve the apparent contradiction
An objection arises from the phrase 'good is in Your hands' which might imply God creates both good and evil. Follow how the author harmonizes the two statements through interpretation.
The resolution involves interpreting 'good in Your hands' as God's enabling (iqdār) and providing proofs, not direct creation of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : الدعاء المشهور المأثور عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو دعاء الاستفتاح وهو قوله : ( اللهم أنت الملك ، لا إله إلا أنت أنت ربي وأنا عبدك ظلمت نفسي واعترفت بذنبي فاغفر لي فإنه لا يغفر الذنوب إلا أنت واهدني لأحسن الأخلاق ، فإنه لا يهدي لأحسنها إلا أنت ، واصرف عني سيئها ، فإنه لا يصرف سيئها إلا أنت . لبيك وسعديك ، والخير كله في يديك . والشر ليس إليك ، إنا بك وإليك ، تباركت وتعاليت ، استغفرك وأتوب إليك ) وجه الاستدلال به: إن قوله : ( والشر ليس إليك ) ، صريح في المسألة . ولا يقال : إن قوله : ( الخير في يديك ) : يقتضي كون الإيمان من الله تعالى ، لأنه أعظم الخيرات. لأنا نقول : لما وقع التعارض وجب التوفيق ، فيحمل قول ( الخير في يديك ) على أن الإيمان حصل بإقدار الله تعالى ، ونصب الدلائل عليه . ويحمل قوله : ( الشر ليس إليك ) على أنه ليس بخلقه وتكوينه . وبهذا الطريق يزول التعارض
The sixth proof: The well-known supplication transmitted from the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, which is the opening supplication, and it is his saying: 'O God, You are the King, there is no god but You. You are my Lord, and I am Your servant. I have wronged myself and confessed my sin. So forgive me, for none forgives sins except You. Guide me to the best of characters, for none guides to the best of them except You. And turn away from me their evil, for none turns away their evil except You. Here I am at Your service, and all good is in Your hands. Evil is not attributed to You. I am by You and to You. Blessed and exalted are You. I seek Your forgiveness and repent to You.' The aspect of proof from it: That his saying 'Evil is not to You' is explicit (ṣarīḥ) on the issue. And let it not be said: That his saying 'good is in Your hands' necessitates that faith is from God, because it is the greatest of good things. For we say: When conflict occurred, reconciliation is necessary. So the saying 'good is in Your hands' is interpreted that faith occurs by God's enabling (iqdār) and setting up proofs for it. And his saying 'evil is not to You' is interpreted that it is not by His creation (khalq) and origination (takwīn). And by this way the conflict is removed.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, from a well-known prophetic supplication (the opening supplication). The author quotes the full du'ā, which includes the phrase 'evil is not to You.' This is taken as an explicit text that evil is not from God. An objection is raised: another part of the same du'ā says 'all good is in Your hands,' which might imply that faith (the greatest good) is from God, potentially contradicting the first phrase. The author resolves this by interpreting 'good is in Your hands' as referring to God's enabling (iqdār) and providing proofs, not direct creation. And 'evil is not to You' means it is not created or originated by God. Thus the two statements are reconciled without contradiction.

Key Terms
دعاء الاستفتاح
الشر ليس إليك
الخير في يديك
التعارض
التوفيق
خلقه وتكوينه
إقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two parts of the du'a contradict each other, implying God creates both good and evil.
Correction: They are reconciled by interpreting 'good in Your hands' as God's enabling, not direct creation, and 'evil not to You' means not by His creation.

Checkpoint
How does the author reconcile the apparent contradiction between 'good is in Your hands' and 'evil is not to You'?
A) He denies the authenticity of one part.
B) He says 'good is in Your hands' means God forces good, and 'evil not to You' means He leaves evil.
C) He interprets 'good is in Your hands' as God's enabling and providing proofs, not direct creation.
D) He says both refer to different types of actions.

Key Takeaway
The prophetic supplication 'evil is not to You' is explicit that God does not create evil, and any apparent contradiction with 'good is in Your hands' is resolved by interpreting that God enables good but does not create evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or evil, do you find yourself unconsciously attributing it to God, even if only in your heart? How does the Prophet's explicit statement 'evil is not to You' challenge that tendency? What concrete step can you take this week to remind yourself of this truth when difficulty strikes?
?
The du'a begins with your own confession: 'I have wronged myself and confessed my sin.' If evil is not from God, then the evil you commit must come from yourself. Are you willing to fully own your sins without shifting blame to God or circumstance? How would your repentance change if you truly believed this?
?
The Prophet prayed 'Guide me to the best of characters' and then said 'evil is not to You.' Do you live as though every good character trait is a gift from God, or do you take pride in your own goodness? What would it look like for you to see your best qualities as solely from God's enabling?

The Hadith-Based Proof that Evil is Not from God
Author proves that evil is not from God using the hadith of seeking refuge from evil of creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of seeking refuge from evil creatures is used to argue that evil is not created by God.

Core Idea
We seek refuge from creatures' evil, not from God's creation of evil.

In this lesson, we examine the seventh proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author cites a hadith where the Prophet taught Fatima a supplication that includes seeking refuge from the evil of every creature. The key phrase is used to argue that evil cannot be attributed to God, but rather to the creatures themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic hadith and its literal translation. Notice the context: Fatima asked for a servant and the Prophet taught her this supplication.
The hadith includes many praises of God before the key phrase.
2
Identify the key phrase
Locate the phrase 'أعوذ بك من شر كل دابة أنت أخذ بناصيتها' (I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature, You are the one who takes hold of its forelock). This is the crux of the proof.
3
Understand the logical inference
The author argues that seeking refuge from the evil of creatures implies that evil comes from the creatures, not from God. If God were the source of evil, seeking refuge from it would be inappropriate.
This inference is explicitly stated in 'وجه الاستدلال'.
4
Consider the author's conclusion
The author concludes that anyone who claims evil is from God is wrong. Reflect on why this argument is compelling from a theological perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : ما روى أبو صالح عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه ، قال : جاءت فاطمة إلى رسول الله وطلبت منه خادماً . فقال لها : « قولي اللهم رب السموات السبع ورب العرش العظيم ، ربنا ورب كل شيء ، أنت الظاهر فليس فوقك شيء ، وأنت الباطن فليس دونك شيء ، منزل التوراة والإنجيل والفرقان ، فالق الحب والنوى ، أعوذ بك من شر كل دابة أنت أخذ بناصيتها » وجه الاستدلال : قوله : ( أعوذ بك من شر كل دابة » يدل على أن الشر ليس من الله ، ومن قال بغير ذلك فإنه باطل
The seventh proof: What Abu Sāliḥ narrated from Abū Hurayra, may God be pleased with him, said: Fatima came to the Messenger of God and asked him for a servant. He said to her: 'Say: O God, Lord of the seven heavens and Lord of the great throne, our Lord and Lord of everything, You are the Apparent so nothing is above You, and You are the Hidden so nothing is below You, Revealer of the Torah, Gospel, and Criterion, Splitter of the grain and the date-stone. I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature, You are the one who takes hold of its forelock.' The aspect of proof: His saying 'I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature' indicates that evil is not from God. And whoever says otherwise, it is false.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, based on a hadith where the Prophet taught Fatima a supplication. The key phrase is 'I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature, You are the one who takes hold of its forelock.' The argument is straightforward: if God were the creator of evil, then seeking refuge from the evil of creatures would be seeking refuge from what God has created directly, which is inappropriate. Therefore, the hadith implies that evil is not created by God; rather, it comes from the creatures themselves. The author declares any contrary view as invalid.

Key Terms
أعوذ بك
شر كل دابة
أخذ بناصيتها
وجه الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeking refuge from evil acknowledges evil as a separate power or that God creates evil directly.
Correction: The hadith shows that evil is attributed to creatures, not to God; we seek refuge from them, not from God's action.

Checkpoint
What is the key phrase in the seventh proof that indicates evil is not from God?
A) 'You are the Apparent'
B) 'Lord of everything'
C) 'I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature'
D) 'Splitter of the grain'

Key Takeaway
The hadith phrase 'I seek refuge in You from the evil of every creature' is used to prove that evil is not created by God, but rather comes from the creatures themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience hardship or see evil in the world, do you truly believe that it does not come from God? How does this hadith challenge the way you respond to tragedy—do you blame God or do you seek refuge from the evil of the creatures involved?
?
If evil is not from God, then what is the source of your own sins? Are you willing to take full responsibility for the evil you commit, knowing it is not ordained by God but a result of your own choices?
?
The Prophet taught this supplication to his beloved daughter. Have you ever used these words with the conviction that the evil you fear is from the creatures, not from God? How would your heart change if you truly believed that God is only good and never the source of harm?

The Eighth Proof: Hadith 'Just in Your Decree' and the Objections
Author proves God's justice using a hadith and responds to two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'عدل في قضائك' from the hadith serves as a proof of God's justice, and how the author responds to an objection that God's actions are just by virtue of His ownership.

Core Idea
'Just in Your decree' explicitly denies injustice in God's actions.

This lesson examines the eighth proof for God's justice, based on a well-known hadith that includes the phrase 'عدل في قضائك' (just in Your decree). After presenting the hadith and its argumentative value, the author addresses an objection that would undermine the proof, and then raises a second objection based on a Qur'anic verse, though the response to this second objection is incomplete in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the full Arabic hadith and its translation. Notice the specific phrase 'عدل في قضائك' which is the core of the proof.
The hadith is well-known; focus on the phrase used for argumentation.
2
Identify the proof from the phrase
The author states that 'عدل في قضائك' indicates that there is no injustice (لا جور) in God's decree. This is the direct textual evidence.
3
Understand the first objection
An objector says that everything God does is just because He acts in His own kingdom; thus the phrase 'just' adds nothing new. Recognize the objection.
This objection tries to undermine the need for explicit proof.
4
Follow the author's response
The author argues that if God's actions are just only because He owns everything, then He would be incapable of injustice (ظلم). But being unable to do injustice is not a flaw, so the objection fails to diminish the praise.
The response shows that the hadith's explicit statement is meaningful.
5
Examine the second objection
The author raises a second objection using the verse 'ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد' which praises God for not doing what is impossible. This objection is incomplete; the author's answer is missing.
This shows a parallel: praising for not doing the impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : روى ابن مسعود أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : « إذا قال العبد عندما يصيبه هم أو حزن : اللهم إني عبدك وابن عبدك وابن أمتك . ناصيتي بيدك ، ماض في حكمك ، عدل في قضائك . أسألك بكل اسم هو لك سميت به نفسك ، وأنزلته في كتابك ، وعلمته أحداً من خلقك ، واستأثرت به في علم الغيب عندك ، أن تجعل القرآن ربيع قلبي ، ونور بصري ، وجلاء حزني ، وذهاب همي » : أذهب الله همه ، وأبدل مكان حزنه فرحاً . ووجه الاستدلال به : آن قوله : ( عدل في قضائك ) يدل على أن لا جور في قضائك . ولا يقال : إن كل ما يفعله الله تعالى بعبده فهو عدل ، لأنه يتصرف في ملك نفسه . لأنا نقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يمنع أن يكون له ظلم على العبد ، يتملح بذلك . لأن من لم يقدر على الظلم فلم يظلم ، لم يكن ذلك قدحاً في حقه . ولقائل أن يقول : يشكل هذا بقوله تعالى : ( ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد ) فإن هذا في معرض المدح، مع أن إيجاد الولد له ممتنع في نفسه
The eighth proof: Ibn Mas'ūd narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'When a servant, when anxiety or sadness befalls him, says: O God, I am Your servant, son of Your servant, son of Your maidservant. My forelock is in Your hand, Your command is executed over me, just is Your decree. I ask You by every name that is Yours, which You have named Yourself, revealed in Your book, taught any of Your creation, or kept in the knowledge of the unseen with You, to make the Qur'an the spring of my heart, the light of my sight, the removal of my sadness, and the departure of my anxiety.' God removes his anxiety and replaces his sadness with joy. The aspect of proof from it: That his saying 'just is Your decree' indicates that there is no injustice in Your decree. And let it not be said: That everything God does to His servant is just, because He acts in His own kingdom. For we say: On this assumption, it prevents that He has injustice towards the servant, thereby beautifying? Because one who is unable to do injustice and did not do injustice, that is not a criticism of him. And one may say: This is problematic by His saying: 'It was not for God to take a son' – for this is in the context of praise, even though creating a son is impossible in itself.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof, from a hadith that includes the phrase 'just is Your decree' ('adl fī qaḍā'ik). The author argues that this explicitly denies any injustice in God's decree. An objection is raised: everything God does is just simply because He is the owner of His kingdom. The author responds that if that were the case, it would mean God is unable to be unjust, and this inability is not a flaw. Then a second objection is brought: the Qur'anic verse 'It was not for God to take a son' (19:35) is a form of praise, even though having a son is impossible for God. The implication is that praising God for not doing what is impossible is similar to the hadith. However, the response to this objection is cut off in the text, leaving the argument incomplete.

Key Terms
عدل في قضائك
لا جور
يتصرف في ملك نفسه
ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'just in Your decree' is redundant if God is just by nature.
Correction: The author uses the phrase to explicitly deny any possibility of injustice, making it a powerful proof against those who might claim otherwise.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the eighth proof?
A) The hadith proves that God always does the best for His servants.
B) The phrase 'just is Your decree' confirms that God never commits injustice.
C) The hadith shows that God's decree is arbitrary.
D) The objection about the verse proves God can have a son.

Key Takeaway
The hadith phrase 'عدل في قضائك' provides explicit textual evidence that God's decree is free from injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you truly believe that God's decree is 'just'? Or do you secretly question His fairness? What would it take for you to say 'عدل في قضائك' with full certainty?
?
The hadith teaches that even in distress, we affirm God's justice. Can you recall a recent trial where you struggled to see justice in God's decree? How can you train your tongue and heart to say 'عدل في قضائك' in that moment?
?
If God's justice were merely a result of His ownership, would that diminish His praise? How does knowing that God explicitly declares His justice in the Quran and hadith affect your trust in Him when life seems unfair?

Ninth Proof: Parable Opening (Fragment)
Incomplete opening of the ninth proof using a parable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ninth proof begins with a hadith quoting a parable of Jesus, but the presented text is only the opening, and the full argument is not provided in this fragment.

Core Idea
A proof's beginning is not the whole proof—context matters.

This lesson presents the first part of the ninth proof from the text, which begins with a prophetic hadith quoting a parable of Jesus about vineyard workers. However, as you will see, the passage cuts off abruptly. Our goal is to recognize this fragment and understand its role as an opening statement rather than a complete argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that it is a narration quoting the Prophet quoting Jesus.
This is a chain of narration: Abu 'Umar heard the Prophet, who quotes Jesus.
2
Identify the structure
Notice that the passage sets up a parable: a master hires workers for a vineyard. But it stops mid-sentence.
The text ends abruptly after 'and sent them to...'.
3
Consider why it is incomplete
Recognize that this is only the beginning; the proof's reasoning is missing. Think about why an author might present a proof in this way.
The proof is cut off, possibly due to manuscript gaps or the author's intended continuation.
4
Reflect on the importance of context
Ask yourself: Can we understand the full argument from this fragment? Why is it important to have the complete proof?
This highlights the need for complete sources in theological study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : روى أبو عمر قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : يقول المسيح عيسى بن مريم عليه السلام : ( فإن ملكوت السموات يشبه رجلاً ، رب بيت ، خرج مع الصبح ، ليستأجر فعلة لكرمه . فاتفق مع الفعلة على دينار في اليوم، وأرسلهم إلى
The ninth proof: Abu 'Umar narrated, said: I heard the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, saying: The Messiah, Jesus son of Mary, peace be upon him, says: 'For the kingdom of heaven is like a man, a master of a house, who went out early in the morning to hire workers for his vineyard. He agreed with the workers on a dinar for the day, and sent them to...'
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage introduces the ninth proof, which begins with a narration from Abu 'Umar quoting the Prophet, who quotes Jesus (peace be upon him) giving a parable about a vineyard owner hiring workers. The text cuts off abruptly in the middle of the parable. Since the argument and istidlal are not provided, this is only a fragment. The parable is known from the Gospel (Matthew 20) and is used in Islamic tradition, but the full argument is absent here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This fragment might be mistaken for a complete argument or a full hadith.
Correction: The text is only the beginning of a proof; the parable and reasoning are incomplete. The full argument would likely draw out a lesson about God's justice or mercy.

Checkpoint
What is the content of the ninth proof as given?
A) A full proof about free will.
B) A parable about a vineyard, but incomplete.
C) A hadith about the Day of Judgment.
D) An argument from the Torah.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof begins with a parable from Jesus about vineyard workers, but the passage is only a fragment, not a complete argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows a fragment of a proof. When have you, in your own study of Islam, assumed you understood a complete argument from only a partial text? How can you guard against that tendency starting today?
?
The chain of narration here includes the Prophet quoting Jesus. Reflect on the weight of that transmission: the words have passed through multiple trustworthy narrators. Do your daily actions honor the care taken to preserve these teachings? What is one concrete step you will take this week to strengthen your engagement with prophetic traditions?
